TRITHEMIUS AND ALCHEMY.
By J. R. PARTINGTON,M.B.E., D.Sc.
JOHANNESTRITHEMIUSwas born in 1462 1. at Trittenheim near Trier on the
Moselle, and takes his name from that of his birthplace. After assiduous but
unsystematic study at Trier and Heidelberg he left for home in 1482, travelling
on foot. He got as far as Sponheim, a mile from Bingen, where deep snow
drove him to shelter in the Benedictine cloister of St. Martin, where he decided
to become a novitiate. Later in the year he made profession, and in 14832,
at the age of barely twenty-two, he was elected Abbot of Sponheim, an office
which he filled for twenty-three years. During this period he re-established

Implementation of Super-Encryption with Trithemius
Algorithm and Double Transposition Cipher in Securing PDF
Files on Android Platform
M A Budiman, D Rachmawati and Jessica
Departemen Ilmu Komputer, Fakultas Ilmu Komputer dan Teknologi Informasi,

Abstract. This study aims to combine the trithemus algorithm and double transposition cipher
in file security that will be implemented to be an Android-based application. The parameters being
examined are the real running time , and the complexity value. The type of file to be used is a file in
PDF format. The overall result shows that the complexity of the two algorithms with duper encryption
method is reported as Œò (ùëõ2).However, the processing time required in the encryption process uses
the Trithemius algorithm much faster than using the Double Transposition Cipher. With the length of
plaintext and password linearly proportional to the processing time.
1. Introduction
The word ‚Äòcryptography‚Äô comes from Greek. According to the language, the word cryptography is
divided into two, namely ‚Äòcrypto‚Äô and ‚Äògraphia‚Äô. ‚ÄòCrypto‚Äô means secret and ‚Äògraphia‚Äô means writing.
According to its terminology, cryptography is the science and art of keeping messages safe when
messages are sent from one place to another [1]. Thus, cryptography can help us in preventing the arrival
of important information, into unauthorized hands. The purpose of cryptography is 4. ie confidentiality,
integrity, authentication,andnon-repudation. Confidentiality focuses on data confidentiality, integrity
focuses on received data is true and genuine data, authentication is done to prevent false data, and nonÔøærepudation aims to prevent the shielding of the data from the sender. Cryptography algorithm divided 2
that is, symmetric algorithm and asymmetric algorithm. However, in this study the algorithm used is a
classical algorithm, namely Trithemius Algorithm and double transposition cipher.
Trithemius Cipher is one of the polyalphabetic codes designed to be easier to use. Instead of using a
random combination of letters of the alphabet, Trithemius uses a special table of Trithemius
Trithemius Cipher is one of the polyalphabetic codes designed to be easier to use. Instead of using a
random combination of letters of the alphabet, Trithemius uses a special table Trithemius, which has 24
rows and columns 24 of 24 letters in the Latin alphabet except the letter j and v [2].
However, in order to make the system more useful, in this study we used a trithemius table consisting
of 65 rows and columns. Where, all characters have entered into the table. Both lowercase and
uppercase. Plus numbers 0 through 9, and some punctuation.
Double Transposition Cipher consists of two rounds of transition columns, using 2 different keys.
After the key in the first round is determined, then each letter in the key is numbered in alphabetical
order, and the plaintext is read per column based on the sequence of numbers. Where, the direction of

THE AUTHOR OF A
RENAISSANCE COMMENTARY
ON PLINY: RIVIUS,
TRITHEMIUS OR AQUAEUS?
T he authorship of the unsigned comÔøæmentary on two chapters of Pliny's
Natural History (xxx, ch. 1-2, or sect. 1-18
in the modern numeration) dealing with
magic, published at Wiurzburg in 1548 by
Johann Myller,1 poses a problem that has
misled nearly all bibliographers. Although
neither the title-page nor the text of the
commentary identifies the author, the titleÔøæpage does carry a cryptic set of initials which
can readily be decoded: 'D.G.H.R. M. & M.'
The student of mid-sixteenth-century German
scientific literature can decipher this line as
an abbreviation for 'Dominus Gualtherus
Hermenius Rivius, Medicus et MatheÔøæmaticus'. Rivius (in German, Walther
Hermann Ryff) was a prolific writer on
medical, biological, occultist and other subÔøæjects: his modern bibliographer lists a total
of 194 titles and editions, including postÔøæhumous ones.2 Furthermore, the printer
Myller is known to have published other
books by him at precisely this period.3

William C. McDonald
Trithemius and the Legend of the Wild Hunt
https://doi.org/10.1515/fabula-2018-0100
Zusammenfassung: Das rege Interesse des vielseitigen und renommierten TheoÔøælogen, Kryptologen und Chronisten Johannes Trithemius (1462‚Äì1516) an allen
Aspekten der Magie ist hinreichend dokumentiert. Bisher ist aber seine EinstelÔøælung zu einem Bereich des Zauberwesens weitgehend unbeachtet, n√§mlich zum
weit verbreiteten, im Volksglauben fest verankerten Legendenkomplex Wilde
Jagd/Wilder J√§ger. Uns ist gelungen, in seinem chronikalischen Werk vier Stellen
ausfindig zu machen, die differenzierten Schattierungen des Ph√§nomens ans
Licht bringen. Sie behandeln ein Totenheer, einen verfluchten, n√§chtlichen J√§ger
und zwei durch die L√ºfte jagende Zauberer. Einer dieser Zauberer ist ein j√ºdischer
Magier, der durch Sinnest√§uschung das Trugbild einer Jagd hervorbringt. Es wird
klar, dass der Abt Trithemius der Geisterjagd gegen√ºber wenig Skepsis aufwies.
Im Gegenteil: durch die genaue Datierung jedes einzelnen Vorkommens (878 bis
zum Jahr 1354) war er bestrebt, seinen Ausf√ºhrungen Glauben zu schenken. Jeder
Beleg l√§sst sich als warnendes Exempel interpretieren, aber der religi√∂se Firnis
bleibt d√ºnn.
Abstract: It is well-established that Johannes Trithemius (1462‚Äì1516), the
renowned polymath (theologian, cryptographer, and chronicler), entertained an
avid interest in all things concerning magic. Scholars have neglected, however, to
pay heed to his views about The Wild Hunt/Wild Huntsman, a widely propagated
tapestry of legends rooted in popular mythology. We examine four passages from
his chronicles to shed light on the many facets of this lore. The subjects of these
testimonies include: a phantom army; a cursed nocturnal hunter; and two aerial
hunting sorcerers, one of whom was a Jewish magician who conjured the illusion
of venery by deceiving the senses. The writings of Trithemius offer little indicaÔøætion of skepticism about the phantasmal chase. On the contrary, the abbot proÔøævides a precise chronology (878‚Äì1354) of these extraordinary tales of the hunt in
order to lend credibility to them. Each entry can be read as a cautionary example,
but the religious veneer seems shallow.
William C. McDonald, Professor, German and Jewish Studies, University of Virginia, CharlottesÔøæville, Virginia, USA 22904, E-Mail: wcm@virginia.edu

196 ‚ÄÉWilliam C. McDonald
Introduction
Lindow so defines the legend of the Wild Hunt:
a legend complex concerning a ghostly hunter, often accompanied by dogs or spirits and
often hunting a woman, who is frequently one of the supernatural beings [‚Ä¶] In other forms
of the legend more common in Germany, the hunter may be a lost soul, or he may lead lost
souls on a wild ride through the sky [‚Ä¶] (Lindow 2000, 1036)
The Wild Hunt is a pan-European myth prominent in folklore, charted since the
eleventh century, and named Die wilde Jagd already in the eighteenth century.1
Despite its ubiquity, the Wild Hunt is neither uniform nor immutable. There are
many versions of the Hunt, which range from a single diabolical huntsman to a
raging/furious host¬†‚Äì troops of the dead or phantom armies. Sounds are promiÔøænent: yelping dogs, wind, and horses‚Äô hoof-beats, each of which portends grievÔøæous affliction: Oft bellen die Hunde der Luft in finsterer Nacht (Grimm 1854, 876).
A widespread belief identifies the furious host as consisting of men, women, and
children¬†‚Äì those having perished before their time, perhaps having experienced a
particularly violent death. Ginzburg calls this group ‚Äúthe menacing apparition of
the unplacated dead‚Äù (Ginzburg 1991, 101).
Generalizations about the Wild Hunt and associated phenomena are difficult.
Menzel, focusing on the role of Odin, a sky god, in the formation of the Wild Hunt,
stresses the many aspects of the concept:
In gewissen N√§chten‚Ä¶sieht und h√∂rt man das wilde Heer durch die L√ºfte ziehen. Dar√ºber
sind alle Sagen einverstanden, da√ü es aus Gespenstern oder D√§monen bestehe, inzwischen
ist keineswegs jedes wilde Heer dem andern gleich. Bald ist es ein eigentliches Heer von
wohlger√ºsteten Kriegern mit Kriegsl√§rm, Trommeln und Trompeten. Bald eine Jagd mit

lachender und toller Weiber, bald ein klagender Zug, wobei viel wimmernde KinderstimÔøæmen geh√∂rt werden, bald ein Zug holder Wesen mit lieblicher Musik (Menzel 1855, 199).
Given the profusion of motifs and exemplars, Lindow expresses the state of scholÔøæarly frustration: ‚ÄúWe are left with no clear interpretation of many of the motifs in
the legend complex of the Wild Hunt‚Äù (Lindow 2000, 1036). For example, there
is no agreement over the name of the Hunt, the purpose of the chase, or the color
of the horse of the phantasmal leader, the Wild Huntsman. This figure, der Wilde
J√§ger, or Nachtj√§ger, is ambiguous, sometimes an historical personality (even

Trithemius and the Legend of the Wild Hunt 197
King Arthur) or an anonymous apparition (Petzoldt 1990, 188‚Äì190). The HuntsÔøæman may fly or remain on the earth, he may manifest himself in order to frighten
and admonish individuals, or he may pursue them, as in the Frauenjagdsage
(R√∂hrich 1965). A constant is the presence, or threat, of the demonic. The Wild
Hunt is closely associated with the Devil.
Much scholarship has been devoted to the Wild Hunt and its Nachtvolk.
Hutton summarizes the theories and then proposes a definition, influenced by
Jacob Grimm, combining:
three [‚Ä¶] mythical components, all of which were said to be apparitions active by night: a
procession of female spirits, often joined by privileged human beings and often led by a
supernatural woman whom medieval clerics called Diana or Herodias and who was known
by a range of local names; a lone spectral huntsman, regarded as demonic, accursed, or
otherworldly; and a procession of the human dead, normally thought to be wandering to
expiate their sins, often noisy and tumultuous, and usually consisting of those who had
died prematurely and violently (Hutton 2014, 175).
Two of these components of the Hunt appear in the Chronicon Sponheimense,
compiled 1495‚Äì1509 (Freher 1601) and the Annales Hirsaugienses, compiled
1509‚Äì1514 (Schlegel 1690) of Johannes Trithemius (1462‚Äì1516), Benedictine abbot
of Sponheim and St. Jacob in W√ºrzburg. Trithemius, a polymath and influential
author with a vast library on demonology and occult studies, was a chronicler,
lexicographer, Hebrew scholar, cryptologist, and enthusiastic investigator of
magic.2 So deep were his forays into natural and artificial magic that Trithemius
was branded a promoter of superstition and illicit magic, a necromancer, a magus
whose views on magic were incompatible with Christianity, and a spirit conÔøæjurer in league with the Devil (Baron 2013, esp. 44‚Äì46). Among the fields that he
investigated were the Hermetic tradition, number mysticism, alchemy, astrology,
Cabala, angelic mediation, geomancy, steganography, exorcism, and witchcraft.
His chronicles hold allusions to demons, ghosts, and magic in all its forms.3
To the long list of marvelous and supernatural topics explored by Trithemius
we add the Wild Hunt. It is surprising that only a single reference to the Hunt has
been located in his chronicles. Lecouteux labels that motif le chasseur maudit
(hereinafter the Cursed Hunter), the tale of the undead Huntsman expiating a sin
committed during his lifetime (Lecouteux 1999, 73). We have discovered three

additional allusions, the first being the Troop of the Dead, which coincides with
Lecouteux‚Äô category les troupes de morts, a sub-grouping of les cohortes nocturnes
(Lecouteux does not cite Trithemius). The second and third tales involve the chase
of the Wild Huntsman through the sky. Trithemius fixes each occurrence of the
Hunt with chronological and spatial markers. But these prove unstable, revealing
themselves to be a narrative tactic aimed at granting the Wild Hunt plausibility
and credibility.
Our thesis is that Trithemius, receptive to the idea that the Hunt had maniÔøæfested itself in recorded history, and hesitant to interpret it from a uniformly ChrisÔøætian perspective, was eager to explore its dimensions¬†‚Äì especially the appearance
of spirits in bodily form. Fearful that too favorable reception of the lore of the
Hunt might contribute to his reputation for embracing topics incompatible with
Christianity, he chose a compensatory two-pronged technique. On the one hand,
he adopts a neutral stance, for example, regarding the physical perceptibility of
the dead, and reports on the same absent consistent moral application or judgÔøæment. On the other hand, he overlays tales of the Hunt with possible religious sigÔøænificance, for example, that the ghostly processions of the dead represent souls
in purgatory. He leaves it to the reader to determine the more reasonable interpreÔøætation. His brother clerics, in contrast, were eager to practice Christian exegesis
on tales of the Wild Hunt.
The Troop of the Dead: The Procession of Souls on
the Hunt

warriors. This noisy throng, composed of cavalry and foot soldiers, is in constant
motion, moving in and out of a mountain near the German city of Worms. TritheÔøæmius here chronicles the version of the Hunt called les troupes de morts (LecouÔøæteux 1999, 35‚Äì44). It involves ‚Äúa procession of the human dead [‚Ä¶] wandering to

TRITHEMIUS, CUSANUS, AND THE WILL TO THE INFINITE:
A PRE-FAUSTIAN PARADIGM
NOEL L. BRANN
Of past figures whose reported exploits have moved from the historical arena
to the arena of legend and even of myth, one of the more intriguing is a certain
Doctor Faustus (‚Ä† ca. 1540). That metamorphosis from historical to legendary
status, notably illustrated by the Lutheran-authored Faustbuch of 1587, had
already begun during its subject‚Äôs own lifetime. Initially construed as a deÔøæmonically inspired sorcerer, Faustus subsequently passed through a series of
further metamorphoses no longer holding to the relatively one-dimensional
image earlier projected.
Already with Christopher Marlowe, who was given access to an English
translation of the Faustbuch, the Faust legend had became transmogrified
from a series of superficial cautionary tales about the dangers of magic into a
deeper dramatic ‚Äútragedy‚Äù in which its central protagonist‚Äôs occult interests
were no longer so easily slighted. The simple religious conflict between the
righteousness of faith and the unrighteousness of demonically incited sorcery
had now become revamped into a conflict between two opposing versions of
how one can best be put into touch with a realm of the spirit transcending the
limitations of nature: one through demonic assistance and the other through
Christian faith. Migrating to the age of the Enlightenment, with a writer like
Lessing enhancing the tragical framework within which the Faust legend could
flourish, it was most famously picked up by Goethe and transformed into a
foremost prototype of the Romantic drive to transcend the finite limits laid
down by the classical heritage.
Further passing into the modern age, it is above all the Marlowe and Goethe
takes on the Faust legend that have had the most durable impact on the conÔøætemporary western mind. In this form the Faust image has been adapted to the
ballet stage by Heinrich Heine and to the opera stage by Berlioz, Gounod and
Boito; it represented for Kierkegaard a tormented ‚Äúapostate of the spirit‚Äù
epitomizing the existential disjunction between the finite and infinite domains;
it has been utilized as a historical metaphor by Spengler and Toynbee, the
former replacing the famous Dionysian-Apollonian antithesis with a FaustianÔøæApollonian antithesis and the latter associating Faust with the Yang side of
what he conceived to be a Yin-Yang dynamic of history; it served Thomas
Mann, in his novel Doktor Faustus, as an allegorical motif symbolizing a cor-
<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 2 / 20>
154 NOEL L. BRANN
relation between genius and psychic alienation; and, as illustrated by a recent
study applying the Faustian theme to a number of literary heroines, it has even
impacted on the modern feminist movement. In sum, the image of Faust has
evolved from one of a mere demon conjuror into a protean-like metaphor for a
heroic-like drive to surpass the bounds of finite mediocrity1
.
Faustus and Trithemius
Faustus, as it happened, was not alone among Renaissance magicians to furÔøænish the modern world with a striking Renaissance paradigm pitting an occult
pursuit of the transrational infinite against the finite limits of human reason.
He was notably anticipated by the Benedictine abbot Trithemius (1462-1516)
of Sponheim and W√ºrzburg2
, who, responding to a query by an acquaintance
cognizant of his burgeoning reputation in the same area of arcane interests as
attracted Faustus, offers us the first documented evidence of the historical perÔøæsonage behind the Faust legend in the form of a highly unflattering epistolary
portrait of its subject. Having by chance spent a night at the same Gelnhausen
inn as Faustus during the year 1506, Trithemius, in the relevant letter marking
this fortuitous intersection of the two magi, portrayed his fellow itinerant in
highly unflattering terms.
Puffing himself up as ‚Äòa fountain of necromantics‚Äô(fons necromanticorum)
Trithemius charged, Faustus in truth was nothing but ‚Äòa vagabond, an utterer of
vain repetitions, and a wandering monk‚Äô deserving, not of admiration, but of
‚Äòchastisement by whipping‚Äô. Boasting ‚Äòthat if all the volumes of Plato and
Aristotle, with all their philosophy, completely perished from the memory of
man, he by his genius, as if he were another Ezra the Hebrew, could restore
them with an even more superior elegance‚Äô, Faustus, in Trithemius‚Äô opinion,
‚Äòbeing ignorant of all good letters, ought rather to be called a fool than a masÔøæter‚Äô. To be sure, the main thrust of this portrayal is one that appears to present

more sinister Faustian reputation of later years. Going so far in his perversity

circumstances under which confraternities were originally founded and their
relationship to the rise of the mendicant orders in the cities. What was the changing
nature of their spiritual mission, and how closely were they connected to the
churches in which many held their meetings and to the clergy who served as their
chaplains and presumably as their spiritual advisors? What role did confraternities
play in the education of their members and in the development of liturgy? These
questions go far beyond the scope of the present study with its emphasis on social
networks. Professor Weissman is definitely to be congratulated for illuminating
one important side of confraternal life. The reader, however, is left longing for
a more comprehensive view of confraternities and their place in the religious and
cultural life of medieval and Renaissance cities.
Melissa Meriam Bullard

The Abbot Trithemius (1462-1516). The Renaissance of Monastic Humanism. By
Noel L. Brann. [Studies in the History of Christian Thought, Volume XXIV.]
(Leiden: E. J. Brill. 1981. Pp. xx, 400. Gld. 124,-.)
Of the two books published in the past ten years on the late fifteenth-century
monastic intellectual and reformer Johannes Trithemius, this is the longer and the
more supportive of Trithemius' claim to historical importance. It is also the more
restricted. Unlike Klaus Arnold's Johannes Trithemius, which appeared in 1971
and presented in little more than 200 pages of text a fascinating portrait of an
ambiguous, not to say self-contradictory, figure with an exciting career in the
German Renaissance, Noel Brann's new study devotes itself to a single theme, which
it pursues through the abbot's life with relentless single-mindedness. Brann's
picture, immensely detailed, is of a man passionately committed to the ideal of
monastic erudition?to the attainment of it in his own life and the propagation of it
in the lives of others. Scientia latet incuculliswas Trithemius' maxim."Knowledge
lies under a monk's hood" is the theme of Brann's book. In order to develop it, he
not only takes over Trithemius' favorite image of himself as a Christian pilgrim, but
also assumes the abbot's own outlook on the world and his contemporaries. This
results in an exceedingly informative, but also very biased treatment, the chief
objective of which seems to be to demonstrate that Renaissance humanism must
not be thought of as a lay monopoly.
This case can be considered proven. Trithemius was formidably versed in all the
subjects considered worth knowing in his time and culture. He was indefatigable as
a historian, biographer, theological commentator, and writer of letters. In his
twenty-two years as abbot of the Benedictine monastery of Sponheim (he was
elected at age 22!)

AMBIX, Vol. 26, Part 3, November 1979
GEORGE RIPLEY AND THE ABBOT TRITHEMIUS:
AN INQUIRY INTO CONTRASTING MEDICAL ATTITUDES
By NOEL L. BRANN*
IF we should take at face value the heading assigned to a section of an alchemical tract by
the fifteenth-century English cleric of Bridlington, George Ripley (d. 1490), included by
Lazarus Zetzner in his seventeenth century Theatrum Chemicum-"Physica-Chemica TriÔøæthemiea"-then the alchemical reputation of the German Benedictine Abbot of Sponheim
Johann Trithemius (1462-1516) took its shape well in advance of his far better kno'wn
steganographic reputation.! To accept this supposition, however, 'would be to run counter
to the premise of Trithemius's principal biographer Klaus Arnold that the magical fame of
the arcane-minded monk was not in manifest evidence prior to the inadvertent circulation of
a letter from his hand to the Carmelite monk Arnold Bastius in 1499 summarizing his partiÔøæally written 5teganographia with its claim to communicate ideas at long distances with the
help of spirits. 2 But inasmuch as the name of Trithemius does not crop up in the text of
Ripley's treatise, there is little need to disturb ourselves on this score. The heading was an
afterthought of the editor Zetzner, who thereby sought to draw a connection in the minds
of his readers between the Hermetic doctrines of the legendary Abbot Trithemius, the alÔøæchemical precepts of Ripley, and the arcane teachings of the sixteenth century spagyric
and true successor of Ripley in the advocacy of alchemical magic, Paracelsus (d. 1541).3
By no means was Zetzner the only seventeenth-century alchemical enthusiast to locate
Trithemius within the mainstream of the spagyrical movement culminating in the doctrines
of Paracelsus. Indeed, following a line taken the century before by the French Paracelsian
Jacques Gohory (= Leo Suavius, d. 1576), not a few of Zetzner's alchemical-minded conÔøætemporaries were of the belief that Trithemius and Paracelsus had developed a personal
association.4 To help buttress this widespread opinion a sizeable corpus of alchemical
writings, bearing such headings as Chemic'Us Nobilis, De lap ide Philosophico, Veterum
sophorum sigilla et imagines magicae, and the like, came to light under the abbot's name in
the first half of the seventeenth century.5 The Chemicus Nobilis, representing the sole
"Trithemius" selection in the Theatrum ChemicU111t,furnishes a typical expression of the state
of mind at the bottom of this spuriously attributed group of alchemical tracts:
He who desires to prolong to its conclusion anything arising from the great depths of
chemical knowledge must consider attentively the celestial bodies, and diligently
apprehend their qualities, natures, and positions. If he does so, he will discover, with
the help of divine grace, the insatiable intellect which lies within them.6
In this statement we. readily comprehend why the spagyrics placed so much stress upon
astrology-to the point, as we would infer from the above words, of considering it a sine
qua non of their alchemical operations. For all magical transformations here below, accordÔøæing to this way of thinking, are fundamentally bound up with corresponding occult changes
in the heavens above.

37916.

GEORGE RIPLEY AND THE ABBOT TRITHEMIUS
Although it is perfectly true that the th-esis of a link .between Trithemius and the
fifteenth-century spagyric Ripley cannot be sustained by the evidence, the principal reason
being that Ripley's life ante-dated the demonstrable nlagical career of the Sponheimabbot,
the allegation oia direct and personal link between- 'Trithemius and the sixteenth century
spagyric Paracelsus is much more difficult to discount.7 Aside from the interesting question
of whether Trithemius and Paracelsus ever actually met one another, there is much in the
abbot's genuine post-:I499writings to lend substance tothe notion of Trithemi'Us Alchemic'Us
upheld by the later Paracelsians. In particular, it ,can be established that Trithemius
consciously based his magical theory upon the Emerald Table (Tabula Smaragdina) of
Hermes Trismegistus, the thirteen precepts of which have served alchemists through the
centuries as a basic creed of their art.8 The key document to consult in verification of this
claim is a letter addressed by Trithemius to the Frenchman Germanus de Ganay in the late
summer of 1505, written in reply to a previous letter from Ganay in which the correspondent
had informed Trithemius that he had come into possession of an epistle from the abbot to a
disciple making obscure reference to Ha very rare and and admirable philosophy, shrouded in
numbers, elements, and enigmas, and abstruse with arcane words", and requested
Trithe~ius to furnish the writer with an explanation. Tritheniius's answer was a virtual
playback of the Hermetic. Emerald Table.9
Thus~ in elucidation of the second and third precepts of the Emerald Table, the second
declaring that "what is superior is like that which is inferior and what is inferior is like that
which is superior ... , so that there maybe accomplished many-miracles of one thing",
and the third that Hall things ¬∑were produced from this one thing by adaptation", Tlithemius
inquires of Ganay: "Is it not true that all things flow from one thing, from the goodness of

Was Paracelsus a Disciple of Trithemius?
Noel L. Brann
Huntington Library
WAS THE RENOWNED alchemical physician Theophrastus Bombastus von
Hohenheim (1493-1541), better known to posterity as Paracelsus, a student of
the Benedictine Abbot Trithernius (1462-1516) of steganographic fame?' This
question sticks like a thorn in the flesh of Paracelsian research. Until recently
it has been taken as more or less an unquestioned axiom of Paracelsus scholarÔøæship, based in the last analysis on nothing more substantial than a single
ambiguous reference by Paracelsus in his Die Grosse Wundarznei (1536) to the
early influences upon his life which had led him to become interested in
alchemy, that at some time in his youth Paracelsus managed to make his way
to Trithernius at home in his second cloister of St. Jacob in Wuirzburg (the
abbot was formerly in charge of St. Martin at Sponheim) for instruction in
the arcana. Among those introducing him into the adept philosophy, ParaÔøæcelsus reported, were his father Wilhelm, four bishops (cited by name), "many
abbots, as the abbot of Sponheim (vil ept, als von Spanheim), and many
others of like persuasion."2 As Will-Erich Peuckert aptly echoed the typical

(1462-1516) (Wiirzburg: Schoningh, 1971), pp. 180 ff. Trithemius had conceived and
composed two books and part of a third book of his projected eight-book Steganographia
by 1500. However, as the result of the vehement commotion stirred up by the inadvertÔøæent circulation of a letter he had penned the year before to the just-deceased Gent
Carmelite Arnold Bostius, outlining the planned tract's contents, and of a bitter denunciaÔøætion of his steganographic magic for being demon-riddled by the French philosopher
Carolus Bovillus in a widely disseminated letter to Germanus de Ganay reputedly dated
1509, Trithemius decided to leave the Steganographia stillborn. But the abbot did not
thereupon desist from his cryptological efforts, following up the controversial steganoÔøægraphic handbook with a safer, entirely spirit-free Polygraphia, completed by 1508 and
posthumously printed a decade later at Basel. The completed portions of the SteganoÔøægraphia were printed at Frankfort a/M in 1606.

72 The Sixteenth Century Journal
response of Paracelsus scholars to this passage in his Pansophie of 1936:
"There is no doubt whom he meant. For at Sponheim no one but Trithemius
had occupied himself with the light of nature, that is, the philosophia
adepta. . . Trithemius was indeed [Paracelsus'] teacher."3
This logical inference, however, has not passed entirely unchallenged.
Indeed, the very year of 1936, which had brought Peuckert's Pansophie to
light, also saw the publication of a new Paracelsus biography by Karl Sudhoff
radically contesting the customary interpretation of Paracelsus' phrase: "vil
ept, als von Spanheim." By the 1536 reference to an abbot of Sponheim
among his early instructors in the arcana, Sudhoff determined, Paracelsus was
not really thinking of Trithemius, as "an old biographical error" in Paracelsian
studies would have it, but on a long-dead twelfth century abbot of St. Paul in
Lavanttal by the name of Bruno, the second in the line of abbots of the
cloister (1117-1140) after its foundation in 1091, who through his father,
Count Bernhard of Sponheim and Lavant, could claim noble lineage going
back to the days of the Carolingians. Somehow Paracelsus had confused
matters while taking Latin lessons from the current abbot of Lavanttal, if we
are to believe Sudhoff, so that, misled by the inscription on the portal of St.
Paul and on the family gravestone marking the spot at which the Sponheim
counts were buried, he inadvertently interchanged the title of the abbot of
Lavanttal with that of the abbot of Sponheim.4
Though this re-interpretation of the key Paracelsus passage by Sudhoff
proved to be sufficiently peruasive to his former student Franz Strunz, who
incorporated it into his Paracelsus biography of the following year (1937), it
was far from convincing to others of a more unyielding disposition.5 Peuckert,
Fuger von Schwaz mit sampt einer anzal seiner gehaltenen laboranten." All subsequent
citations from Paracelsus are from this same edition.

schwarzen Magie (Stuttgart: Kohihammer, 1936), p. 228. Cf

J. R. Partington, ‚ÄúTrithemius and Alchemy‚Äù (Ambix 1938)
Partington reconstructs Trithemius‚Äôs life, career, and reputation with particular attention to the question of whether he should be counted as an alchemist in any substantive sense. He situates Trithemius as a Benedictine reformer and polymath rather than as a laboratory practitioner, emphasizing his extraordinary role as abbot of Sponheim and later W√ºrzburg, his rebuilding of monastic discipline, and his creation of one of the most impressive monastic libraries of the period. Partington stresses that Trithemius‚Äôs fame rested primarily on historiography, theology, chronography, and cryptography, especially the Steganographia and Polygraphia, works that were widely misread as manuals of magical conjuration. He carefully disentangles Trithemius‚Äôs own writings from later alchemical and magical texts spuriously attributed to him in the seventeenth century, noting how Paracelsians and compilers of the Theatrum Chemicum retrospectively constructed ‚ÄúTrithemius the alchemist.‚Äù While acknowledging Trithemius‚Äôs deep interest in Hermetic, numerological, and cosmological speculation, Partington concludes that there is no firm evidence he practiced alchemy in the technical sense; rather, his importance lies in shaping a learned discourse in which natural philosophy, astrology, theology, and symbolic interpretation overlapped. The essay is cautious, archival, and skeptical, exemplifying early twentieth-century historiography that resists romanticizing Renaissance esotericism while still recognizing Trithemius‚Äôs central role in its transmission. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn0file0ÓàÅ

Budiman, Rachmawati, Jessica, ‚ÄúImplementation of Super-Encryption with Trithemius Algorithm and Double Transposition Cipher‚Ä¶‚Äù (JPCS 2018)
This paper recontextualizes Trithemius as a namesake within modern computer science by treating the Trithemius cipher as a classical polyalphabetic algorithm suitable for combination with other encryption methods. The authors explain the historical Trithemius table and then expand it into a modernized character set including uppercase, lowercase, numerals, and punctuation. They implement a ‚Äúsuper-encryption‚Äù scheme by applying Trithemius substitution followed by double transposition, targeting PDF file security on Android devices. The study focuses on algorithmic complexity, execution time, and practical feasibility rather than historical interpretation. Results show quadratic time complexity Œò(n¬≤) for the combined method, with Trithemius encryption proving faster than double transposition, and processing time scaling linearly with plaintext and key length. The paper exemplifies how Renaissance cryptographic ideas survive as pedagogical and conceptual ancestors within contemporary information security, stripped of their metaphysical associations and reinterpreted as neutral technical tools. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn0file1ÓàÅ

Charles G. Nauert, ‚ÄúThe Author of a Renaissance Commentary on Pliny: Rivius, Trithemius or Aquaeus?‚Äù (JWCI 1979)
Nauert addresses a bibliographical and intellectual puzzle concerning the authorship of a 1548 W√ºrzburg commentary on Pliny‚Äôs Natural History dealing with magic. Although Trithemius‚Äôs name appears prominently in appended apologetic material and in the cultural memory of the text, Nauert demonstrates through careful analysis of initials, printing history, and stylistic evidence that the principal author was almost certainly Walther Hermann Ryff (Rivius), not Trithemius or Aquaeus. Trithemius‚Äôs presence is shown to be ancillary and reputational: his authority on occult philosophy was invoked to lend legitimacy to discussions of magic, fascination, and incantation. The article illustrates how Trithemius functioned as a symbolic guarantor of learned magic in mid-sixteenth-century print culture even when he was not the author, revealing how his name circulated as a mark of intellectual credibility in debates over natural versus illicit magic. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn0file2ÓàÅ

E. C. Harrington, ‚ÄúJohannes Trithemius‚Äù (Notes and Queries 1851)
This short nineteenth-century antiquarian notice reflects early English-language interest in Trithemius as a learned curiosity rather than a systematic subject of historical analysis. Harrington presents brief biographical remarks and bibliographical observations, drawing on earlier German sources and emphasizing Trithemius‚Äôs reputation as an erudite abbot whose name had become entangled with occult speculation. The piece exemplifies Victorian scholarly habits: fragmentary, source-driven, and more concerned with cataloguing references than interpreting intellectual contexts. Trithemius appears here as a figure hovering between respectable learning and dubious magic, a tone that foreshadows later debates over his place in Renaissance intellectual history. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn0file3ÓàÅ

William C. McDonald, ‚ÄúTrithemius and the Legend of the Wild Hunt‚Äù (Fabula 2018)
McDonald analyzes Trithemius as a chronicler of popular supernatural belief, focusing on four passages in his chronicles that reference variants of the Wild Hunt legend. These include accounts of phantom armies, cursed nocturnal hunters, and airborne sorcerers, one explicitly identified as a Jewish magician producing illusions. McDonald argues that Trithemius showed little skepticism toward these phenomena, instead carefully dating and localizing them to enhance credibility. While Trithemius occasionally overlays Christian moral interpretations‚Äîsuch as purgatorial readings of ghostly processions‚Äîthe religious framing remains thin and inconsistent. The article positions Trithemius as unusually receptive to folkloric and visionary material, treating it as historically real rather than merely symbolic or delusional. This reading complicates portrayals of Trithemius as a purely learned humanist by showing his willingness to integrate popular myth, demonology, and sensory deception into historical narrative. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn0file4ÓàÅ

Noel L. Brann, ‚ÄúTrithemius, Cusanus, and the Will to the Infinite‚Äù (Aries 2002)
Brann situates Trithemius within a pre-Faustian intellectual paradigm concerned with transcending the finite limits of human knowledge. Comparing Trithemius to Nicholas of Cusa and contrasting him with the later Faust legend, Brann emphasizes Trithemius‚Äôs commitment to a spiritually sanctioned pursuit of the infinite through Hermetic, Neoplatonic, and Kabbalistic frameworks. Central to the essay is Trithemius‚Äôs famous denunciation of Faustus as a fraudulent necromancer, which paradoxically contributed to later conflations of their legends. Brann argues that unlike Faust, Trithemius left an extensive written record articulating his motivations: magic was legitimate insofar as it was aligned with divine order rather than demonic deception. The essay portrays Trithemius as a transitional figure whose intellectual ambitions helped generate the very mythic structures that later cast Renaissance magic as dangerous hubris. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn0file5ÓàÅ

Gerald Strauss, Review of Noel L. Brann, The Abbot Trithemius (Catholic Historical Review 1983)
Strauss‚Äôs review evaluates Brann‚Äôs monograph as a deeply researched but highly focused interpretation of Trithemius as a champion of monastic humanism. He praises the exhaustive documentation of Trithemius‚Äôs learning, productivity, and reformist zeal, especially the argument that Renaissance humanism was not solely a lay phenomenon. At the same time, Strauss criticizes the book‚Äôs narrowness and sympathetic bias, noting that Brann largely adopts Trithemius‚Äôs own self-image and neglects the ambiguities, contradictions, and broader cultural tensions highlighted by other scholars. The review situates Trithemius historiographically, underscoring ongoing debates over whether he should be read primarily as a humanist reformer, an esoteric thinker, or an unstable blend of both. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn0file6ÓàÅ

Hans-Martin Kirn on Paola Zambelli, White Magic, Black Magic in the European Renaissance (CHRC 2008)
Kirn‚Äôs review summarizes Zambelli‚Äôs treatment of Trithemius within a broader genealogy of Renaissance magic from Ficino and Pico to Agrippa and Bruno. Trithemius is characterized as representing a more ritualistic and practical form of magic, distinct from the more literary and philosophical approaches of Italian humanists. The review highlights Trithemius‚Äôs ‚Äúdouble strategy‚Äù: advocating esoteric ceremonial practices while publicly condemning witchcraft and demonic magic. This tension is interpreted as partly a response to censorship and inquisitorial pressure. Kirn underscores Zambelli‚Äôs suggestion that Trithemius‚Äôs wide-ranging interests‚Äîfrom angelology to historical fabrication‚Äîform a coherent but playful intellectual ‚Äúgame,‚Äù raising questions about sincerity, irony, and strategy in Renaissance esotericism. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn0file7ÓàÅ

Noel L. Brann, ‚ÄúGeorge Ripley and the Abbot Trithemius‚Äù (Ambix 1979)
Brann investigates alleged links between Trithemius and the English alchemist George Ripley, ultimately rejecting claims of direct influence while explaining why later Paracelsians believed such connections existed. He shows how seventeenth-century editors and alchemical enthusiasts retrospectively positioned Trithemius as a central authority within spagyric traditions, attributing to him texts and doctrines aligned with Paracelsian astrology and Hermetic cosmology. The article closely analyzes a genuine 1505 letter in which Trithemius expounds the Emerald Tablet, demonstrating his deep engagement with Hermetic symbolism even if he was not a practicing alchemist. Brann portrays Trithemius as a theoretical architect of magical-cosmological thought whose authority was later amplified by spurious attributions. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn0file8ÓàÅ

Noel L. Brann, ‚ÄúWas Paracelsus a Disciple of Trithemius?‚Äù (Sixteenth Century Journal 1979)
Brann addresses the long-standing question of whether Paracelsus studied directly under Trithemius. Through meticulous textual and historiographical analysis, he shows how a single ambiguous reference by Paracelsus generated a durable scholarly myth. Brann reviews competing interpretations, including Karl Sudhoff‚Äôs attempt to redirect the reference to a different abbot, and weighs documentary evidence concerning chronology, geography, and intellectual transmission. While acknowledging ideological affinities between Trithemius‚Äôs Hermetic theology and Paracelsian medicine, Brann concludes that direct discipleship cannot be proven. The article exemplifies Brann‚Äôs broader project of disentangling Trithemius‚Äôs authentic intellectual legacy from later mythmaking while still affirming his profound indirect influence on sixteenth-century esoteric thought. [L18] T he authorship of the unsigned comÔøæmentary on two chapters of Pliny's
[L19] Natural History (xxx, ch. 1-2, or sect. 1-18
[L20] in the modern numeration) dealing with
[L21] magic, published at Wiurzburg in 1548 by
[L22] Johann Myller,1 poses a problem that has
[L23] misled nearly all bibliographers. Although
[L24] neither the title-page nor the text of the
[L25] commentary identifies the author, the titleÔøæpage does carry a cryptic set of initials which
[L26] can readily be decoded: 'D.G.H.R. M. & M.'
[L27] The student of mid-sixteenth-century German
[L28] scientific literature can decipher this line as
[L29] an abbreviation for 'Dominus Gualtherus
[L30] Hermenius Rivius, Medicus et MatheÔøæmaticus'. Rivius (in German, Walther
[L31] Hermann Ryff) was a prolific writer on
[L32] medical, biological, occultist and other subÔøæjects: his modern bibliographer lists a total
[L33] of 194 titles and editions, including postÔøæhumous ones.2 Furthermore, the printer
[L34] Myller is known to have published other
[L35] books by him at precisely this period.3
[L36] 1 In Caii Plinii Secundi Naturalis historiae argutisÔøæsimi scriptoris I. & II. Cap. Libri XXX. commenÔøætarius, naturalis quidem magiae compendiariam
[L37] rationem, plenam mysteriis, profundissimamque rerum
[L38] secretissimarum contemplationem, naturam, potenÔøætiam, qualitatem, substantiam & virtutem, totiusque
[L39] naturae cognitionem complectens. Cui praeterea
[L40] adiecta est De fascinationibus disputatio elegans &
[L41] erudita, ad cognoscendosque naturae secretiores
[L42] effectus mire utilis & necessaria. Item de incantatione
[L43] & adiuratione, collique suspensione epistola incerti
[L44] authoris, una cum loannis Tritemii abbatis SpancheyÔøæmensis viri doctissimi, occultiorisque philosophiae
[L45] atque magicarum artium indagatoris solertissimi, in
[L46] libros suos Steganographiae epistola apologetica &c.
[L47] Quibus difficultates Plinianae, praesertim magicae,

[L1] During recent
[L2] work on a census of medieval and RenaisÔøæsance commentaries on Pliny, I found that
[L3] the Catalogue gindral des livres imprimis of the
[L4] Bibliotheque Nationale in Paris ascribes to
[L5] another writer, ttienne de L'Aigue (in
[L6] Latin, Stephanus Aquaeus), authorship of
[L7] the commentary in Myller's Wiirzburg
[L8] edition of 1548, the one here under disÔøæcussion. The Paris catalogue entry makes no
[L9] reference to Ryff, not even as editor. ComÔøæparison of the 1548 commentary with the
[L10] same chapters in the complete commentary
[L11] by L'Aigue, In omnes C. Plinii Secundi Naturalis
[L12] historiae arguitissimi scriptoris libros (Paris 1530)
[L13] shows that in compiling the volume here
[L14] under study, Ryff reprinted the complete
[L15] text of L'Aigue's commentary on the first
[L16] two chapters of Pliny's Book XXX.10 The
[L17] only changes he made seem to have been
[L18] calculated to suppress materials which might
[L19] offend the occultist readers for whom the
[L20] book was intended, and perhaps also to
[L21] disguise his source. In the commentary on
[L22] Chapter I of Book XXX, Ryff suppressed
[L23] the lemma at the opening of L'Aigue's comÔøæmentary, 'Magicas vanitates, &c,' and on
[L24] fol. A2r changed the words 'in hoc magiae
[L25] praetexta acturi sumus' (L'Aigue, fol.
[L26] CCCXXVIIv) into the more neutral 'in
[L27] hoc Magiae compendio acturi sumus'. A

[L29] editions), Aquaeus's rather neutral words,

[L31] apicem, ob id constituimus hic de ea eiusque
[L32] generibus agere, primum de duobus hic, et
[L33] caeteris capitulo sequenti', have become more
[L34] positive towards magic: 'scilicet Magiam

[L1] Elsewhere we show that Trithemius created the myth of Sedechias the master
[L2] magician, borrowing details from monastic chronicles (McDonald 2015‚Äì2016). In
[L3] so doing, Trithemius affirms one of the most persistent and malign tropes of the
[L4] era: the purported relationship of Jews to black magic¬†‚Äì even witchcraft. Newall
[L5] reminds us about the inter-connectedness of the legends of the Wandering Jew (der
[L6] ewige Jude) and the Wild Huntsman (Newall 1973, esp. 103‚Äì104; cf. Simrock 1878,
[L7] 206). A popular designation for the Hunt, she reminds us, was ‚ÄúCain‚Äôs Hunt,‚Äù
[L8] Cain being the progenitor of the Wandering Jew. At the very least, Newall gives
[L9] the Hunt a Jewish imprint, a perspective that we believe Trithemius exploited (Cf.
[L10] Trachtenberg 1993, 66). It is a fact that authors of the following centuries were
[L11] quick to brand Sedechias a black magician and a walking spirit, or specter whose
[L12] tricks are demonically inspired (Lavater 1569, 91). One claimed that Sedechias
[L13] ‚Äúwar ein so gro√üer Schwartz-K√ºnstler/ da√ü de√ü Pharaonis Zauberer gegen ihm
[L14] [sic] nur f√ºr Lehr-Sch√ºler zu achten/ er flog in der Lufft herum wie Simon Magus‚Äù
[L15] (Schudt 1714, 6th book, 210).
[L16] The Sedechias-figure as seen in Trithemius is complex, serving as the locus
[L17] and ingathering point for legends and tropes. One of his habitats, as expressed
[L18] by Robert Harrison, sixteenth-century translator of Ludwig Lavater (1527‚Äì1586),
[L19] is ‚Äúthe aire and cloudes.‚Äù In that place, Harrison continues, ‚Äúhe was seene and
[L20] hearde of all men to exercise hunting [‚Ä¶]‚Äù (Harrison 1572, 168). This is a referÔøæence, we have argued, to the Wild Hunt.
[L21] Conclusion
[L22] Trithemius treats four occurrences of the Wild Hunt, showing good awareness of
[L23] its lore. One passage concerns the Troop of the Dead; another treats the Cursed
[L24] 19‚ÄÇTrithemius offers two endings to his Sedechias anecdote, the already cited allusion to the
[L25] Wild Hunt (Chronicon Hirsaugiense, ed. Freher, 18), and the general reference in the Annales HirÔøæsaugienses (ed. Schlegel, vol. 1, 34) regarding the role of demonic aid in his trickery: ‚ÄúMulta his
[L26] operabatur similia, quae vel daemonum auxilio, vel alicujus subtilissimae fictionis similitudine,
[L27] ut pictura vesicarum facta fuisse creduntur.‚Äù (He wrought many things like this, which were
[L28] believed to have been made with the aid of demons or by the likeness of some crafty fiction¬†‚Äìas,
[L29] for example, the apparent projection of images.) Note that for this latter citation Trithemius gives
[L30] the year as 879, not 878, as he had done elsewhere. See previous note.

[L15] This logical inference, however, has not passed entirely unchallenged.
[L16] Indeed, the very year of 1936, which had brought Peuckert's Pansophie to
[L17] light, also saw the publication of a new Paracelsus biography by Karl Sudhoff
[L18] radically contesting the customary interpretation of Paracelsus' phrase: "vil
[L19] ept, als von Spanheim." By the 1536 reference to an abbot of Sponheim
[L20] among his early instructors in the arcana, Sudhoff determined, Paracelsus was
[L21] not really thinking of Trithemius, as "an old biographical error" in Paracelsian
[L22] studies would have it, but on a long-dead twelfth century abbot of St. Paul in
[L23] Lavanttal by the name of Bruno, the second in the line of abbots of the
[L24] cloister (1117-1140) after its foundation in 1091, who through his father,
[L25] Count Bernhard of Sponheim and Lavant, could claim noble lineage going
[L26] back to the days of the Carolingians. Somehow Paracelsus had confused
[L27] matters while taking Latin lessons from the current abbot of Lavanttal, if we
[L28] are to believe Sudhoff, so that, misled by the inscription on the portal of St.
[L29] Paul and on the family gravestone marking the spot at which the Sponheim
[L30] counts were buried, he inadvertently interchanged the title of the abbot of
[L31] Lavanttal with that of the abbot of Sponheim.4

[L33] proved to be sufficiently peruasive to his former student Franz Strunz, who

[L2] All use subject to JSTOR Terms and Conditions<IMAGE FOR PAGE: 6 / 14><PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 7 / 14>Was Paracelsus a Disciple of Trithemius? 75
[L3] Of the assorted aids traditionally available to those who have sought to
[L4] back up their claim that it was indeed Trithemius who was meant by ParaÔøæcelsus in his reference to an "ept von Spanheim" among his earliest teachers,
[L5] one of the more frequently invoked has been a letter reputedly from the hand
[L6] of Paracelsus now recognized to be spurious. The pseudo-Paracelsus behind
[L7] this letter, which first came to light in the anonymous Alchimia Vera of 1604
[L8] under the heading "Theophrastus schreibet an einen seiner guten Freund vom
[L9] Lapide Philosophico Anno 1534," attributed "this secret of our art" (diese
[L10] Geheimnis unser Kunst) first of all to God, but secondarily to certain excepÔøætional mortals privileged to mediate between God and man for its transmisÔøæsion. Among these great mortal exponents of the dark philosophy, we are
[L11] further informed, "the great and secret philosopher of the German nation,
[L12] that is, Father Trithemius, the abbot of Sponheim, produced many million
[L13] gold-pieces with the highest tincture."1 1
[L14] Aside from the fact that this letter implying Paracelsus' great admiration
[L15] of Trithemius is a stark forgery of a later day, it also outrightly contradicts
[L16] the abbot's many strident warnings against the very practice therein indicated.
[L17] Such a use (or rather misuse) of alchemy was, in Trithemius' mind, an expresÔøæsion, not of virtue, but of vice - that is, of the vice of avarice. Trithemius'
[L18] attitude toward the attempt to increase material wealth through alchemical
[L19] means is effectively recorded in a memorable excursus upon the art in his
[L20] Hirsau Annals apropos of the fourteenth century alchemist Johannes
[L21] Rupescissa:
[L22] Alchemy, as we say in the human manner, is a chaste whore (casta
[L23] meretrix), who has many lovers but by deluding them all is successfully
[L24] embraced by none among them. From foolish men it makes insane ones,
[L25] from rich men poor ones, from philosophers fatuous men, from deceived
[L26] men very garrulous deceivers, all who, though they know nothing, proÔøæfess to know all things, and although they are very poor promise to give
[L27] their followers the riches of Croesus. Hence are their goals full of conÔøæfusion.1 2
[L28] ' ' I.P.S.M.S., Alchimia Vera, Das ist: Der wahren und von Gott /zochbenedeyten
[L29] Naturgemessen Edlen Kunst Alchimnia wahre beschreibung (n.p., 1604), p. 58: "Diese
[L30] Geheimnis unser Kunst ist von Gott sonderlichen Leuten mermeindt die andern armen
[L31] Leuten zur Ehre Gottes darmit helffende wie dann der grosse und geheime Philosophus
[L32] Deudtscher Nation quasi noster Pater Thritemius, Abt zu Spanheim viel Million Goldes
[L33] mit der h6chsten Tinctur er6bert...."

[L4] Finally, his titles appear in precisely the form
[L5] indicated above on both the title-page and
[L6] the dedicatory epistle of his best known work,
[L7] Vitruvius Teutsch (Nirnberg, 1548), the first
[L8] German translation of Vitruvius's De archiÔøætectura: 'D. Gualtherus H. Rivius, Medicus
[L9] et Mathematicus.'
[L10] The volume which contains the comÔøæmentary on Pliny's chapters on magic also
[L11] contains other texts: the two chapters of
[L12] Pliny covered by the commentary; an unÔøæattributed Defascinationibus disputatio elegans et
[L13] erudita; an unattributed De incantatione et
[L14] adiuratione collique suspensione epistola incerti
[L15] authoris which Lynn Thorndike has identified
[L16] as a ninth-century work of Costa ben Luca ;4
[L17] and an apology by the learned abbot
[L18] Johannes Trithemius of Spanheim for his
[L19] occultist book Steganographia, here entitled In
[L20] libros suos Steganographiae epistola apologetica.
[L21] As Thorndike has noted, the whole book
[L22] catered to the sixteenth-century taste for
[L23] magic and the other occult arts. Ryff's role
[L24] as editor of the volume is evident from the
[L25] abbreviation on the title-page and from his
[L26] known association with the publisher. His
[L27] authorship of the commentary on Pliny's
[L28] chapters has seemed to follow from his work
[L29] as compiler of the entire volume of which it is
[L30] the most important part, and most biblioÔøægraphers have regarded Ryff as the comÔøæmentator. 5
[L31] This attribution, though incorrect, has
[L32] seemed plausible in large part because
[L33] interest in a section of Pliny that dealt with
[L34] natural magic would have come easily to
[L35] Ryff. Although there is no evidence except
[L36] his own claims to prove that he actually held
[L37] degrees in medicine or even in mathematical
[L38] arts, there is some evidence that he practised
[L39] medicine in several German towns: at
[L40] Gi-strow in Mecklenburg (where he may have
[L41] worked as a pharmacist rather than as a
[L42] physician, and where he probably met the
[L43] woman from Rostock whom he married), at
[L44] Strasbourg, Frankfurt-am-Main, Mainz,
[L45] Ntirnberg and Wiirzburg. His real proÔøæfession, however, was neither medicine nor
[L46] pharmacy but writing; and he was closely
[L47] associated with a number of important West
[L48] German printers, including Jakob CammerÔøælander, Balthasar Beck and Georg MesserÔøæschmidt of Strasbourg; Christian Egenolff of
[L49] Frankfurt; Johann Petreius ofNtirnberg; and
[L50] the printer of the Pliny commentary, Johann
[L51] Myller of Wiirzburg. For several of these
[L52] printers, Ryff appears to have functioned as
[L53] the firm's resident author and editor. He
[L54] was (to use a descriptive term which has
[L55] unfortunately dropped out of current usage
[L56] though not out of current practice) a literary
[L57] hack, engaged in producing works designed
[L58] to appeal to the large book-buying public of
[L59] Germany.

[L1] 44‚Äì46). Among the fields that he
[L2] investigated were the Hermetic tradition, number mysticism, alchemy, astrology,
[L3] Cabala, angelic mediation, geomancy, steganography, exorcism, and witchcraft.
[L4] His chronicles hold allusions to demons, ghosts, and magic in all its forms.3
[L5] To the long list of marvelous and supernatural topics explored by Trithemius
[L6] we add the Wild Hunt. It is surprising that only a single reference to the Hunt has
[L7] been located in his chronicles. Lecouteux labels that motif le chasseur maudit
[L8] (hereinafter the Cursed Hunter), the tale of the undead Huntsman expiating a sin
[L9] committed during his lifetime (Lecouteux 1999, 73). We have discovered three

[L17] additional allusions, the first being the Troop of the Dead, which coincides with
[L18] Lecouteux‚Äô category les troupes de morts, a sub-grouping of les cohortes nocturnes
[L19] (Lecouteux does not cite Trithemius). The second and third tales involve the chase
[L20] of the Wild Huntsman through the sky. Trithemius fixes each occurrence of the
[L21] Hunt with chronological and spatial markers. But these prove unstable, revealing
[L22] themselves to be a narrative tactic aimed at granting the Wild Hunt plausibility
[L23] and credibility.
[L24] Our thesis is that Trithemius, receptive to the idea that the Hunt had maniÔøæfested itself in recorded history, and hesitant to interpret it from a uniformly ChrisÔøætian perspective, was eager to explore its dimensions¬†‚Äì especially the appearance
[L25] of spirits in bodily form. Fearful that too favorable reception of the lore of the
[L26] Hunt might contribute to his reputation for embracing topics incompatible with
[L27] Christianity, he chose a compensatory two-pronged technique. On the one hand,
[L28] he adopts a neutral stance, for example, regarding the physical perceptibility of
[L29] the dead, and reports on the same absent consistent moral application or judgÔøæment. On the other hand, he overlays tales of the Hunt with possible religious sigÔøænificance, for example, that the ghostly processions of the dead represent souls
[L30] in purgatory. He leaves it to the reader to determine the more reasonable interpreÔøætation. His brother clerics, in contrast, were eager to practice Christian exegesis
[L31] on tales of the Wild Hunt.

[L18] Abstract: It is well-established that Johannes Trithemius (1462‚Äì1516), the
[L19] renowned polymath (theologian, cryptographer, and chronicler), entertained an
[L20] avid interest in all things concerning magic. Scholars have neglected, however, to
[L21] pay heed to his views about The Wild Hunt/Wild Huntsman, a widely propagated
[L22] tapestry of legends rooted in popular mythology. We examine four passages from
[L23] his chronicles to shed light on the many facets of this lore. The subjects of these
[L24] testimonies include: a phantom army; a cursed nocturnal hunter; and two aerial
[L25] hunting sorcerers, one of whom was a Jewish magician who conjured the illusion
[L26] of venery by deceiving the senses. The writings of Trithemius offer little indicaÔøætion of skepticism about the phantasmal chase. On the contrary, the abbot proÔøævides a precise chronology (878‚Äì1354) of these extraordinary tales of the hunt in
[L27] order to lend credibility to them. Each entry can be read as a cautionary example,
[L28] but the religious veneer seems shallow.
[L29] William C. McDonald, Professor, German and Jewish Studies, University of Virginia, CharlottesÔøæville, Virginia, USA 22904, E-Mail: wcm@virginia.edu

[L1] This is a referÔøæence, we have argued, to the Wild Hunt.
[L2] Conclusion
[L3] Trithemius treats four occurrences of the Wild Hunt, showing good awareness of
[L4] its lore. One passage concerns the Troop of the Dead; another treats the Cursed
[L5] 19‚ÄÇTrithemius offers two endings to his Sedechias anecdote, the already cited allusion to the
[L6] Wild Hunt (Chronicon Hirsaugiense, ed. Freher, 18), and the general reference in the Annales HirÔøæsaugienses (ed. Schlegel, vol. 1, 34) regarding the role of demonic aid in his trickery: ‚ÄúMulta his
[L7] operabatur similia, quae vel daemonum auxilio, vel alicujus subtilissimae fictionis similitudine,
[L8] ut pictura vesicarum facta fuisse creduntur.‚Äù (He wrought many things like this, which were
[L9] believed to have been made with the aid of demons or by the likeness of some crafty fiction¬†‚Äìas,
[L10] for example, the apparent projection of images.) Note that for this latter citation Trithemius gives
[L11] the year as 879, not 878, as he had done elsewhere. See previous note.

[L13] Authenticated
[L14] Download Date | 12/5/18 1:17 PM <PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 17 / 22> Trithemius and the Legend of the Wild Hunt 211
[L15] Hunter; and two regard flying Huntsmen. At first glance these stories, sometimes
[L16] separated by centuries, betray no authorial design. A closer reading reveals that
[L17] Trithemius wished to explore various configurations of the legend complex, and
[L18] that his choice of the four was not random. Generalizations are possible. He
[L19] embeds each within the narrative frame of monastic history. Despite the presÔøæence of chronological and geographical markers, however, Trithemius aims to
[L20] contribute to the body of legend, not to the historical record. The apparatus of
[L21] history writing serves him as veneer, functioning to grant incredible stories a full
[L22] measure of credibility. Religious scaffolding proves equally unstable. Its presence
[L23] is accountable more for personal reasons of self-defense against accusations of
[L24] black magic than for the edification of the believer. Admittedly, each of his anecÔøædotes can be read to convey a moral lesson (the avoidance of evil, the need for
[L25] repentance, attachment to the world, and the like), but each episode of the Hunt
[L26] in Trithemius‚Äô hands can also be appreciated as an entertaining story because he
[L27] prefers to let the uncanny speak for itself. We have characterized this technique as
[L28] a neutrality of narration, meaning that Trithemius chronicles marvelous events
[L29] without consistent authorial intervention. By and large he trusts the reader to
[L30] judge whether the Wild Hunt deserves to be a warning example.
[L31] Finally, Trithemius reminds us that Sedechias and Theoodo are praestigiaÔøætores. One sixteenth-century author links the pejorative term praestigator with
[L32] the concept Gauckler, claiming that both ‚Äúmit h√ºlffe des Sathans der Menschen
[L33] Augen mit Zauberey vnnd Gesp√ºgn√º√ü verblenden vnnd betriegen/da√ü sie nicht
[L34] sehen die Dinge/so da sindt/vnd meynen sie sehen die Ding/so nicht sindt‚Äù (NigriÔøænus 1592, 22).

[L4] Abstract: It is well-established that Johannes Trithemius (1462‚Äì1516), the
[L5] renowned polymath (theologian, cryptographer, and chronicler), entertained an
[L6] avid interest in all things concerning magic. Scholars have neglected, however, to
[L7] pay heed to his views about The Wild Hunt/Wild Huntsman, a widely propagated
[L8] tapestry of legends rooted in popular mythology. We examine four passages from
[L9] his chronicles to shed light on the many facets of this lore. The subjects of these
[L10] testimonies include: a phantom army; a cursed nocturnal hunter; and two aerial
[L11] hunting sorcerers, one of whom was a Jewish magician who conjured the illusion
[L12] of venery by deceiving the senses. The writings of Trithemius offer little indicaÔøætion of skepticism about the phantasmal chase. On the contrary, the abbot proÔøævides a precise chronology (878‚Äì1354) of these extraordinary tales of the hunt in
[L13] order to lend credibility to them. Each entry can be read as a cautionary example,
[L14] but the religious veneer seems shallow.

[L22] Abstract. This study aims to combine the trithemus algorithm and double transposition cipher
[L23] in file security that will be implemented to be an Android-based application. The parameters being
[L24] examined are the real running time , and the complexity value. The type of file to be used is a file in
[L25] PDF format. The overall result shows that the complexity of the two algorithms with duper encryption
[L26] method is reported as Œò (ùëõ2).However, the processing time required in the encryption process uses
[L27] the Trithemius algorithm much faster than using the Double Transposition Cipher. With the length of
[L28] plaintext and password linearly proportional to the processing time.
[L29] 1. Introduction
[L30] The word ‚Äòcryptography‚Äô comes from Greek. According to the language, the word cryptography is
[L31] divided into two, namely ‚Äòcrypto‚Äô and ‚Äògraphia‚Äô. ‚ÄòCrypto‚Äô means secret and ‚Äògraphia‚Äô means writing.
[L32] According to its terminology, cryptography is the science and art of keeping messages safe when
[L33] messages are sent from one place to another [1]. Thus, cryptography can help us in preventing the arrival
[L34] of important information, into unauthorized hands. The purpose of cryptography is 4. ie confidentiality,
[L35] integrity, authentication,andnon-repudation. Confidentiality focuses on data confidentiality, integrity
[L36] focuses on received data is true and genuine data, authentication is done to prevent false data, and nonÔøærepudation aims to prevent the shielding of the data from the sender. Cryptography algorithm divided 2
[L37] that is, symmetric algorithm and asymmetric algorithm. However, in this study the algorithm used is a
[L38] classical algorithm, namely Trithemius Algorithm and double transposition cipher.
[L39] Trithemius Cipher is one of the polyalphabetic codes designed to be easier to use.

[L3] Trithemius within the mainstream of the spagyrical movement culminating in the doctrines

[L14] apprehend their qualities, natures, and positions. If he does so, he will discover, with

[L19] in the heavens above.

[L12] Of the two books published in the past ten years on the late fifteenth-century
[L13] monastic intellectual and reformer Johannes Trithemius, this is the longer and the
[L14] more supportive of Trithemius' claim to historical importance. It is also the more
[L15] restricted. Unlike Klaus Arnold's Johannes Trithemius, which appeared in 1971
[L16] and presented in little more than 200 pages of text a fascinating portrait of an
[L17] ambiguous, not to say self-contradictory, figure with an exciting career in the
[L18] German Renaissance, Noel Brann's new study devotes itself to a single theme, which
[L19] it pursues through the abbot's life with relentless single-mindedness. Brann's
[L20] picture, immensely detailed, is of a man passionately committed to the ideal of
[L21] monastic erudition?to the attainment of it in his own life and the propagation of it
[L22] in the lives of others. Scientia latet incuculliswas Trithemius' maxim."Knowledge
[L23] lies under a monk's hood" is the theme of Brann's book. In order to develop it, he
[L24] not only takes over Trithemius' favorite image of himself as a Christian pilgrim, but
[L25] also assumes the abbot's own outlook on the world and his contemporaries. This
[L26] results in an exceedingly informative, but also very biased treatment, the chief
[L27] objective of which seems to be to demonstrate that Renaissance humanism must
[L28] not be thought of as a lay monopoly.
[L29] This case can be considered proven. Trithemius was formidably versed in all the
[L30] subjects considered worth knowing in his time and culture. He was indefatigable as
[L31] a historian, biographer, theological commentator, and writer of letters. In his
[L32] twenty-two years as abbot of the Benedictine monastery of Sponheim (he was
[L33] elected at age 22!) he built a superb library of nearly 2,000 volumes on almost every
[L34] subject. The purpose of this collection, in addition to satisfying Trithemius' passion
[L35] for books, was to help raise the level and increase the refinement of monastic
[L36] learning, a cause of which Trithemius became the leading advocate in Germany. In
[L37] touch with everyone who counted?the list of his visitors and correspondents,

[L1] <PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 1 / 9>AMBIX, Vol. 26, Part 3, November 1979
[L2] GEORGE RIPLEY AND THE ABBOT TRITHEMIUS:
[L3] AN INQUIRY INTO CONTRASTING MEDICAL ATTITUDES
[L4] By NOEL L. BRANN*
[L5] IF we should take at face value the heading assigned to a section of an alchemical tract by
[L6] the fifteenth-century English cleric of Bridlington, George Ripley (d. 1490), included by
[L7] Lazarus Zetzner in his seventeenth century Theatrum Chemicum-"Physica-Chemica TriÔøæthemiea"-then the alchemical reputation of the German Benedictine Abbot of Sponheim
[L8] Johann Trithemius (1462-1516) took its shape well in advance of his far better kno'wn
[L9] steganographic reputation.! To accept this supposition, however, 'would be to run counter
[L10] to the premise of Trithemius's principal biographer Klaus Arnold that the magical fame of
[L11] the arcane-minded monk was not in manifest evidence prior to the inadvertent circulation of
[L12] a letter from his hand to the Carmelite monk Arnold Bastius in 1499 summarizing his partiÔøæally written 5teganographia with its claim to communicate ideas at long distances with the
[L13] help of spirits. 2 But inasmuch as the name of Trithemius does not crop up in the text of
[L14] Ripley's treatise, there is little need to disturb ourselves on this score. The heading was an
[L15] afterthought of the editor Zetzner, who thereby sought to draw a connection in the minds
[L16] of his readers between the Hermetic doctrines of the legendary Abbot Trithemius, the alÔøæchemical precepts of Ripley, and the arcane teachings of the sixteenth century spagyric
[L17] and true successor of Ripley in the advocacy of alchemical magic, Paracelsus (d. 1541).3
[L18] By no means was Zetzner the only seventeenth-century alchemical enthusiast to locate
[L19] Trithemius within the mainstream of the spagyrical movement culminating in the doctrines
[L20] of Paracelsus. Indeed, following a line taken the century before by the French Paracelsian
[L21] Jacques Gohory (= Leo Suavius, d. 1576), not a few of Zetzner's alchemical-minded conÔøætemporaries were of the belief that Trithemius and Paracelsus had developed a personal
[L22] association.4 To help buttress this widespread opinion a sizeable corpus of alchemical
[L23] writings, bearing such headings as Chemic'Us Nobilis, De lap ide Philosophico, Veterum
[L24] sophorum sigilla et imagines magicae, and the like, came to light under the abbot's name in
[L25] the first half of the seventeenth century.5 The Chemicus Nobilis, representing the sole
[L26] "Trithemius" selection in the Theatrum ChemicU111t,furnishes a typical expression of the state
[L27] of mind at the bottom of this spuriously attributed group of alchemical tracts:
[L28] He who desires to prolong to its conclusion anything arising from the great depths of
[L29] chemical knowledge must consider attentively the celestial bodies, and diligently
[L30] apprehend their qualities, natures, and positions. If he does so, he will discover, with
[L31] the help of divine grace, the insatiable intellect which lies within them.6
[L32] In this statement we.

„Äê7‚Ä†[The Catholic Historical Review 1983-oct vol. 69 iss. 4] Review by_ Gerald Strauss - The Abbot Trithemius (1462-1516). The Renaissance of Monastic Humanismby Noel L. Brann (1983) [10.2307_25021685] - libgen.li.pdf‚Ä†file_0000000033107230845f888b6d9248ae„Äë

Title: [The Catholic Historical Review 1983-oct vol. 69 iss. 4] Review by_ Gerald Strauss - The Abbot Trithemius (1462-1516). The Renaissance of Monastic Humanismby Noel L. Brann (1983) [10.2307_25021685] - libgen.li.pdf
Citation Marker: ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn2file7ÓàÅ
Mclick Target: "2:7"
Content Snippet:
[L1] Speaking for himself, and speaking of his notable
[L2] predecessors in the history of the Church, he struggled to resolve the conflicts
[L3] between faith and understanding, piety and learning, Christian values and pagan
[L4] literature, meaning and eloquence. He managed to sustain them in what Brann calls
[L5] a "tense equilibrium." In 1506 he carried his literary activities to the monastery of
[L6] St. Jacob in W?rzburg, touching bases with the many interests of his fellow
[L7] humanists in writing patriotic German history, local chronicles, an autobiography,
[L8] and exercises in the occult. By the time of his death in 1516 he had gained a sizable
[L9] group of followers who strove to guard the values of monastic humanism from
[L10] virulent attacks under which monasticism was coming on the eve of the
[L11] Reformation.
[L12] Noel Brann writes about all this with the warmest sympathy for his hero, but
[L13] unfortunately (from my point of view) also without any detachment. As a result of
[L14] this lack of distance between author and subject there is not conveyed to us much of
[L15] a critical understanding of Trithemius as a phenomenon in the northern
[L16] Renaissance. Most of the abbot's positions on matters of current intellectual
[L17] interest were entirely conventional: the common coin of literary men of the time,
[L18] often mere topoi. But they are presented here solely in the context of Trithemius'
[L19] life, as though they had been uniquely his own inventions. Unlike Arnold's book,
[L20] which frequently moves outward from the abbot's life to observe it in a wider frame
[L21] of reference, Brann's sees everything through Trithemius' eyes. In its detail and its
[L22] use of long quotations, it is excessively pedantic, even pausing to explain at one
[L23] point that the abbot's famous dog, which was trained to respond to commands in
[L24] Greek, didn't really understand Greek. Trithemius' transparently spurious
[L25] fabrication of historical sources for use in his history of the ancient Franks receives
[L26] only the barest mention, and there is no discussion at all of his occult interests and
[L27] his reputation as a magician and a prototype of Dr. Faust. Above all, Brann subjects
[L28] his reader to endless reiteration of Trithemius' problem of reconciling piety with
[L29] erudition and rhetoric. No doubt Trithemius did agonize over this. But after turning
[L30] the pages for the umpteenth time on this familiar theme one is tempted to dismiss
[L31] the plaint as a pose.
[L32] Trithemius led an interesting life, and his writings project a complex and
[L33] appealing personality. There is much to be learned from him and, through him,
[L34] about his age. But he was not a saint. His biography should not be treated as
[L35] hagiography.
[L36] Gerald Strauss
[L37] Indiana University, Bloomington

Content Snippet:
[L1] PDF files are considered safe for static
[L2] file formats . The advantage of a document created in PDF format is that it can not be changed directly
[L3] by the recipient of a document unlike a document file with the format .doc, .txt or the other [4].
[L4] The purpose of this study are using Trithemius Algorithm and Double Transposition Cipher in super
[L5] encryption scheme to avoid theft of files / data, calculates the super encryption complexity in Big Theta
[L6] (Big-Œ∏), and measure Real Running Time super encryption in millisecond (ms).
[L7] 2. Method
[L8] On the encryption process, Trithemius algorithm will be used first. In the encryption process, the next
[L9] letter on the plaintext will be replaced by the relevant letter of the next row in the table. After using the
[L10] last line, one should move back to the first line. This means that all letters on the plaintext change
[L11] according to the number of positions specified by the actual row. Therefore the first letter is encrypted
[L12] without a shift, the second letter with the shift is determined by the second row (so by one position), the
[L13] third letter with the shift determined by the third row (so by two positions) and so on. Or, can also be
[L14] calculated by the formula:
[L15] ùê∂ ‚â° ùëÉ + ùêæ(ùëöùëúùëë ùëõ) (1)
[L16] ùëÉ ‚â° ùê∂ ‚àí ùêæ(ùëöùëúùëë ùëõ) (2)
[L17] Where :
[L18] C: Ciphertext
[L19] P: Plaintext
[L20] K: Key
[L21] N: number of letters used
[L22] This cipher consists of two rounds of transition columns, using 2 different keys. After the key in the
[L23] first round is determined, then each letter in the key is numbered in alphabetical order, and the plaintext
[L24] is read per column based on the sequence of numbers. Where, the direction of the sequence should be
[L25] determined first whether left-to-right, or right-to-left, to facilitate when there is the same letter on the
[L26] key.
[L27] Ciphertext generated from the first round, encrypted again in the second round in the same way, but
[L28] using a different key. Ciphertext generated from the second round, then divided into blocks. Where,
[L29] each block consists of 5 letters to facilitate the transmission process. One of the requirements of
[L30] encryption process using this cipher is, the table we use must be fully loaded. In order for the table used
[L31] to be fully charged, padding character is used.
[L32] While in the decryption process by using Double Transposition Cipher, start by using the second key
[L33] first. Where, the number of rows in the table is determined by the division of ciphertext length by key.
[L34] (Salomon, 2005). Then, the ciphertext is inserted into the table based on the number of fragments in
[L35] alphabetical order in the key used.

[L38] is tested with Personal Computer with processor intel core i7 ‚Äì 3632QM CPU @ 2.20 GHz, Memory 4
[L39] GB RAM.
