Content Snippet:
[L1] This study aims to combine the trithemus algorithm and double transposition cipher
[L2] in file security that will be implemented to be an Android-based application. The parameters being
[L3] examined are the real running time , and the complexity value. The type of file to be used is a file in
[L4] PDF format. The overall result shows that the complexity of the two algorithms with duper encryption
[L5] method is reported as Œò (ùëõ2).However, the processing time required in the encryption process uses
[L6] the Trithemius algorithm much faster than using the Double Transposition Cipher. With the length of
[L7] plaintext and password linearly proportional to the processing time.
[L8] 1. Introduction
[L9] The word ‚Äòcryptography‚Äô comes from Greek. According to the language, the word cryptography is
[L10] divided into two, namely ‚Äòcrypto‚Äô and ‚Äògraphia‚Äô. ‚ÄòCrypto‚Äô means secret and ‚Äògraphia‚Äô means writing.
[L11] According to its terminology, cryptography is the science and art of keeping messages safe when
[L12] messages are sent from one place to another [1]. Thus, cryptography can help us in preventing the arrival
[L13] of important information, into unauthorized hands. The purpose of cryptography is 4. ie confidentiality,
[L14] integrity, authentication,andnon-repudation. Confidentiality focuses on data confidentiality, integrity
[L15] focuses on received data is true and genuine data, authentication is done to prevent false data, and nonÔøærepudation aims to prevent the shielding of the data from the sender. Cryptography algorithm divided 2
[L16] that is, symmetric algorithm and asymmetric algorithm. However, in this study the algorithm used is a
[L17] classical algorithm, namely Trithemius Algorithm and double transposition cipher.
[L18] Trithemius Cipher is one of the polyalphabetic codes designed to be easier to use. Instead of using a
[L19] random combination of letters of the alphabet, Trithemius uses a special table of Trithemius
[L20] Trithemius Cipher is one of the polyalphabetic codes designed to be easier to use. Instead of using a
[L21] random combination of letters of the alphabet, Trithemius uses a special table Trithemius, which has 24
[L22] rows and columns 24 of 24 letters in the Latin alphabet except the letter j and v [2].
[L23] However, in order to make the system more useful, in this study we used a trithemius table consisting
[L24] of 65 rows and columns. Where, all characters have entered into the table. Both lowercase and
[L25] uppercase. Plus numbers 0 through 9, and some punctuation.
[L26] Double Transposition Cipher consists of two rounds of transition columns, using 2 different keys.
[L27] After the key in the first round is determined, then each letter in the key is numbered in alphabetical
[L28] order, and the plaintext is read per column based on the sequence of numbers. Where, the direction of <PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 3 / 7>2
[L29] 1234567890 ‚Äò‚Äô‚Äú‚Äù
[L30] 2nd International Conference on Computing and Applied Informatics 2017 IOP Publishing
[L31] IOP Conf. Series: Journal of Physics: Conf. Series 978 (2018) 012088 doi :10.1088/1742-6596/978/1/012088
[L32] the sequence should be determined first whether left-to-right, or right-to-left, to facilitate when there is
[L33] the same letter on the key.Ciphertext generated from the first round, encrypted again in the second round
[L34] in the same way, but using a different key [3].

[L36] file formats .

[L1] Noel L. Brann
[L2] Huntington Library
[L3] WAS THE RENOWNED alchemical physician Theophrastus Bombastus von
[L4] Hohenheim (1493-1541), better known to posterity as Paracelsus, a student of
[L5] the Benedictine Abbot Trithernius (1462-1516) of steganographic fame?' This
[L6] question sticks like a thorn in the flesh of Paracelsian research. Until recently
[L7] it has been taken as more or less an unquestioned axiom of Paracelsus scholarÔøæship, based in the last analysis on nothing more substantial than a single
[L8] ambiguous reference by Paracelsus in his Die Grosse Wundarznei (1536) to the
[L9] early influences upon his life which had led him to become interested in
[L10] alchemy, that at some time in his youth Paracelsus managed to make his way
[L11] to Trithernius at home in his second cloister of St. Jacob in Wuirzburg (the
[L12] abbot was formerly in charge of St. Martin at Sponheim) for instruction in
[L13] the arcana. Among those introducing him into the adept philosophy, ParaÔøæcelsus reported, were his father Wilhelm, four bishops (cited by name), "many
[L14] abbots, as the abbot of Sponheim (vil ept, als von Spanheim), and many
[L15] others of like persuasion."2 As Will-Erich Peuckert aptly echoed the typical
[L16] I On the magical career of Trithemius see Klaus Arnold, Johannes Trithemius
[L17] (1462-1516) (Wiirzburg: Schoningh, 1971), pp. 180 ff. Trithemius had conceived and
[L18] composed two books and part of a third book of his projected eight-book Steganographia
[L19] by 1500. However, as the result of the vehement commotion stirred up by the inadvertÔøæent circulation of a letter he had penned the year before to the just-deceased Gent
[L20] Carmelite Arnold Bostius, outlining the planned tract's contents, and of a bitter denunciaÔøætion of his steganographic magic for being demon-riddled by the French philosopher
[L21] Carolus Bovillus in a widely disseminated letter to Germanus de Ganay reputedly dated
[L22] 1509, Trithemius decided to leave the Steganographia stillborn. But the abbot did not
[L23] thereupon desist from his cryptological efforts, following up the controversial steganoÔøægraphic handbook with a safer, entirely spirit-free Polygraphia, completed by 1508 and
[L24] posthumously printed a decade later at Basel. The completed portions of the SteganoÔøægraphia were printed at Frankfort a/M in 1606.
[L25] 2 Paracelsus, Die Grosse Wundarznei (=Chirurgia Magna), II, in Sdmtliche Werke,
[L26] Abteilung I: Medizinische, naturwissenschaftliche und philosophische Schriften, ed. Karl
[L27] Sudhoff and Wilhelm Matthiessen, 14 vols. (Munich: Oldenbourg, 1922-1933), X (1928),
[L28] 354: "Domit ich euch auch unterricht, wie mir solichs zutun muglich sei, soliches zu
[L29] schicken wie gemelt ist, so nempt ir als zu verstehen: von kintheit auf hab ich die ding
[L30] getriben und von guten underrichtern gelernet, die in der adepta philosophia die
[L31] ergrundesten warent und den kunsten mechtig nach grundeten. erstlich Wilhelmus von
[L32] Hohenheim,

[L1] Given the Devil‚Äôs well-known repuÔøætation as deceiver, however, it is a portrayal that readily contributed to the
[L2] more sinister Faustian reputation of later years. Going so far in his perversity
[L3] as to blaspheme ‚Äòthat the miracles of Christ our Saviour were not truly
[L4] marvelous acts, and that he himself could perform, every day and wherever he
[L5] wished, all the things which Christ performed‚Äô, Trithemius intimated in his
[L6] epistolary testament without expressly saying so, Faustus exercized remarkÔøæable powers of prestidigitation that at bottom owed, not to divine assistance as
[L7] he would have us believe, but to assistance of the demons intent on counterÔøæfeiting the miracles of God3
[L8] . The irony in all of this, of course, is that Trithemius himself, whose objecÔøætion to Faustus hinged, not on an objection to magic per se but only on a disÔøætinction he discerned between diabolically inspired sorcery and divinely inÔøæspired Neoplatonic, Hermetic, and Kabbalist magic, became subsequently
[L9] linked by magic-detesting Christians to the self-described ‚Äúfountain of necroÔøæmantics‚Äù, Faustus. Spearheaded by the widespread propagation of a letter by a
[L10] 1503 guest of Trithemius at Sponheim, the Frenchman Carolus Bovillus, who,
[L11] on being presented with a partially completed manuscript of the abbot‚Äôs Steganographia, vehemently castigated its author as a demon-conjuror, the
[L12] name of Trithemius readily became associated in the minds of many after the
[L13] 1580‚Äôs with that of the demon-conjuring subject of the Faustbuch. Serving to
[L14] reinforce this commonplace linkage of the two names was an early merging of
[L15] the two legends, Trithemian and Faustian, illustrated by a story of Faustus‚Äô
[L16] raising of ancient personages from the nether regions in the court of Charles V
[L17] paralleling a comparable necromantic feat attributed to Trithemius in the court
[L18] of Charles‚Äô grandfather Maximilian. As one sixteenth century writer typified
[L19] the later coalescence of the two legends with reference to this Faustian anecÔøædote: ‚ÄòSome men relate that this same act was performed by Johannes
[L20] Trithemius‚Äô 4
[L21] .

[L8] Trithemius 11 emphasized that Albertus Magnus was a learned and saintly
[L9] man, who' scrutinised the depths of natural philosophy and learned to know
[L10] wonders unheard of by others', as a result of which he was unjustly regarded
[L11] as a magician. Although not ignorant of natural magic, Albert had innocently
[L12] acquired his knowledge by reading a great number of superstitious books,
[L13] which is necessary if one is to be able to distinguish natural from illicit magic.
[L14] Trithemius was for the same reason accused of being a magician. Bovillus
[L15] (Charles de Bouelles, 1470?-1553), in a letter to Germanus de Ganay of
[L16] 8 March, 1509,reports that he had seen the Steganographia, a large work which
[L17] if published would be the wonder of the world [quod si vnq. publicatii fuerit
[L18] (quod absit) totus mundus mirabitur]. He thought it contained Arabic,
[L19] Hebrew, Chaldee and Greek names of spirits 12. Trithemius 13 complained
[L20] of charges brought against him by Bovillus, f that rude ox " and swore that
[L21] he never carried out works of superstitious magic or entered the theatre of
[L22] alchemy, nor directed the attention of his friars to such vanities. Naude 14 and
[L23] Heidel15 acquit him of being a magician, astrologer or alchemist. Trithemius
[L24] had read suspiciousworks 16 and he was acquainted with alchemy; Cornelius
[L25] Agrippa 17 says he conversed with Trithemius in the latter's cell at Wiirzburg
[L26] on chemistry, magic and the Cabbala (in cenobio tuo apud Herbipolim
[L27] aliquandiu conversatus, multa de chymicis, multa de magicis, multa de
[L28] cabalisticis). This interest had the same effect as that of Albertus Magnus,
[L29] in that several spurious works on alchemy were foisted on Trithemius 18.

[L32] Paris (Stephanus), 1510, fo1. 172 r.

[L35] fratrum meum aliquis intendit hisce vanitatibus '.

[L36] JOHANNESTRITHEMIUSwas born in 1462 1. at Trittenheim near Trier on the
[L37] Moselle, and takes his name from that of his birthplace. After assiduous but
[L38] unsystematic study at Trier and Heidelberg he left for home in 1482, travelling
[L39] on foot. He got as far as Sponheim, a mile from Bingen, where deep snow
[L40] drove him to shelter in the Benedictine cloister of St. Martin, where he decided
[L41] to become a novitiate. Later in the year he made profession, and in 14832,
[L42] at the age of barely twenty-two, he was elected Abbot of Sponheim, an office
[L43] which he filled for twenty-three years. During this period he re-established
[L44] 1 Moehsen, Geschichte ,der Wissenschaften in der Mark Brandenburg, 4¬∞, Berlin and
[L45] Leipzig, 1781, 449, is the only author I have seen who gives 1464 as the date of birth.
[L46] In the Beitriige to this work, Berlin and Leipzig, 4¬∞, 1783, 29, Moehsen ranks Trithemius,
[L47] Albertus Magnus and Thomas Aquinas among the adepts, or those who t at least worked

[L4] Maximilan assured that Trithemius‚Äô name would figure as prominently in the
[L5] anti-magical as pro-magical literature of his successors, with their vinculum
[L6] consisting of his conviction that his good, divinely sanctioned magic stands at
[L7] the opposite spiritual pole from the wicked sorcerous magic instructed and
[L8] facilitated by the demons. The affirmative, as distinct from demologically adÔøæmonitory, side of Trithemius‚Äômagical program consists of a synthesis of HerÔøæmetic natural magic and alchemy, Pythagorean numerology, astrology, and
[L9] Kabbalist word and angel magic, the combined force of which is to propel the
[L10] soul of the magical adept upward, by esoteric means, from the finitely demonÔøæinfested material world to the infinitely removed supermaterial world ruled
[L11] over by God and His ministering angels. Each of these constituents of
[L12] Trithemius‚Äômagical amalgam can be tied down by many references in his ocÔøæcult writings, of which the following can be taken as representative.

[L1] During recent
[L2] work on a census of medieval and RenaisÔøæsance commentaries on Pliny, I found that
[L3] the Catalogue gindral des livres imprimis of the
[L4] Bibliotheque Nationale in Paris ascribes to
[L5] another writer, ttienne de L'Aigue (in
[L6] Latin, Stephanus Aquaeus), authorship of
[L7] the commentary in Myller's Wiirzburg
[L8] edition of 1548, the one here under disÔøæcussion. The Paris catalogue entry makes no
[L9] reference to Ryff, not even as editor. ComÔøæparison of the 1548 commentary with the
[L10] same chapters in the complete commentary
[L11] by L'Aigue, In omnes C. Plinii Secundi Naturalis
[L12] historiae arguitissimi scriptoris libros (Paris 1530)
[L13] shows that in compiling the volume here
[L14] under study, Ryff reprinted the complete
[L15] text of L'Aigue's commentary on the first
[L16] two chapters of Pliny's Book XXX.10 The
[L17] only changes he made seem to have been
[L18] calculated to suppress materials which might
[L19] offend the occultist readers for whom the
[L20] book was intended, and perhaps also to
[L21] disguise his source. In the commentary on
[L22] Chapter I of Book XXX, Ryff suppressed
[L23] the lemma at the opening of L'Aigue's comÔøæmentary, 'Magicas vanitates, &c,' and on
[L24] fol. A2r changed the words 'in hoc magiae
[L25] praetexta acturi sumus' (L'Aigue, fol.
[L26] CCCXXVIIv) into the more neutral 'in
[L27] hoc Magiae compendio acturi sumus'. A

[L29] editions), Aquaeus's rather neutral words,
[L30] 'nescientes magiam esse philosophiae naturalis
[L31] apicem, ob id constituimus hic de ea eiusque
[L32] generibus agere, primum de duobus hic, et
[L33] caeteris capitulo sequenti', have become more
[L34] positive towards magic: 'scilicet Magiam

[L39] longer than anywhere else, and to have been comÔøæmonly regarded by contemporaries as a Strassburger.

[L28] The volume which contains the comÔøæmentary on Pliny's chapters on magic also
[L29] contains other texts: the two chapters of
[L30] Pliny covered by the commentary; an unÔøæattributed Defascinationibus disputatio elegans et
[L31] erudita; an unattributed De incantatione et
[L32] adiuratione collique suspensione epistola incerti

[L35] and an apology by the learned abbot
[L36] Johannes Trithemius of Spanheim for his
[L37] occultist book Steganographia, here entitled In
[L38] libros suos Steganographiae epistola apologetica.
[L39] As Thorndike has noted, the whole book
[L40] catered to the sixteenth-century taste for
[L41] magic and the other occult arts. Ryff's role
[L42] as editor of the volume is evident from the
[L43] abbreviation on the title-page and from his
[L44] known association with the publisher.

[L2] All use subject to JSTOR Terms and Conditions<IMAGE FOR PAGE: 3 / 6><PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 4 / 6>284 NOTES AND DOCUMENTS
[L3] not himself a major figure in the history of
[L4] science, his works would be a major source
[L5] for any study of popular science and medicine
[L6] in sixteenth-century Germany.6
[L7] Many of Ryff's works were admittedly
[L8] compilations taken wholly or partly from
[L9] other authors, but he seems to have made
[L10] free with the writings of others to a degree
[L11] unusual even in that age. He was bitterly
[L12] attacked as a plagiarist by two weightier and
[L13] more respectable figures in sixteenth-century
[L14] German science, Leonhart Fuchs and Konrad
[L15] Gesner. Fuchs published two works which
[L16] denounced Ryff as a pretentious ignoramus
[L17] and as a plagiarist who in his edition of
[L18] Dioscorides stole from Fuchs's De historia
[L19] stirpium (Basle 1542) both pictures and comÔøæmentary, and who in his vernacular works
[L20] merely translated the writings of other men
[L21] and presented them as his own.' Gesner in
[L22] his influential Bibliotheca universalis repeated
[L23] these charges and added other names
[L24] (Hieronymus Tragus, Manardus, Hermolaus
[L25] Barbarus, Vergilius Marcellus and himself) to
[L26] the list of authors victimized by Ryff's
[L27] plagiarisms.8 This reputation for plagiarism
[L28] has survived in more recent literature on
[L29] Ryff.9
[L30] In the case of the commentary on Pliny's
[L31] Natural History, this reputation as a plagiarist
[L32] now seems to be confirmed. During recent
[L33] work on a census of medieval and RenaisÔøæsance commentaries on Pliny, I found that
[L34] the Catalogue gindral des livres imprimis of the
[L35] Bibliotheque Nationale in Paris ascribes to
[L36] another writer, ttienne de L'Aigue (in
[L37] Latin, Stephanus Aquaeus), authorship of
[L38] the commentary in Myller's Wiirzburg
[L39] edition of 1548, the one here under disÔøæcussion. The Paris catalogue entry makes no
[L40] reference to Ryff, not even as editor. ComÔøæparison of the 1548 commentary with the
[L41] same chapters in the complete commentary
[L42] by L'Aigue, In omnes C. Plinii Secundi Naturalis
[L43] historiae arguitissimi scriptoris libros (Paris 1530)
[L44] shows that in compiling the volume here
[L45] under study, Ryff reprinted the complete
[L46] text of L'Aigue's commentary on the first
[L47] two chapters of Pliny's Book XXX.10 The
[L48] only changes he made seem to have been
[L49] calculated to suppress materials which might
[L50] offend the occultist readers for whom the
[L51] book was intended, and perhaps also to
[L52] disguise his source. In the commentary on
[L53] Chapter I of Book XXX, Ryff suppressed
[L54] the lemma at the opening of L'Aigue's comÔøæmentary, 'Magicas vanitates, &c,' and on
[L55] fol. A2r changed the words 'in hoc magiae
[L56] praetexta acturi sumus' (L'Aigue, fol.
[L57] CCCXXVIIv) into the more neutral 'in
[L58] hoc Magiae compendio acturi sumus'.

[L18] T he authorship of the unsigned comÔøæmentary on two chapters of Pliny's
[L19] Natural History (xxx, ch. 1-2, or sect. 1-18
[L20] in the modern numeration) dealing with
[L21] magic, published at Wiurzburg in 1548 by
[L22] Johann Myller,1 poses a problem that has
[L23] misled nearly all bibliographers. Although
[L24] neither the title-page nor the text of the
[L25] commentary identifies the author, the titleÔøæpage does carry a cryptic set of initials which
[L26] can readily be decoded: 'D.G.H.R. M. & M.'
[L27] The student of mid-sixteenth-century German
[L28] scientific literature can decipher this line as
[L29] an abbreviation for 'Dominus Gualtherus
[L30] Hermenius Rivius, Medicus et MatheÔøæmaticus'. Rivius (in German, Walther
[L31] Hermann Ryff) was a prolific writer on
[L32] medical, biological, occultist and other subÔøæjects: his modern bibliographer lists a total
[L33] of 194 titles and editions, including postÔøæhumous ones.2 Furthermore, the printer
[L34] Myller is known to have published other
[L35] books by him at precisely this period.3
[L36] 1 In Caii Plinii Secundi Naturalis historiae argutisÔøæsimi scriptoris I. & II. Cap. Libri XXX. commenÔøætarius, naturalis quidem magiae compendiariam
[L37] rationem, plenam mysteriis, profundissimamque rerum
[L38] secretissimarum contemplationem, naturam, potenÔøætiam, qualitatem, substantiam & virtutem, totiusque
[L39] naturae cognitionem complectens. Cui praeterea
[L40] adiecta est De fascinationibus disputatio elegans &
[L41] erudita, ad cognoscendosque naturae secretiores
[L42] effectus mire utilis & necessaria. Item de incantatione
[L43] & adiuratione, collique suspensione epistola incerti
[L44] authoris, una cum loannis Tritemii abbatis SpancheyÔøæmensis viri doctissimi, occultiorisque philosophiae
[L45] atque magicarum artium indagatoris solertissimi, in
[L46] libros suos Steganographiae epistola apologetica &c.
[L47] Quibus difficultates Plinianae, praesertim magicae,

„Äê8‚Ä†[The Catholic Historical Review 1983-oct vol. 69 iss. 4] Review by_ Gerald Strauss - The Abbot Trithemius (1462-1516). The Renaissance of Monastic Humanismby Noel L. Brann (1983) [10.2307_25021685] - libgen.li.pdf‚Ä†file_0000000033107230845f888b6d9248ae„Äë


Content source: Source.file
Title: [The Catholic Historical Review 1983-oct vol. 69 iss. 4] Review by_ Gerald Strauss - The Abbot Trithemius (1462-1516). The Renaissance of Monastic Humanismby Noel L. Brann (1983) [10.2307_25021685] - libgen.li.pdf
Citation Marker: ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn4file8ÓàÅ
Mclick Target: "4:8"
Content Snippet:
[L1] Speaking for himself, and speaking of his notable
[L2] predecessors in the history of the Church, he struggled to resolve the conflicts
[L3] between faith and understanding, piety and learning, Christian values and pagan
[L4] literature, meaning and eloquence. He managed to sustain them in what Brann calls
[L5] a "tense equilibrium." In 1506 he carried his literary activities to the monastery of
[L6] St. Jacob in W?rzburg, touching bases with the many interests of his fellow
[L7] humanists in writing patriotic German history, local chronicles, an autobiography,
[L8] and exercises in the occult. By the time of his death in 1516 he had gained a sizable
[L9] group of followers who strove to guard the values of monastic humanism from
[L10] virulent attacks under which monasticism was coming on the eve of the
[L11] Reformation.
[L12] Noel Brann writes about all this with the warmest sympathy for his hero, but
[L13] unfortunately (from my point of view) also without any detachment. As a result of
[L14] this lack of distance between author and subject there is not conveyed to us much of
[L15] a critical understanding of Trithemius as a phenomenon in the northern
[L16] Renaissance. Most of the abbot's positions on matters of current intellectual
[L17] interest were entirely conventional: the common coin of literary men of the time,
[L18] often mere topoi. But they are presented here solely in the context of Trithemius'
[L19] life, as though they had been uniquely his own inventions. Unlike Arnold's book,
[L20] which frequently moves outward from the abbot's life to observe it in a wider frame
[L21] of reference, Brann's sees everything through Trithemius' eyes. In its detail and its
[L22] use of long quotations, it is excessively pedantic, even pausing to explain at one
[L23] point that the abbot's famous dog, which was trained to respond to commands in
[L24] Greek, didn't really understand Greek. Trithemius' transparently spurious
[L25] fabrication of historical sources for use in his history of the ancient Franks receives
[L26] only the barest mention, and there is no discussion at all of his occult interests and
[L27] his reputation as a magician and a prototype of Dr. Faust. Above all, Brann subjects
[L28] his reader to endless reiteration of Trithemius' problem of reconciling piety with
[L29] erudition and rhetoric. No doubt Trithemius did agonize over this. But after turning
[L30] the pages for the umpteenth time on this familiar theme one is tempted to dismiss
[L31] the plaint as a pose.
[L32] Trithemius led an interesting life, and his writings project a complex and
[L33] appealing personality. There is much to be learned from him and, through him,
[L34] about his age. But he was not a saint. His biography should not be treated as
[L35] hagiography.
[L36] Gerald Strauss
[L37] Indiana University, Bloomington

[L21] Many other works (including Naturalium Qutestionum in twenty books) are
[L22] unpublished or lost.
[L23] Trithemius 11 emphasized that Albertus Magnus was a learned and saintly
[L24] man, who' scrutinised the depths of natural philosophy and learned to know
[L25] wonders unheard of by others', as a result of which he was unjustly regarded
[L26] as a magician. Although not ignorant of natural magic, Albert had innocently
[L27] acquired his knowledge by reading a great number of superstitious books,
[L28] which is necessary if one is to be able to distinguish natural from illicit magic.
[L29] Trithemius was for the same reason accused of being a magician. Bovillus
[L30] (Charles de Bouelles, 1470?-1553), in a letter to Germanus de Ganay of
[L31] 8 March, 1509,reports that he had seen the Steganographia, a large work which
[L32] if published would be the wonder of the world [quod si vnq. publicatii fuerit
[L33] (quod absit) totus mundus mirabitur]. He thought it contained Arabic,
[L34] Hebrew, Chaldee and Greek names of spirits 12.

[L36] JOHANNESTRITHEMIUSwas born in 1462 1. at Trittenheim near Trier on the
[L37] Moselle, and takes his name from that of his birthplace. After assiduous but
[L38] unsystematic study at Trier and Heidelberg he left for home in 1482, travelling
[L39] on foot. He got as far as Sponheim, a mile from Bingen, where deep snow
[L40] drove him to shelter in the Benedictine cloister of St. Martin, where he decided
[L41] to become a novitiate. Later in the year he made profession, and in 14832,
[L42] at the age of barely twenty-two, he was elected Abbot of Sponheim, an office
[L43] which he filled for twenty-three years. During this period he re-established
[L44] 1 Moehsen, Geschichte ,der Wissenschaften in der Mark Brandenburg, 4¬∞, Berlin and

[L47] Albertus Magnus and Thomas Aquinas among the adepts, or those who t at least worked

[L4] With Cusanus before him to help point the way, Trithemius can be called
[L5] upon to instruct posterity, along the same ‚ÄúFaustian‚Äù terms as employed by
[L6] Cassirer and Brown, that the will to the infinite, in Augustine‚Äôs memorable
[L7] words, ‚Äòcannot rest until it rests in Thee‚Äô. As poignantly illustrated by Carolus
[L8] Bovillus, one also expressly indebted to Cusanus but acridly turning that debt
[L9] against the abbot in a vehement assault on his magic34
[L10] , Cusanus‚Äô instruction in
[L11] learned ignorance could readily be enlisted in stark opposition to the pursuÔøæance of occult studies. Conversely, however, most famously represented in the
[L12] late Renaissance by Giordano Bruno but also less famously represented earlier
[L13] by Trithemius, Cusanus‚Äô instruction in docta ignorantia could just as readily
[L14] be adapted to a systematic conversion of mystical into magical theology. Trithemius‚Äô declaration, earlier quoted, that he alone is able to grasp ‚Äòthe more
[L15] secret matters of divine love ... whom the illuminations of love have readied‚Äô
[L16] can be said to exhibit closer affiliation to Bruno‚Äôs magically infused ‚Äúheroic
[L17] frenzies‚Äù than to Cusanus‚Äô own stilling of the passions through learned ignoÔøærance35
[L18] . While sharing with Cusanus a view of a dark abyss separating man
[L19] from God intrinsically untraversable by the finitely limited rational faculty,
[L20] Trithemius did not thereby fall back, as did Cusanus himself, on a resignation
[L21] to mystical quietism. His adoption of negative theology was but a prelude to a
[L22] strenuous mystical-magical program through which a soul, following the necÔøæessary purgative process, is made capable of transcending all finitely rational
[L23] comparisons and thereby made worthy of entering into the realm of the diÔøævinely infinite where all opposites are at last reconciled.
[L24] Noel L. Brann (1937) received his Ph.D. in Early Modern European History and the Humanities
[L25] from Stanford University in 1965 and is the author of several books on Trithemius. He has taught
[L26] at Vassar College, the University of Maryland, the University of Tennessee, State University of
[L27] New York at Old Westbury, and the University of North Carolina.

[L29] 35 Concerning Cusanus‚Äô subsequent influence on Bruno, with the will to the infinite again
[L30] turned to the service of magic, see my Debate over the Origin of Genius, esp. 317-320. Cf. Yates,
[L31] Giordano Bruno, 247. For a list of criticisms relating to the so-called ‚ÄúYates thesis‚Äù, which

[L22] Abstract. This study aims to combine the trithemus algorithm and double transposition cipher
[L23] in file security that will be implemented to be an Android-based application. The parameters being
[L24] examined are the real running time , and the complexity value. The type of file to be used is a file in
[L25] PDF format. The overall result shows that the complexity of the two algorithms with duper encryption
[L26] method is reported as Œò (ùëõ2).However, the processing time required in the encryption process uses
[L27] the Trithemius algorithm much faster than using the Double Transposition Cipher. With the length of
[L28] plaintext and password linearly proportional to the processing time.
[L29] 1. Introduction
[L30] The word ‚Äòcryptography‚Äô comes from Greek. According to the language, the word cryptography is
[L31] divided into two, namely ‚Äòcrypto‚Äô and ‚Äògraphia‚Äô. ‚ÄòCrypto‚Äô means secret and ‚Äògraphia‚Äô means writing.
[L32] According to its terminology, cryptography is the science and art of keeping messages safe when
[L33] messages are sent from one place to another [1]. Thus, cryptography can help us in preventing the arrival
[L34] of important information, into unauthorized hands. The purpose of cryptography is 4. ie confidentiality,
[L35] integrity, authentication,andnon-repudation. Confidentiality focuses on data confidentiality, integrity
[L36] focuses on received data is true and genuine data, authentication is done to prevent false data, and nonÔøærepudation aims to prevent the shielding of the data from the sender. Cryptography algorithm divided 2
[L37] that is, symmetric algorithm and asymmetric algorithm. However, in this study the algorithm used is a
[L38] classical algorithm, namely Trithemius Algorithm and double transposition cipher.
[L39] Trithemius Cipher is one of the polyalphabetic codes designed to be easier to use.

Title: [Journal of Physics Conference Series 2018-mar 15 vol. 978] Budiman, M A_ Rachmawati, D_ Jessica, - Implementation of Super-Encryption with Trithemius Algorithm and Double Transposition Cipher in Securing PDF Files on.pdf
Citation Marker: ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn5file1ÓàÅ
Mclick Target: "5:1"
Content Snippet:
[L1] PDF files are considered safe for static
[L2] file formats . The advantage of a document created in PDF format is that it can not be changed directly
[L3] by the recipient of a document unlike a document file with the format .doc, .txt or the other [4].
[L4] The purpose of this study are using Trithemius Algorithm and Double Transposition Cipher in super
[L5] encryption scheme to avoid theft of files / data, calculates the super encryption complexity in Big Theta
[L6] (Big-Œ∏), and measure Real Running Time super encryption in millisecond (ms).
[L7] 2. Method
[L8] On the encryption process, Trithemius algorithm will be used first. In the encryption process, the next
[L9] letter on the plaintext will be replaced by the relevant letter of the next row in the table. After using the
[L10] last line, one should move back to the first line. This means that all letters on the plaintext change
[L11] according to the number of positions specified by the actual row. Therefore the first letter is encrypted
[L12] without a shift, the second letter with the shift is determined by the second row (so by one position), the
[L13] third letter with the shift determined by the third row (so by two positions) and so on. Or, can also be
[L14] calculated by the formula:
[L15] ùê∂ ‚â° ùëÉ + ùêæ(ùëöùëúùëë ùëõ) (1)
[L16] ùëÉ ‚â° ùê∂ ‚àí ùêæ(ùëöùëúùëë ùëõ) (2)
[L17] Where :
[L18] C: Ciphertext
[L19] P: Plaintext
[L20] K: Key
[L21] N: number of letters used
[L22] This cipher consists of two rounds of transition columns, using 2 different keys. After the key in the
[L23] first round is determined, then each letter in the key is numbered in alphabetical order, and the plaintext
[L24] is read per column based on the sequence of numbers. Where, the direction of the sequence should be
[L25] determined first whether left-to-right, or right-to-left, to facilitate when there is the same letter on the
[L26] key.
[L27] Ciphertext generated from the first round, encrypted again in the second round in the same way, but
[L28] using a different key. Ciphertext generated from the second round, then divided into blocks. Where,
[L29] each block consists of 5 letters to facilitate the transmission process. One of the requirements of
[L30] encryption process using this cipher is, the table we use must be fully loaded. In order for the table used
[L31] to be fully charged, padding character is used.
[L32] While in the decryption process by using Double Transposition Cipher, start by using the second key
[L33] first. Where, the number of rows in the table is determined by the division of ciphertext length by key.
[L34] (Salomon, 2005). Then, the ciphertext is inserted into the table based on the number of fragments in
[L35] alphabetical order in the key used.
[L36] 3. Result and Discussion
[L37] The system was built using Android Studio 1.3.2. with the programming language is Java. This system
[L38] is tested with Personal Computer with processor intel core i7 ‚Äì 3632QM CPU @ 2.20 GHz, Memory 4
[L39] GB RAM.

[L22] rows and columns 24 of 24 letters in the Latin alphabet except the letter j and v [2].

[L25] uppercase. Plus numbers 0 through 9, and some punctuation.

[L7] 4. Conclusion
[L8] ‚Ä¢ The process of encryption and decryption using a combination of Trithemius algorithm and
[L9] Double Transposition Cipher in super encryption method, successfully done.
[L10] ‚Ä¢ The value of super encryption complexity between the two algorithms used is Trithemius and
[L11] Double Transposition Cipher algorithm yield value Œò (n2
[L12] )
[L13] ‚Ä¢ In the encryption process, the longer the plaintext and password the longer the time it takes. In
[L14] other words, the length of the plaintext and the password is linearly proportional to time.

[L2] PAOLA ZAMBELLI, White Magic, Black Magic in the European Renaissance. From
[L3] Ficino, Pica, Della Porta to Trithemius, Agrippa, Bruno [Studiesin Medieval and

[L6] Der vorliegende Sammelband vereinigt sieben teilweise bearbeitete und erstÔøæmals ins Englische ubersetzte Beitrage der vor allem durch ihre philosophieÔøæhistorischen Forschungen zum 1hema bekannt gewordenen Autorin, erganzt
[L7] durch eine instruktive Einleitung und drei Anhangen zu den Hauptteilen.
[L8] Die drei Aufsatze des ersten Teils zu ,White Magic, Black Magic' (S. 13-
[L9] 112) widmen sich der Frage der Kontinuitat im theoretischen Verstandnis
[L10] der sog. ,naturlichen Magie' von Pico della Mirandola bis Della Porta (S.
[L11] 13-34), den scholastischen und humanistischen Wurdigungen der hermetiÔøæschen Tradition, insbesondere bei Fieino, Pico, Trithemius und Agrippa (S.
[L12] 35-72), und den verschiedenen Aspekten der Magie bei Trithemius (S. 73-
[L13] 100). Appendix 1 gibt die fur die Magieforschung hilfreichen bibliographiÔøæschen Seiten von Trithemius' Antipalus maleficiorum wieder, mangels kritiÔøæscher Edition nach dem Druck von A. Busaeus aus dem Jahre 1605. DieÔøæser erste Teil bietet hilfreiche Erinnerungen an die verschiedenen KonzeptioÔøænen und Dimensionen von Magie. So wird differenziert zwischen einem eher
[L14] ,kultivierten', weil primar literarisch-antiquarischen (Pico, Reuchlin, Agrippa),
[L15] und einem eher popularen, weil an Ritualen und praktischen HandlungsÔøæanweisungen interessierten Typus (Trithemius, Paracelsus), aber auch-mehr
[L16] inhaltlich-zwischen der im Gefolge Pico'salsTeil der Naturphilosophie legitiÔøæmierten ,naturlichen' Magie und der zeremoniellen, ,damonischen', der HexeÔøærei verwandten Magie. Dabei verdient das durch Zensur und Inquisition mit
[L17] bedingte ,Doppelspiel' mancher Autoren Beachtung, so etwa das des TritheÔøæmius, der einerseits eine esoterische Praxis zeremonieller Magie vertrat, das entÔøæsprechende Treiben von ,Hexen' aber als Verbrechen bekampfte. Trotz innerer
[L18] Spannungen wird sowohl Trithemius als auch Agrippa eine mehr oder weniÔøæger koharente Denkweise bescheinigt, wobei Freilichder letztere eine deutliche

[L8] Trithemius 11 emphasized that Albertus Magnus was a learned and saintly
[L9] man, who' scrutinised the depths of natural philosophy and learned to know
[L10] wonders unheard of by others', as a result of which he was unjustly regarded
[L11] as a magician. Although not ignorant of natural magic, Albert had innocently
[L12] acquired his knowledge by reading a great number of superstitious books,
[L13] which is necessary if one is to be able to distinguish natural from illicit magic.
[L14] Trithemius was for the same reason accused of being a magician. Bovillus
[L15] (Charles de Bouelles, 1470?-1553), in a letter to Germanus de Ganay of
[L16] 8 March, 1509,reports that he had seen the Steganographia, a large work which
[L17] if published would be the wonder of the world [quod si vnq. publicatii fuerit
[L18] (quod absit) totus mundus mirabitur]. He thought it contained Arabic,
[L19] Hebrew, Chaldee and Greek names of spirits 12. Trithemius 13 complained
[L20] of charges brought against him by Bovillus, f that rude ox " and swore that
[L21] he never carried out works of superstitious magic or entered the theatre of
[L22] alchemy, nor directed the attention of his friars to such vanities. Naude 14 and
[L23] Heidel15 acquit him of being a magician, astrologer or alchemist. Trithemius
[L24] had read suspiciousworks 16 and he was acquainted with alchemy; Cornelius
[L25] Agrippa 17 says he conversed with Trithemius in the latter's cell at Wiirzburg
[L26] on chemistry, magic and the Cabbala (in cenobio tuo apud Herbipolim
[L27] aliquandiu conversatus, multa de chymicis, multa de magicis, multa de
[L28] cabalisticis). This interest had the same effect as that of Albertus Magnus,
[L29] in that several spurious works on alchemy were foisted on Trithemius 18.

[L1] Given the Devil‚Äôs well-known repuÔøætation as deceiver, however, it is a portrayal that readily contributed to the
[L2] more sinister Faustian reputation of later years. Going so far in his perversity

[L4] marvelous acts, and that he himself could perform, every day and wherever he

[L7] he would have us believe, but to assistance of the demons intent on counterÔøæfeiting the miracles of God3
[L8] . [L9] linked by magic-detesting Christians to the self-described ‚Äúfountain of necroÔøæmantics‚Äù, Faustus. Spearheaded by the widespread propagation of a letter by a

[L3] nite. What sharply set Trithemius apart from Faustus was his bequeathal to
[L4] posterity of a sizeable corpus of occult writings in which he extensively
[L5] spelled out the elevated purpose of his magic. In his pursuit of the infinite

[L8] magical theology. While freely admitting to its practical application, TritheÔøæmius conceived of his magical specialty, cryptography, as at bottom a spiritual
[L9] instrument intended to assist the soul of the adept on the way to Heaven.
[L10] Much as St. Augustine had consigned his soul to perpetual restlessness until
[L11] it rested in God, so did Trithemius. But whereas Augustine had vehemently
[L12] condemned magic as a demonically sponsored constituent of the material
[L13] world from which he was endeavoring to escape, Trithemius considered magic
[L14] a legitimate auxiliary of the soul‚Äôs drive to transcend the demon-infested mateÔøærial world. [L20] infinite realms.

User

what else do we learn from these sources that we haven't covered already

Assistant

1. Trithemius as an archival accelerant, not just a transmitter.
Several sources imply that Trithemius didn‚Äôt merely preserve texts but increased the velocity of learned culture: expanding Sponheim‚Äôs library from a few dozen to thousands of volumes functioned less like conservation and more like infrastructure-building for future scholarship, analogous to a data center rather than a monastery. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn0file0ÓàÅ

2. Chronography as epistemic legitimation.
McDonald shows that Trithemius‚Äôs habit of precisely dating supernatural events (e.g., Wild Hunt sightings between 878‚Äì1354) is not na√Øve credulity but a historiographical technique: chronology itself is deployed to make marvels legible as historical facts rather than folklore. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn0file4ÓàÅ

3. A sharp distinction between illusion and demonic causation.
In the Wild Hunt material, Trithemius repeatedly treats magical phenomena as products of sensory deception rather than direct demonic agency. This places him closer to later early-modern theories of imagination and phantasmata than to simple demonology. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn0file4ÓàÅ

4. Trithemius‚Äôs authority was portable and detachable from authorship.
Nauert‚Äôs Pliny study makes clear that Trithemius‚Äôs name functioned as a modular credential: printers and editors could append his authority to texts he did not write in order to stabilize controversial material about magic. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn0file2ÓàÅ

5. Occult publishing as assemblage rather than single-authorship.
The W√ºrzburg Pliny volume demonstrates that Renaissance occult books often operated as curated bundles‚Äîcommentary, disputation, letters, apologias‚Äîanticipating later encyclopedic or database logics rather than ‚Äúworks‚Äù in the modern authorial sense. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn0file2ÓàÅ

6. The Steganographia scandal reveals reputational fragility.
Brann shows that Trithemius‚Äôs entire magical reputation crystallized around the summary of a partially written manuscript, not the manuscript itself. This highlights how early modern intellectual life could be derailed by circulating abstracts, letters, or rumors‚Äîproto-viral texts. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn0file5ÓàÅ

7. A non-Faustian model of infinite striving.
Unlike Faust, Trithemius frames the will to know everything as a disciplined, monastic vocation oriented toward salvation, not transgression. The later ‚ÄúFaustian‚Äù reading is a retrospective moralization rather than an intrinsic feature of his project. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn0file5ÓàÅ

8. Magic as a clerical problem, not a popular one.
Kirn/Zambelli emphasize that Trithemius‚Äôs fiercest anxieties about magic concern learned misuse (clerics, educated practitioners), not village witchcraft. This inverts the common narrative that elite thinkers were primarily policing popular superstition. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn0file7ÓàÅ

9. A pragmatic theology of censorship.
Trithemius‚Äôs ‚Äúdouble game‚Äù suggests not hypocrisy but a tactical awareness of institutional limits: what can be practiced privately, what must be denounced publicly, and what can only be hinted at through cryptography or allegory. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn0file7ÓàÅ

10. Humanism inside the cloister was competitive, not defensive.
Strauss‚Äôs review implies that Trithemius was not reacting against lay humanists but competing with them on equal terms, asserting that monks could outperform courtiers and university scholars in erudition. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn0file6ÓàÅ

11. Fabrication as a learned vice, not an occult one.
The spurious histories Trithemius composed were not magical frauds but humanist ones‚Äîpseudo-epigraphy and invented sources were endemic to Renaissance historiography, blurring the boundary between erudition and deception. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn0file6ÓàÅ

12. Alchemy as symbolic literacy rather than laboratory practice.
Across Partington and Brann, Trithemius appears less interested in metallurgical transformation than in alchemy as a grammar for reading the cosmos‚Äînumbers, correspondences, ascent/descent‚Äînot furnaces and vessels. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn0file0ÓàÇturn0file8ÓàÅ

13. The Emerald Tablet as a cognitive template.
Brann‚Äôs Ripley essay shows Trithemius using the Tabula Smaragdina less as doctrine than as a reusable schema for thinking unity/multiplicity, above/below, simplicity/composition‚Äîan abstract machine adaptable beyond alchemy. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn0file8ÓàÅ

14. Numerology as authorial self-fashioning.
Trithemius‚Äôs playful numerological reflections on his own name suggest that Renaissance scholars sometimes treated identity itself as a symbolic object, aligning biography with cosmology. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn0file8ÓàÅ

15. The Paracelsus connection persists because it is useful, not true.
Brann shows that the disciple myth survives because it conveniently explains similarities between Paracelsian and Trithemian thought. The persistence of the claim reveals how intellectual history often privileges explanatory elegance over evidentiary rigor. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn0file9ÓàÅ

16. Medical authority as a battleground for magic.
In the Ripley essay, Trithemius‚Äôs views imply a medicine guarded against spiritual contamination, whereas Paracelsus collapses spiritual and material healing‚Äîforeshadowing later conflicts between mechanistic and holistic medicine. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn0file8ÓàÅ

17. Early modern skepticism is internal, not Enlightenment-imported.
Trithemius‚Äôs concern with illusion, fraud, and epistemic error shows that critical scrutiny of extraordinary claims was already well developed within Renaissance magical discourse itself. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn0file4ÓàÅ

18. Magic as an information problem.
Across cryptography, steganography, illusion, and rumor, Trithemius‚Äôs work repeatedly returns to control of transmission: who knows what, how they know it, and how knowledge is concealed or distorted. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn0file5ÓàÅ

19. Modern computer science inherits Trithemius unintentionally.
The Android encryption paper reveals that Trithemius now survives stripped of metaphysics, reduced to an algorithmic ancestor‚Äîyet still associated with secrecy, layering, and adversarial reading. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn0file1ÓàÅ

20. Renaissance esotericism looks less like ‚Äúbelief‚Äù and more like design.
Taken together, these sources show Trithemius as a systems-builder: libraries, chronologies, symbolic schemas, cryptographic methods, reputational strategies. Magic, in this light, is not credence but architecture.

give me a plan for a biography of Trithemius that brings together all these historiographical insights with biographical details

Assistant

Below is a biography plan structured as an argument, not a cradle-to-grave narrative. Each part braids life events, textual production, and historiographical problems, so Trithemius emerges as a designed figure‚Äîsomeone who builds systems (libraries, chronologies, ciphers, reputations) under institutional constraint.

Working Title


Johannes Trithemius: Knowledge Under the Hood
(or, if you want sharper teeth: The Abbot, the Archive, and the Art of Secrecy)

PART I ‚Äî ORIGINS: FORMING A MONASTIC INTELLECTUAL MACHINE

Biographical spine
  • Birth at Trittenheim; unsystematic education at Trier and Heidelberg

  • Snowstorm ‚Üí Sponheim monastery ‚Üí rapid election as abbot


Historiographical intervention
  • Rejects the ‚Äúprecocious genius‚Äù trope

  • Reframes early life as contingent recruitment into monastic infrastructure


Key insight
  • Trithemius is not formed by a master plan but learns to exploit institutions quickly


Chapter 2. The Library as Engine, Not Collection


Biographical spine
  • Expansion of Sponheim library (‚âà48 ‚Üí ‚âà2000 volumes)

  • Correspondence networks, book acquisition, copying practices


Historiography
  • Against ‚Äúmonk preserves books‚Äù clich√©

  • Library as an accelerator of knowledge circulation, not conservation


Analytical payoff
  • Trithemius as early information architect

  • Sets up later interest in secrecy, transmission, filtering

  • Historical, theological, and epistolary production

  • Self-fashioning as learned abbot


Historiography
  • Pushes back on ‚Äúhumanism = lay / courtly‚Äù models

  • Engages Strauss vs. Brann tension directly


Key contradiction
  • Trithemius both embodies and mythologizes monastic erudition

  • Frankish history controversies

Payoff

PART III ‚Äî MAGIC, BUT WHOSE MAGIC?

Chapter 5. The Steganographia Affair: When a Summary Becomes a Scandal


Biographical spine
  • 1499 letter summarizing Steganographia

  • Bovillus, Bostius, and reputational blowback


Historiography
  • Reputation crystallizes around circulating abstracts

  • Early modern version of virality and misinterpretation


Concept
  • Knowledge control failures produce ‚Äúmagical‚Äù reputations

CONCLUSION ‚Äî WHAT TRITHEMIUS WAS ACTUALLY DOING

Final synthesis (no moral verdict)


  • Trithemius as:

- Builder of knowledge infrastructures
- Manager of reputational risk
- Designer of symbolic systems
- Monk operating at the edge of institutional tolerance

Thesis statement

Trithemius is not best understood as magician, alchemist, or humanist, but as an early modern systems designer working under monastic constraints, whose greatest legacy is not belief but architecture.

Chapter 1
Introduction: The Theoretical and Biographical Ingredients
The Theological-Magical Nexus
One of the more prominently featured themes in the history of ideas,
extensively spelled out by Lynn Thorndike in a multivolumed study, is
the association between magic and science. ''My idea," explained
Thorndike at the outset of his vast scholarly undertaking, "is that
magic and experimental science have been connected in their
development; that magicians were perhaps the first to experiment; and
that the history of both magic and experimental science can be better
understood by studying them together."
1 Refining this thesis for the early modern period is Frances Yates,
who, by claiming that the Hermetic expression of magic in particular
played a substantive role in the evolution of modern science, has raised
a virtual hornets' nest in the midst of Renaissance studies.
The intellectual breakthrough setting the stage for the modern
scientific temper, according to Yates, required a critical change in the
conception of man's relation to the universe, the key of which lay in
the shift from a geocentric to heliocentric cosmic outlook. Instrumental
in promoting this revolutionary shift, Yates has argued,
< previous page page_1 next page >
If you like this book, buy it!
<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 17 / 746>
< previous page page_2 next page >
Page 2
was a revived Hermeticism. With Giordano Bruno her main focus,
whose Hermeticized world view reputedly resonated with and helped
to confirm the heliocentric cosmic outlook of Copernicus and Galileo,
Yates forced a reevaluation among Renaissance historians concerning
the proper connection between the Hermetic revival and the
seventeenth-century scientific revolution. While intrinsically
fascinating in its own right, however, it will not be our object here to
enter into the controversy surrounding the so-called Yates thesis
concerning the origins of modern science.
2 The subject of the present study, the abbot Trithemius (14621516),
rather highlights the affiliation of magic with another discipline more
basic than science, since even science was traditionally said to hinge
from it. Reference of course is to theology, which represents the
intellectualization of religious attitude and practice.
One of the founders of modern anthropology, Bronislaw Malinowski,
concluded after a lifelong investigation of the magically conditioned
Polynesian Trobrianders that, in their primitive beginnings, magic and
religion are essentially indistinguishable. Magic, Malinowski
determined on the basis of his Trobriander study, springs from an
instinctual and emotional need of the human being to press forth "into
impasses where gaps in his knowledge and the limitations of his early
power of observation and reason betray him in a crucial moment." An
important social consequence of this inner drive to resolve the enigmas
of existence, Malinowski maintained, is the formation of a system of
"rudimentary modes of behavior and rudimentary beliefs," with the
function of magical ritual being to fix and standardize such behavior
and beliefs into permanent forms.3 Inasmuch as these are traits which
also inherently belong to religion, it followed for Malinowski that
magic and religion, at bottom, are essentially one and the same.
When such primitive impulses to bridge the gaps of knowledge and
overcome human limitations are organized by religion into an
<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 18 / 746>
intellectual system, the result is theology. To the extent that religion, as
Malinowski would persuade us, can be identified with magic, the
result is "magical theology"theologia magica. It will be the object of
the present study to go one step further. When a conscious attempt is
made, as in the case of the abbot Trithemius, to recapture the religious
origins of magic and to harmonize its precepts with Christian dogma,
the outcome is more properly termed "Christian
< previous page page_2 next page >
If you like this book, buy it!
<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 19 / 746>
< previous page page_3 next page >
Page 3
magical theology"theologia magica Christiana. Though himself only
marginally immersed in practical magic (his specialty lay in the
techniques of cryptography, or secret communication through magical
means), Trithemius, first at Sponheim and later at W√ºrzburg, was not
content to leave the matter there. He devoted considerable time and
effort to the problem of how to rationalize the theory of magical
theology upon which his practical operations rested.
4
Living to the threshold of the Lutheran revolt from Rome, Trithemius
was immersed in a Christian tradition of magic that linked him with
occultist strains in the scholastic and humanist movements of the past
and the reform movements of the future on both sides of the CatholicÔøæProtestant divide. Much as Trithemius could scarcely uphold the
legitimacy and benefits of magic without reference to certain of its
leading champions of the Middle Ages, so did the sixteenth- and
seventeenth-century exponents of magical theology commonly call
upon the name and ideas of Trithemius among their forerunners to
justify their magical speculations and operations. Indeed, as we will
subsequently note, some were so taken by his magic, originally
deemed to constitute a bridge from the ancient pagan and Jewish
theologies to the theology of Christ, as to perceive in it a further bridge
to reconciliation between Catholics and Protestants.
If, as will be conceded, Trithemius shares some blame for the ensuing
witch persecutions by authoring certain writings which played a part in
their justification, it will be pointed out that he also helped set the
stage for the Renaissance and seventeenth-century movement,
spearheaded by the Paracelsians, to preserve a legitimate place for
magic within the orthodox theological schemes of Catholic and
Protestants alike. There were predecessors and even contemporaries, it
is true, who, assuming a more thoroughgoing and systematic approach
to the revival of the ancient arcane traditions than is represented by
<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 20 / 746>
Trithemius's sporadic outbursts in the same vein, may have exercised a
more decisive influence on later occult theory. Among the latter were
the Italian Platonists Marsilio Ficino and Giovanni Pico della
Mirandola and Trithemius's German acquaintances Johann Reuchlin
and Agrippa of Nettesheim.5 The point of the present study, however,
is not to uphold a dominant influence by Trithemius on the history of
magic. Rather,
< previous page page_3 next page >
If you like this book, buy it!
<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 21 / 746>
< previous page page_4 next page >
Page 4
on a more modest scale, it is to present the theological rationale for
magic of one of its more provocative Renaissance spokesmen, and to
show how that rationale was diversely received during the highly
volatile two centuries after the abbot's death commensurate with the
Reformation, the witch hunts, and the onset of the scientific revolution.
Needless to say, Trithemius's theory of magical theology did not arise
out of a vacuum. It was forged out of a particular life experience which
embraced, in conjunction with the occult studies, a strong desire to
effect spiritual reform both in its author and in the wider Christian
Church to which he devoted his monastic way of life. Prior to entering
into the theoretical intricacies of the magical program formulated by
Trithemius, accordingly, we need first, in a concluding section to this
introductory chapter, take stock of the concrete circumstances within
which that program was forged.
The Biographical Setting
Trithemius, according to a story put into circulation during the latter
half of the sixteenth century, was once summoned into the presence of
Emperor Maximilian I where, in a dramatic demonstration of
necromantic powers for which he had earned widespead notoriety, he
conjured from the dead, together with sundry ancient heroes,
Maximilian's own deceased wife Mary of Burgundy. That a similar tale
was contemporaneously afloat concerning a certain Doctor Faustus,
who was said to have performed a comparable feat for Maximilian's
son Charles V, was not lost to the demonological critics of both men.
As one among these, Christoph Zeisseler, observed in relation to the
Faustian anecdote: "Some men relate that this same act was performed
by Johannes Trithemius."
6
How, then, did the magical legend of the abbot Trithemius, coalesced
as it came to be with the legend of Faustus, take shape? Unlike the
<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 22 / 746>
historical Faustus, Trithemius came to his magical studies from what
initially was an entirely different slate of interests. Trithemius was first
and foremost a Christian monk, a member of the Benedictine order,
who dedicated his life to fulfilling the requirements of monastic piety.
Secondly, Trithemius was an exceptionally erudite monk who, as the
abbot of two monasteries during
< previous page page_4 next page >
If you like this book, buy it!
<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 23 / 746>
< previous page page_5 next page >
Page 5
his lifetime, zealously advocated in the pursuance of his monastic
goals, not piety alone, but learned piety. This feature of Trithemius's
career led him not only into more traditional ventures expected of the
monastic career, such as mystical theology and ecclesiastical history,
but also into the not so traditional venture of humanistic studies which
were in a state of widespread revival in his day. Thirdly, Trithemius
joined forces with other learned theologians of his day in taking the
offensive against those he considered to be foremost human conveyors
of the demonic arts, the witches and sorcerers. Only fourthly did
Trithemius himself enter into the arcane field of magic, centering on
the techniques of cryptography for his special interest but furnishing
them with a solid foundation of occult theory which also drew on
affiliated magical studies such as astrology, Pythagorean number
theory, alchemy, and Cabala.
7
What, then, are the biographical particulars that led Trithemius from
his uncontroversial beginnings to the controversies of his final
decades? Christened Johann Heidenberg, Trithemius acquired his Latin
name from his birthplace of Trittenheim on the banks of the Mosel.
Following the premature death of his father, his mother eventually
remarried and, adopting the new family name Zell, produced several
more children, of which only one, Johann's half-brother Jacob,
survived to maturity.8 Convinced by a "miraculous" dream at the age
of fifteen that he was destined to a life of letters, and frustrated at
every turn in this regard by a stern stepfather who had a very different
idea about his future, the young Johann was anxious to leave home as
soon as possible to pursue his education. Upon breaking away from his
parents Trithemius at first adopted an itinerant way of life, spending a
short spell in nearby Trier, moving on into the Netherlands, and at last
making his way to Heidelberg where, enrolled in the studium generale,
he came into association with some of the foremost German humanists
of his day. In the company of these, who included in their number
<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 24 / 746>
Johann von Dalberg, Conrad Celtis, Jacob Wimpfeling, and Johann
Reuchlin, Trithemius helped to form the Rhenish Literary Sodality.9
After completing his Heidelberg studies, in 1482, Trithemius set out in
the company of a friend on a journey back to the Mosel valley. He did
not, however, reach his goal, being caught up in a snow storm on his
way home and taking shelter in the nearby
< previous page page_5 next page >
If you like this book, buy it!
<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 25 / 746>
< previous page page_6 next page >
Page 6
Benedictine monastery of St. Martin at Sponheim, located in the
diocese of Mainz. In obedience to what he construed to be a
providential hand guiding his way, Trithemius, once the storm ceased,
chose to remain within the cloister's walls as a novice. By the age of
twenty-one he was elevated to the Sponheim abbacy, which office he
occupied for the next twenty-three years.
10
In his abbatial role Trithemius vigorously pursued the career of letters
which he believed that God had marked out for him. His avowed ideal
was "true monastic erudition"vera eruditio monastica, conceived as
the union of learning with piety, of the intellect with the will, of pagan
philosophy with the "philosophy of Christ." In keeping with this ideal
he gathered on the shelves of his abbey library a large collection of
texts, "of about two thousand volumes, both handwritten and printed,''
as he later nostalgically recalled after being compelled to forsake them
following his move to W√ºrzburg. To promote this venture he pressured
his monks into handwriting texts in the monastic scriptorium even as
the printing revolution was making that task seemingly superfluous.11
And at his writing desk he undertook his own private literary career,
concentrating for most of his Sponheim period in the areas of monastic
reform, mystical theology, ecclesiastical history, and Christian
humanism in keeping with the principles of literary elegance he shared
with his associates of the Rhenish sodality.12 In both functions, as
bibliophile and literary scholar, Trithemius became renowned
throughout Europe, in which dual role he served as an attractive
magnet for some of the most illustrious men of his age. Among these
were not only leading literary scholars of his day, but also a number of
princely patrons. Of the topics of interest championed by Trithemius
which increasingly drew these visitors to his cloister, not the least
noteworthy is that of magic.13
<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 26 / 746>
If Trithemius gave much to his many studious visitors, he also took
much from them. This held true especially in the area of language
study, concerning which, he later acknowledged, he owed his earliest
lessons in Hebrew to an unnamed Jew he had met in Heidelberg, his
first Greek lessons to Celtis, and his advanced lessons in both
languages to Reuchlin. It was especially as a Greek scholar that
Trithemius earned fame in humanist quarters, with one visitor to
Sponheim revelling in this regard: "The abbot was Greek, his monks
Greeks, and likewise Greek were his dogs, stones, and vineyards.
< previous page page_6 next page >
If you like this book, buy it!
<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 27 / 746>
< previous page page_7 next page >
Page 7
And that entire monastery seemed as though it were located in the
middle of Ionia."
14 Both the proficient Greek and the less proficient Hebrew of the
abbot, as it turned out, directly played into the formulation of his
magical theories, since, through Greek, he gained access to the obscure
mysteries of Pythagoras and Hermes Trismegistus, and through
Hebrew, to the mysteries of Cabala.
Until 1499 the visible scope of Trithemius's intellectual activity was
largely circumscribed by the conventional boundaries of the medieval
liberal arts curriculum. Then, as if out of the blue, Trithemius in that
year declared himself to be an exponent of the occult arts. The form in
which this announcement took place was a letter addressed to a
Carmelite monk of Ghent, Arnold Bostius, the object of which was to
inform the correspondent of a treatise the abbot was currently
composing. The subject was steganography, that is, the art of writing
secret messages and transmitting them over long distances through the
mediation of angelic messengers. Unhappily for the future reputation
of Trithemius, Bostius was not in a position to receive the abbot's
letter, having died shortly before its arrival. As a result, words intended
for Bostius's eyes alone fell into the hands of the unsympathetic prior
of the cloister, who, expressing shock by what he beheld therein to be
an admission of illicit demonic magic, circulated the abbot's words to
the general public. Thus was born Trithemius's magical legend.15
Reinforcing the abbot's notoriety as a black magician was a sharply
negative response to the cryptographic tract in question by a visitor to
Sponheim a few years later, the French scholar Carolus Bovillus, who,
during a fortnight stay at Sponheim, was requested by his host to read
and comment upon the completed portions of his steganographical
tract. To the abbot's surprise and consternation Bovillus was far from
pleased by what he gleaned therein, and at some later date, after
pondering the experience, wrote a letter to a mutual friend of the two
<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 28 / 746>
men in which he recounted his Sponheim visit and acrimoniously
stigmatized his host as a demonic magician.16 While not discouraged
by these traumatic events from further engaging in his magical
speculations, Trithemius was chastened by them into being more
circumspect about their disclosure, one facet of which lay in his
decision to retain the completed portions of the steganographical
handbook in manuscript form accessible only to specially selected
disciples.
< previous page page_7 next page >
If you like this book, buy it!
<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 29 / 746>
< previous page page_8 next page >
Page 8
In the ensuing years Trithemius and his Sponheim monks developed
increasingly strained relations, and by 1505 they mutually agreed to a
parting of their ways. There is no direct evidence that Trithemius's
emerging magical interests played a part in this decision, the more
probable bone of contention lying in the abbot's imposition of highly
demanding scholarly standards upon his monks which even included
the hand copying of texts as if the art of printing had never come into
existence. As the years of his Sponheim abbacy wore on, Trithemius
evidently became looked upon by his monks as an uncompromising
taskmaster whose exactions from them went well beyond what they
believed to be the just requirements of their vows. However, while
Trithemius's rapidly proliferating reputation as a magician may have
played no direct role in the quarrel with his Sponheim monks, it may
well have played at least an indirect role. For very likely exacerbating
the tension between the resident abbot and monks was the disturbing
intrusion into their cloistered tranquillity of many visitors. While some
of these were certainly attracted by the conventional monastic subjects
of Trithemius's scholarly expertise, others were undoubtedly attracted
by his reputation for unconventional subjects in the arcana and by the
many magical offerings on his monastic shelves.
In any case the bad rapport between Trithemius and his Sponheim
monks reached a breaking point, and in 1505 the abbot was handed a
convenient means to finalize the break by accepting an invitation from
the margrave-elector Joachim of Brandenburg to meet him during an
assembly of the German princes in Cologne. Trithemius's official
resignation from Sponheim came the following year when, after
spending a period itinerantly (part of the time as Joachim's guest in
Berlin), he succeeded in finding a new post at the head of the
monastery of St. Jacob in W√ºrzburg. Here Trithemius was to last out
the remainder of his life, dying in 1516 at the relatively young age of
fifty-four.
<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 30 / 746>
17 It is true that, in his new W√ºrzburg home, Trithemius often found
cause to lament the loss of his Sponheim cloister and the magnificent
library which he had collected there. The trade-off, however, was that
his changed environment provided him with much-needed peace and
quiet. Being no longer besieged by the strife characteristic of his
Sponheim years, Trithemius at last felt free to pursue untrammeled the
ambitious
< previous page page_8 next page >
If you like this book, buy it!
<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 31 / 746>
< previous page page_9 next page >
Page 9
literary program to which he had dedicated himself years before.
18 Among the works completed during this seminal period were some,
such as the Hirsau annals, which might be expected of a monastic
scholar. Other writings appearing during this period of intense literary
activity, however, did not so easily

4: Converging Magical Legends:
Faustus, Paracelsus, and Trithemius
Urs Leo Gantenbein
Introduction to an Old Association
AS EARLY AS 1834, two years after Goethe‚Äôs death, the apparent resemÔøæblance of his Faust figure with Paracelsus led John S. Blackie, in the
preface to his rendition of Goethe‚Äôs tragedy into English verse, to the folÔøælowing statement: ‚ÄúThere is much in all that is told of him [Faust] that
recalls to our mind the biography of Paracelsus.‚Äù1 By the end of the nineÔøæteenth century, this assumption acquired a degree of certainty for Goethe
scholars. In 1870, Gustav von Loeper2 remarked that Goethe‚Äôs Faust is a
physician and a physician‚Äôs son, as is Paracelsus. Both figures detested the
medicine of their time and had a predilection for the German language.
Von Loeper‚Äôs second edition (1879) added that the historical Faust and
Paracelsus were both traveling scholars.3 Karl Julius Schr√∂er in 1879,4
1881, and 1886;5 Calvin Thomas in 1892;6 and Jacob Minor, in 1901,7
added to or qualified the assessment.
The efforts to shed light on the origins of the Faust character culmiÔøænated in 1911 with Agnes Bartscherer‚Äôs Paracelsus, Paracelsisten und Goethes
Faust, which remains by far the most important study of this theme.8 Based
on a profound knowledge of the Paracelsian works, she treats the metaphysiÔøæcal subjects that occur in the Faust tragedy: magic, demonology, alchemy,
astrology, witchcraft, mantic arts, and cosmology. Bartscherer gives positive
proof of the evident fact that the more the student of Goethe knows about
Paracelsus, the more she discovers links to Faust.
A similar development took place in Paracelsus studies with Henry
Pachter (Heinz P√§chter), whose 1955 biography of Paracelsus bore the
attribution ‚ÄúDas Urbild des Doktor Faustus‚Äù (the archetype of Doctor
Faustus).9 He implies that our image of Faust had been influenced by
Paracelsus rather than by the historical Faustus. According to Dietlinde
Goltz, however, this was not always based on historical-critical facts but
sometimes mirrored the authors‚Äô imaginations and romantic conceptions
of Faust.10 As we will see, Goethe indeed used the inspiration he drew
from the writings of Paracelsus to embellish his Faust figure.

94 URS LEO GANTENBEIN
The reasons for the likeness between the historical Georg (or Johann)
Faustus (ca. 1480‚Äìca. 1540) and Theophrastus of Hohenheim, called
Paracelsus (1493/94‚Äì1541), may be rather simple. Both were traveling
scholars with interests in magic and the mantic arts, and both boasted of
their knowledge and gained widespread fame. Given the dearth of historiÔøæcal facts about Faustus, these might be the only parallels we can detect.
However, Faustus and Paracelsus were not singular occurrences. They
represent the type of the Renaissance man who engaged with natural
magic.11 For them, magical speculation was a prescientific attempt to
understand the whole of nature not only its visible and observable aspects
but also the invisible ones, including the metaphysical cosmos, which in
their eyes was swarming with elementary and planetary spirits, angels, and
devils. In the spirit of the Renaissance, they returned to the available
magical and metaphysical sources: Hermetical lore, the philosophies of
Plato and Plotinus, the Hebrew Kabbalah and the Arabic picatrix. They
operated in a network of Renaissance magi encompassing Marsilio Ficino,
Giovanni Pico della Mirandola, Nicholas of Cusa, Johannes Trithemius,
Johannes Reuchlin, and Agrippa von Nettesheim. Paracelsus must be seen
in the light of these endeavors, though not exclusively so, for he was also
a child of the early Reformation with its focus on the literal word of the
Bible.12 There are indeed even closer similarities between Heinrich
Cornelius Agrippa von Nettesheim (1486‚Äì1535) and Paracelsus than
between Faustus and Paracelsus.13 For Agrippa was a student of
Trithemius, the abbot of Sponheim, and Paracelsus reported having been
taught by ‚Äúvil Ept als von Spanhaym‚Äù (many abbots, as the one of
Spanheim).14 Both had been army physicians and traveled widely in
Europe; and both had pursued an interest in magic and written books
about the subject, though Agrippa was a more systematic writer than
Paracelsus.
How much the topos of the traveling scholar practicing magic and
divination was imprinted on early modern mentalities is impressively
shown in a carnival play by Hans Sachs from 1551. In his Der farend
schuler mit dem teuffel-pannen (the traveling scholar with his conjuration
of the devil),15 he depicts a student who boasts of the black arts he has
learned at the universities. Sachs is alluding to the fact that in early modern
Europe universities like Salamanca and Cracow offered magic as an official
subject. Faustus is said to have studied and even taught in Cracow. The
abilities of Sachs‚Äôs ‚Äúfarend schuler‚Äù include retrieving stolen goods, fabriÔøæcating amulets for aching eyes and teeth, preventing gunshot wounds by
means of blessings, soothsaying, finding treasures, and riding the devil‚Äôs
billy goat (Bock) in the night.16 To protect the pastor planning an amorous
t√™te-√†-t√™te with a peasant woman from the untimely return and wrath of
her husband, the scholar feigns a conjuration with the blackened pastor in
the guise of a devil. Against this background, it is clear why men like

Trithemius and the Legend of the Wild Hunt
https://doi.org/10.1515/fabula-2018-0100
Zusammenfassung: Das rege Interesse des vielseitigen und renommierten TheoÔøælogen, Kryptologen und Chronisten Johannes Trithemius (1462‚Äì1516) an allen

Abstract: It is well-established that Johannes Trithemius (1462‚Äì1516), the
renowned polymath (theologian, cryptographer, and chronicler), entertained an
avid interest in all things concerning magic. Scholars have neglected, however, to
pay heed to his views about The Wild Hunt/Wild Huntsman, a widely propagated
tapestry of legends rooted in popular mythology. We examine four passages from
his chronicles to shed light on the many facets of this lore. The subjects of these
testimonies include: a phantom army; a cursed nocturnal hunter; and two aerial
hunting sorcerers, one of whom was a Jewish magician who conjured the illusion
of venery by deceiving the senses. The writings of Trithemius offer little indicaÔøætion of skepticism about the phantasmal chase. On the contrary, the abbot proÔøævides a precise chronology (878‚Äì1354) of these extraordinary tales of the hunt in
order to lend credibility to them. Each entry can be read as a cautionary example,
but the religious veneer seems shallow.

Introduction
Lindow so defines the legend of the Wild Hunt:
a legend complex concerning a ghostly hunter, often accompanied by dogs or spirits and
often hunting a woman, who is frequently one of the supernatural beings [‚Ä¶] In other forms
of the legend more common in Germany, the hunter may be a lost soul, or he may lead lost
souls on a wild ride through the sky [‚Ä¶] (Lindow 2000, 1036)
The Wild Hunt is a pan-European myth prominent in folklore, charted since the
eleventh century, and named Die wilde Jagd already in the eighteenth century.1
Despite its ubiquity, the Wild Hunt is neither uniform nor immutable. There are
many versions of the Hunt, which range from a single diabolical huntsman to a
raging/furious host¬†‚Äì troops of the dead or phantom armies. Sounds are promiÔøænent: yelping dogs, wind, and horses‚Äô hoof-beats, each of which portends grievÔøæous affliction: Oft bellen die Hunde der Luft in finsterer Nacht (Grimm 1854, 876).
A widespread belief identifies the furious host as consisting of men, women, and
children¬†‚Äì those having perished before their time, perhaps having experienced a
particularly violent death. Ginzburg calls this group ‚Äúthe menacing apparition of
the unplacated dead‚Äù (Ginzburg 1991, 101).
Generalizations about the Wild Hunt and associated phenomena are difficult.
Menzel, focusing on the role of Odin, a sky god, in the formation of the Wild Hunt,
stresses the many aspects of the concept:

Ninth-Century Figural Poetry and Medieval Easter
Tables‚ÄîPossible Inspirations for the Square Tables
of Trithemius and Vigenere?
GERHARD F. STRASSER
Abstract While it is not possible to identify exact sources for the square tables
that Johannes Trithemius and, after him, Blaise de Vigenere presented in their
cryptographic publications, there is good evidence that Trithemius may have been
influenced by a variety of materials: He knew the figural poetry of Rabanus
Maurus (780‚Äì856) that frequently used squares with a grid of 36 letters; he was
fully aware of the medieval ars combinatoria and the works of the Mallorcan
philosopher and theologian Raymundus Lullus (1233‚Äì1316), where he would have
also found combinatorial circular disks; and he may have discerned a pattern for
his square table in the medieval Easter or Lenten tables used for the calculation of
the forty days of Lent and the days of Easter over a period of years.
Keywords ars combinatoria, Easter or lenten tables, figural poetry, Polygraphiae
libri VI, Rabanus Maurus, Raymundus Lullus, square table, Steganographia,
Trithemius, Vigene`re
In a recent article in this journal on ‚Äò‚ÄòThe Future of the Past,‚Äô‚Äô [2] David Kahn
wondered whether the tables that were used in the Catholic church throughout the
Middle Ages to predict the date of Easter might have been a possible inspiration
for the square table (tabula transpositionis) (Figure 1) that Johannes Trithemius first
published in Book V of the Polygraphiae libri VI, which was printed in 1518, two
years after his death. While Leon Battista Alberti had first proposed the use of disks
for polyalphabetic substitution in 1466, Trithemius‚Äôs square table does not require
such a device and greatly facilitates encryption. His cryptologic invention therefore
represents a
