tell me more about trithemius relationship to hermeticism

Assistant

Trithemius‚Äôs relationship to Hermeticism, as it emerges from the sources you‚Äôve been working through (especially Brann, Partington, Zika-by-context, and the Ripley/Paracelsus studies), is deep, selective, and structural rather than devotional or revivalist. He is not a Hermetic enthusiast in the Ficinian sense, nor a system-builder like later Rosicrucians. Hermeticism for Trithemius is a framework for thinking about mediation, ascent, secrecy, and order, not a self-contained body of revealed doctrine.

What stands out first is that Trithemius approaches Hermeticism as a Christian abbot already committed to orthodoxy, not as someone seeking an alternative wisdom tradition. He never treats Hermes Trismegistus as an independent pagan authority competing with Scripture. Instead, Hermetic texts‚Äîespecially the Emerald Tablet‚Äîare valuable because they appear to articulate, in compressed symbolic form, truths that Christianity already knows: the correspondence of above and below, the return of multiplicity to unity, and the possibility of ascent through purification. In Brann‚Äôs reading, Trithemius consistently subordinates Hermetic insight to Christian teleology. Hermes is not a prophet; he is a witness.

This helps explain why Trithemius‚Äôs most explicit engagement with Hermeticism takes the form of commentary and interpretation rather than compilation or translation. The 1505 letter to Germanus de Ganay on the Tabula Smaragdina is crucial. Trithemius does not present the Tablet as a recipe or technical guide. Instead, he treats it as a theological diagram. The famous lines about ascent and descent, unity and multiplicity, are read as describing the soul‚Äôs movement toward God, not metallurgical transmutation. This places Trithemius closer to George Ripley‚Äôs contemplative alchemy than to Paracelsian spagyrics, even though Paracelsians later claimed him as a precursor.

Hermeticism, for Trithemius, is therefore primarily symbolic and cosmological. It supplies a language for hierarchy‚Äîangelic orders, planetary intelligences, numerical harmonies‚Äîbut that hierarchy is always integrated into Christian angelology and moral theology. This is why Trithemius can freely use Hermetic vocabulary while simultaneously condemning ‚Äúfalse alchemists‚Äù and ‚Äúvain seekers of gold.‚Äù The problem is not Hermes; it is misreading Hermes.

A second defining feature of Trithemius‚Äôs Hermeticism is its obsession with mediation. Hermetic texts are valuable because they dramatize how divine power does not act directly, but through layers: numbers, names, angels, celestial influences, and signs. This dovetails perfectly with Trithemius‚Äôs broader intellectual project. Libraries, chronologies, cryptographic tables, angelic hierarchies, and Hermetic correspondences are all doing the same work: managing distance between God and the world. Hermeticism becomes one strand in a larger theory of indirect causation.

This is where Trithemius sharply diverges from later occultist readings. He does not treat Hermeticism as a license for operational experimentation. On the contrary, Hermetic insight increases the danger of misuse. Because Hermetic principles are powerful and abstract, they must be restricted to trained, morally qualified readers. This is why Hermeticism and secrecy become inseparable in his thought. The Steganographia‚Äôs Hermetic surface‚Äîangelic names, planetary orders‚Äîfunctions as both invitation and barrier. Those who read it literally expose themselves as unfit.

Hermeticism also plays a crucial role in Trithemius‚Äôs polemics. Against proto-Protestant critiques that collapse ritual into superstition, Hermeticism helps defend the idea that signs can act without being demonic. Against popular magic and conjuration, it provides a model of non-demonic efficacy rooted in cosmic order rather than spirits coerced by force. In this sense, Hermeticism becomes a middle position between miracle and superstition, one that Trithemius desperately tries to stabilize but never fully succeeds in doing.

The sources also show that Trithemius‚Äôs Hermeticism is textually conservative. Unlike Ficino or later Hermetic revivalists, he does not aggressively expand the Hermetic corpus, speculate on lost books, or imagine secret oral traditions. His Hermeticism is bounded by what can be defended through patristic precedent, medieval transmission, and moral interpretation. This conservatism is one reason later generations find him unsatisfying as a ‚Äúmagus‚Äù and retroactively inflate his reputation with spurious texts.

Finally, Trithemius‚Äôs Hermeticism is inseparable from his anxiety about error. Hermetic truths are easily corrupted: mistranslated, literalized, detached from theology, or weaponized for gain. This is why Hermeticism never appears alone in his work. It is always paired with warnings, hierarchies, filters, and apologies. In Trithemius‚Äôs hands, Hermeticism is not a promise of liberation; it is a burden of responsibility.

Taken together, the sources show a Trithemius who uses Hermeticism as a structural resource rather than a spiritual identity. It helps him think about ascent without enthusiasm, power without possession, and knowledge without democratization. That is precisely why he becomes so important for later figures‚Äîand why those figures so often misunderstand him.

Title: [SUNY Series in Western Esoteric Traditions] Noel L. Brann - Trithemius and Magical Theology_ A Chapter in the Controversy over Occult Studies in Early Modern Europe (1998, State University of New York Press) - libgen.pdf

Mclick Target: "9:2"
Content Snippet:
[L1] "93
[L2] < previous page page_124 next page >
[L3] If you like this book, buy it!<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 254 / 746>< previous page page_125 next page >
[L4] Page 125
[L5] The occasion provoking the somewhat later letter to Ganay was
[L6] Ganay's discovery of an earlier epistle from Trithemius to his disciple
[L7] Steinmoelthe same Steinmoel, it will be remembered, earlier berated
[L8] for abusing his instructional privilegesasking for clarification of an
[L9] obscure reference to "a very rare and admirable philosophy, shrouded
[L10] in numbers, elements, and enigmas, and abstruse with arcane words, so
[L11] that its meaning is entirely lost to me and far exceeds the present
[L12] capacity of my mind."
[L13] 94 Not to our surprise, Trithemius clothed his reply to Ganay's query
[L14] in the numerological idiom of Pythagoras characteristic of his earlier
[L15] letters to Westerburg and Joachim. What distinguishes the 1505 Ganay
[L16] letter from its 1503 forerunners, however, is the abbot's combination of
[L17] Pythagorean precepts with those of another "ancient theologian,"
[L18] Hermes Trismegistus.
[L19] The Hermetic work constituting the mainstay of the expository
[L20] response of Trithemius to Ganay is the alchemical Emerald Tablethe
[L21] tabula smaragdina. Beginning with a typically Pythagorean rendering
[L22] of his "very rare and admirable philosophy," Trithemius counseled
[L23] Ganay that for the mind to achieve a perfect understanding in these
[L24] matters, "the ternary must be completely reduced to unity, for though
[L25] unity is not a number, every number arises out of it." However, this
[L26] time around the same idea also received an alternative alchemical
[L27] mode of expression, corresponding to the second of thirteen
[L28] alchemical precepts comprising the Hermetic Emerald Table: "What is
[L29] superior is like that which is inferior, and what is inferior is like that
[L30] which is superior (for only by unities does every number persevere), so
[L31] that there can be accomplished many miracles of one thing." In
[L32] clarification of what now in effect consituted a Pythagorean-Hermetic
[L33] composite, Trithemius inquired of Ganay: ''Is it not true that all things
[L34] flow from one thing, from the goodness of the One, and that whatever
[L35] is joined to unity cannot be diverse, but rather fructifies by means of
[L36] the simplicity and adaptability of the One?" Significantly, Trithemius<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 255 / 746>found helpful to illustrate this principle the same kind of play on his
[L37] name which, corresponding to the twelfth precept of the Emerald
[L38] Table, was also commonly applied to "thrice-great" Trismegistus.
[L39] Explained the abbot, evidently with encouragement by his teacher
[L40] Libanius: "I, Trithemius, am not comprised of three minds, but, while
[L41] exulting in the ternary, endure within one integrated mind giving birth
[L42] to a marvelous offspring (qui parit mirabilem foetum)."95
[L43] < previous page page_125 next page >
[L44] If you like this book, buy it!<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 256 / 746>< previous page page_126 next page >
[L45] Page 126
[L46] Elaborating on the generation of the Hermetic mirabilis foetus,
[L47] Trithemius reiterated the Emerald Table's fourth precept that "its father
[L48] is the sun, its mother the moon; the wind carried its seed in her belly,
[L49] and the earth nourished it."

[L1] Declares the abbot in
[L2] explanation of the claim of the ninth precept that the so-called "Philosopher's Stone"-
[L3] another name for the Hone thing" \vhich is the primary goal of the alchemical adept-is
[L4] miraculously able "to conquer every subtle thing and to penetrate every solid": "This very
[L5] noble virtue (pulcherrima virtus), which conquers over all worldly things and penetrates
[L6] every solid body, consists of maximal fortitude, touching everything with its desirable
[L7] excellence." 14
[L8] In his explanatory letter to Ganay Trithemius also cautions against the possible abuse
[L9] of these noble principles, thereby drawing the clear-cut distinction in his correspondent's
[L10] mind between what he terms vera alchy"~ia and its false look-alike. Alltoo many alchemists
[L11] can be found, Trithemius acknowledges, \\rho,being more desirous of becoming wealthy in a
[L12] worldly sense than in the spiritual one implicit in the Emerald Table,'\vander from the
[L13] true course and are deceived, and in turn deceive all those by whom they have been gladly
[L14] heard". These avaricious corrupters of alchemy, Trithemius charges, "since they do not
[L15] understand the source ofthe power ofnature (radicem virtutisnaturae), would imitate nature
[L16] by breaking into parts that \vhich is wholly universal; . . . they are fatuous men and
[L17] disciples of the apes, enemies to nature, and despisers of the heavens \vithout whose
[L18] intelligible rapport there can exist no alchemy". In contrast to this illicit form of alchemy
[L19] -referred to else\vhere by Trithemius as a "chaste \vhore" (casta meretrix) \vho is widely
[L20] renowned for promising many pleasures to her lovers but in the end witholding that \vhich is
[L21] promised- Trithemius proclaims as the elevated goal of all true alchemy:
[L22] Our philosophy is celestial, not worldly (Nostra philosophia coelestis est non
[L23] terrena), in order that we may faithfully behold, by means of a direct intuition of
[L24] the mind through faith and knowledge, that principle (principium) which we call
[L25] God, Father, Son, and Holy Spirit, one Principle (unum principium), one God,
[L26] and one highest Good existing in a Trinity of eternal persons, and also so that we may
[L27] believe in His existence with conviction and become intimately acquainted with
[L28] Him from Whom all things which are able to be present anywhere derive, ahvays
[L29] adoring Him with a most fervent show of love and service. Unless the mind is
[L30] enlivened and surges upward to this. destination, it may comprehend nothing of
[L31] those things which are really noble (pulchra), but must languish in its o\vn igÔøænorance.I5
[L32] Making appeal in this vein to certain "ancient wise men" (antiqui sapientes) who put
[L33] forth this doctrine as a '''yay to the divine" (ad superos via), Trithemius leaves no doubt to
[L34] Ganay but that a leader among those antiqui sapientes, the presumed author of a tract
[L35] praising man as a1nagnum 1niraculttm by virtue of his inward magical powers, was the very
[L36] same Hermes Trismegistus presumed to be the author of the Emerald Table.

Title: [Sixteenth Century Journal vol. 10 iss. 1] Noel L. Brann - Was Paracelsus a Disciple of Trithemius_ (1979) [10.2307_2539686] - libgen.li.pdf

[L1] "2 4
[L2] Just as Paracelsus sometimes appeared to lose sight of the forcibly stated
[L3] distinction made in his Paragranum between the macrocosmic light of nature
[L4] and the microcosmic light of the inner soul, so did Trithemius on the opposite
[L5] side of the question from that of Paracelsus occasionally show signs of blurÔøæring the distinction between the two parallel Hermetic currents. In this
[L6] tendency to obfuscate the contrast between the internal and external apÔøæproaches to magic the Abbot Trithemius and his alleged disciple Paracelsus
[L7] were anticipated alike by Ripley, who in the very midst of advocating a
[L8] spagyrical form of medicine in the Paracelsian manner enunciated the ironclad
[L9] rule highly reminiscent of the somewhat later advice of Trithemius to the
[L10] Count of Westerburg that "no one is able to initiate us in these mysteries
[L11] unless, by a divine gift, he has received the incomparable light of underÔøæstanding:" "It is impossible for anyone among mortals to understand this
[L12] [alchemical] art unless he is first illuminated by a divine light."2 5
[L13] As time was to tell, moreover, the same critical distinction between the
[L14] two versions of Hermeticism, one predominantly microcosmic and the other
[L15] predominantly macrocosmic, proved to be too subtle to be sustained with any
[L16] consistency by the great majority of Paracelsus enthusiasts in the later sixÔøæteenth and seventeenth centuries. Hence was it able to come about, beginning
[L17] with the sixteenth century Paracelsus commentator Jacquey Gohory (=Leo
[L18] Suavius, d. 1576), that a foremost medium for the later defense of TriÔøæthemius' magic against its detractors was the Paracelsian movement.26 In spite
[L19] 2 According to tradition the Emerald Table, or Tabula Smaragdina, was discovered
[L20] in Hermes' Egyptian tomb clasped in the hands of its occupying mummy. For a complete
[L21] listing of its thirteen precepts, allegedly translated from their original Phoenician language
[L22] into Latin, see Julius Ruska, Tabula Smaragdina: Ein Beitrag zur Geschichte der HerÔøæmetischen Literatur (Heidelberg: Winter, 1926), p. 2. The English rendering of the
[L23] seventh precept here used is by John Read, Prelude to Chemistry: An Outline of
[L24] Alchemy (New York, 1937; Cambridge, Mass.: M.I.T., 1966), p. 54. An explicit paraÔøæphrase of the Emerald Table is provided by Trithemius in a letter to Germanus de Ganay,
[L25] Speyer, August 24, 1505, Epist. fam., 1:34, in Freher, ed., Op. hist., II, 271-272. On
[L26] this epistle see Lynn Thorndike, A History of Magic and Experimental Science, 8 vols.
[L27] (New York: Macmillan, 1929-1958), VI (1951), 438.
[L28] 2"Ripley, in Zetzner, II, 22: "Impossibile ut aliquis mortalius hance artem intelÔøæligat, nisi divino lumine prius illustretur." For the perpetuation of this notion, through
[L29] Paracelsian mediation, into the seventeenth century see Frances Yates, The Rosicrucian
[L30] Enlightenment (London: Routledge and Kegan Paul, 1972).

[L27] mind between what he terms vera alchy"~ia and its false look-alike. Alltoo many alchemists
[L28] can be found, Trithemius acknowledges, \\rho,being more desirous of becoming wealthy in a
[L29] worldly sense than in the spiritual one implicit in the Emerald Table,'\vander from the
[L30] true course and are deceived, and in turn deceive all those by whom they have been gladly
[L31] heard". These avaricious corrupters of alchemy, Trithemius charges, "since they do not
[L32] understand the source ofthe power ofnature (radicem virtutisnaturae), would imitate nature
[L33] by breaking into parts that \vhich is wholly universal; . . . they are fatuous men and
[L34] disciples of the apes, enemies to nature, and despisers of the heavens \vithout whose
[L35] intelligible rapport there can exist no alchemy".

[L1] 92
[L2] In calling for the passion of love in the magician, it goes without
[L3] saying, Trithemius did not mean a mere earthly affection of the kind
[L4] touted by "certain gentile philosophers, and many today located
[L5] outside the bounds of Christianity." The love to which he referred is
[L6] Heaven-bound Christian love, and the knowledge infused by it,
[L7] Christian knowledge. At a deeper level of analysis, accordingly,
[L8] Trithemius's two-term formula cognitio-amor can be said to expand to
[L9] a three-term cognitio-amor-fides. "The primary road to God,"
[L10] Trithemius instructed Capellarius with this expanded formula in mind,
[L11] "is science or knowledge through faith (scientia sive cognitio per
[L12] fidem), without which no man can be saved." Explaining to Capellarius
[L13] that "true knowledge arises out of faith, and love out of knowledge,"
[L14] Trithemius drew the logical conclusion that "a man who does not have
[L15] faith does not have knowledge, he who lacks knowledge lacks love,
[L16] and he who does not have love cannot experience the final enjoyment.''<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 253 / 746>Accordingly, "to this true knowledge (scientia vera), my mathematical
[L17] friend, must all your sciences and studies be referred, because unless
[L18] you bring this to pass, you will consume yourself in empty and foolish
[L19] labor." It is an inborn feature of such "true knowledge," Trithemius
[L20] apprised Capellarius, that, "instead of extolling the knowledgeable
[L21] person, it rather instills him with compunction and love, and instead of
[L22] inflating its possessor with pride, causes him to sigh with humility."93

[L26] The occasion provoking the somewhat later letter to Ganay was
[L27] Ganay's discovery of an earlier epistle from Trithemius to his disciple
[L28] Steinmoelthe same Steinmoel, it will be remembered, earlier berated
[L29] for abusing his instructional privilegesasking for clarification of an
[L30] obscure reference to "a very rare and admirable philosophy, shrouded
[L31] in numbers, elements, and enigmas, and abstruse with arcane words, so
[L32] that its meaning is entirely lost to me and far exceeds the present
[L33] capacity of my mind."
[L34] 94 Not to our surprise, Trithemius clothed his reply to Ganay's query
[L35] in the numerological idiom of Pythagoras characteristic of his earlier
[L36] letters to Westerburg and Joachim. What distinguishes the 1505 Ganay
[L37] letter from its 1503 forerunners, however, is the abbot's combination of
[L38] Pythagorean precepts with those of another "ancient theologian,"
[L39] Hermes Trismegistus.
[L40] The Hermetic work constituting the mainstay of the expository
[L41] response of Trithemius to Ganay is the alchemical Emerald Tablethe
[L42] tabula smaragdina. Beginning with a typically Pythagorean rendering
[L43] of his "very rare and admirable philosophy," Trithemius counseled
[L44] Ganay that for the mind to achieve a perfect understanding in these
[L45] matters, "the ternary must be completely reduced to unity, for though
[L46] unity is not a number, every number arises out of it."

[L225] Chapter 1
[L226] Introduction: The Theoretical and Biographical Ingredients
[L227] The Theological-Magical Nexus
[L228] One of the more prominently featured themes in the history of ideas,
[L229] extensively spelled out by Lynn Thorndike in a multivolumed study, is
[L230] the association between magic and science. ''My idea," explained
[L231] Thorndike at the outset of his vast scholarly undertaking, "is that
[L232] magic and experimental science have been connected in their
[L233] development; that magicians were perhaps the first to experiment; and
[L234] that the history of both magic and experimental science can be better
[L235] understood by studying them together."
[L236] 1 Refining this thesis for the early modern period is Frances Yates,
[L237] who, by claiming that the Hermetic expression of magic in particular
[L238] played a substantive role in the evolution of modern science, has raised
[L239] a virtual hornets' nest in the midst of Renaissance studies.
[L240] The intellectual breakthrough setting the stage for the modern
[L241] scientific temper, according to Yates, required a critical change in the
[L242] conception of man's relation to the universe, the key of which lay in
[L243] the shift from a geocentric to heliocentric cosmic outlook. Instrumental
[L244] in promoting this revolutionary shift, Yates has argued,
[L245] < previous page page_1 next page >
[L246] If you like this book, buy it!
[L247] <PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 17 / 746>
[L248] < previous page page_2 next page >
[L249] Page 2
[L250] was a revived Hermeticism. With Giordano Bruno her main focus,
[L251] whose Hermeticized world view reputedly resonated with and helped
[L252] to confirm the heliocentric cosmic outlook of Copernicus and Galileo,
[L253] Yates forced a reevaluation among Renaissance historians concerning
[L254] the proper connection between the Hermetic revival and the
[L255] seventeenth-century scientific revolution. While intrinsically
[L256] fascinating in its own right, however, it will not be our object here to
[L257] enter into the controversy surrounding the so-called Yates thesis
[L258] concerning the origins of modern science.
[L259] 2 The subject of the present study, the abbot Trithemius (14621516),
[L260] rather highlights the affiliation of magic with another discipline more
[L261] basic than science, since even science was traditionally said to hinge
[L262] from it. Reference of course is to theology, which represents the
[L263] intellectualization of religious attitude and practice.
[L264] One of the founders of modern anthropology, Bronislaw Malinowski,
[L265] concluded after a lifelong investigation of the magically conditioned
[L266] Polynesian Trobrianders that, in their primitive beginnings, magic and
[L267] religion are essentially indistinguishable. Magic, Malinowski
[L268] determined on the basis of his Trobriander study, springs from an
[L269] instinctual and emotional need of the human being to press forth "into
[L270] impasses where gaps in his knowledge and the limitations of his early
[L271] power of observation and reason betray him in a crucial moment." An
[L272] important social consequence of this inner drive to resolve the enigmas
[L273] of existence, Malinowski maintained, is the formation of a system of
[L274] "rudimentary modes of behavior and rudimentary beliefs," with the
[L275] function of magical ritual being to fix and standardize such behavior
[L276] and beliefs into permanent forms.3 Inasmuch as these are traits which
[L277] also inherently belong to religion, it followed for Malinowski that
[L278] magic and religion, at bottom, are essentially one and the same.
[L279] When such primitive impulses to bridge the gaps of knowledge and
[L280] overcome human limitations are organized by religion into an
[L281] <PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 18 / 746>
[L282] intellectual system, the result is theology. To the extent that religion, as
[L283] Malinowski would persuade us, can be identified with magic, the
[L284] result is "magical theology"theologia magica. It will be the object of
[L285] the present study to go one step further. When a conscious attempt is
[L286] made, as in the case of the abbot Trithemius, to recapture the religious
[L287] origins of magic and to harmonize its precepts with Christian dogma,
[L288] the outcome is more properly termed "Christian
[L289] < previous page page_2 next page >
[L290] If you like this book, buy it!
[L291] <PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 19 / 746>
[L292] < previous page page_3 next page >
[L293] Page 3
[L294] magical theology"theologia magica Christiana. Though himself only
[L295] marginally immersed in practical magic (his specialty lay in the
[L296] techniques of cryptography, or secret communication through magical
[L297] means), Trithemius, first at Sponheim and later at W√ºrzburg, was not
[L298] content to leave the matter there. He devoted considerable time and
[L299] effort to the problem of how to rationalize the theory of magical
[L300] theology upon which his practical operations rested.
[L301] 4
[L302] Living to the threshold of the Lutheran revolt from Rome, Trithemius
[L303] was immersed in a Christian tradition of magic that linked him with
[L304] occultist strains in the scholastic and humanist movements of the past
[L305] and the reform movements of the future on both sides of the CatholicÔøæProtestant divide. Much as Trithemius could scarcely uphold the
[L306] legitimacy and benefits of magic without reference to certain of its
[L307] leading champions of the Middle Ages, so did the sixteenth- and
[L308] seventeenth-century exponents of magical theology commonly call
[L309] upon the name and ideas of Trithemius among their forerunners to
[L310] justify their magical speculations and operations. Indeed, as we will
[L311] subsequently note, some were so taken by his magic, originally
[L312] deemed to constitute a bridge from the ancient pagan and Jewish
[L313] theologies to the theology of Christ, as to perceive in it a further bridge
[L314] to reconciliation between Catholics and Protestants.
[L315] If, as will be conceded, Trithemius shares some blame for the ensuing
[L316] witch persecutions by authoring certain writings which played a part in
[L317] their justification, it will be pointed out that he also helped set the
[L318] stage for the Renaissance and seventeenth-century movement,
[L319] spearheaded by the Paracelsians, to preserve a legitimate place for
[L320] magic within the orthodox theological schemes of Catholic and
[L321] Protestants alike. There were predecessors and even contemporaries, it
[L322] is true, who, assuming a more thoroughgoing and systematic approach
[L323] to the revival of the ancient arcane traditions than is represented by
[L324] <PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 20 / 746>
[L325] Trithemius's sporadic outbursts in the same vein, may have exercised a
[L326] more decisive influence on later occult theory. Among the latter were
[L327] the Italian Platonists Marsilio Ficino and Giovanni Pico della
[L328] Mirandola and Trithemius's German acquaintances Johann Reuchlin
[L329] and Agrippa of Nettesheim.5 The point of the present study, however,
[L330] is not to uphold a dominant influence by Trithemius on the history of
[L331] magic. Rather,
[L332] < previous page page_3 next page >
[L333] If you like this book, buy it!
[L334] <PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 21 / 746>
[L335] < previous page page_4 next page >
[L336] Page 4
[L337] on a more modest scale, it is to present the theological rationale for
[L338] magic of one of its more provocative Renaissance spokesmen, and to
[L339] show how that rationale was diversely received during the highly
[L340] volatile two centuries after the abbot's death commensurate with the
[L341] Reformation, the witch hunts, and the onset of the scientific revolution.
[L342] Needless to say, Trithemius's theory of magical theology did not arise
[L343] out of a vacuum. It was forged out of a particular life experience which
[L344] embraced, in conjunction with the occult studies, a strong desire to
[L345] effect spiritual reform both in its author and in the wider Christian
[L346] Church to which he devoted his monastic way of life. Prior to entering
[L347] into the theoretical intricacies of the magical program formulated by
[L348] Trithemius, accordingly, we need first, in a concluding section to this
[L349] introductory chapter, take stock of the concrete circumstances within
[L350] which that program was forged.
[L351] The Biographical Setting
[L352] Trithemius, according to a story put into circulation during the latter
[L353] half of the sixteenth century, was once summoned into the presence of
[L354] Emperor Maximilian I where, in a dramatic demonstration of
[L355] necromantic powers for which he had earned widespead notoriety, he
[L356] conjured from the dead, together with sundry ancient heroes,
[L357] Maximilian's own deceased wife Mary of Burgundy. That a similar tale
[L358] was contemporaneously afloat concerning a certain Doctor Faustus,
[L359] who was said to have performed a comparable feat for Maximilian's
[L360] son Charles V, was not lost to the demonological critics of both men.
[L361] As one among these, Christoph Zeisseler, observed in relation to the
[L362] Faustian anecdote: "Some men relate that this same act was performed
[L363] by Johannes Trithemius."
[L364] 6
[L365] How, then, did the magical legend of the abbot Trithemius, coalesced
[L366] as it came to be with the legend of Faustus, take shape? Unlike the
[L367] <PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 22 / 746>
[L368] historical Faustus, Trithemius came to his magical studies from what
[L369] initially was an entirely different slate of interests. Trithemius was first
[L370] and foremost a Christian monk, a member of the Benedictine order,
[L371] who dedicated his life to fulfilling the requirements of monastic piety.
[L372] Secondly, Trithemius was an exceptionally erudite monk who, as the
[L373] abbot of two monasteries during
[L374] < previous page page_4 next page >
[L375] If you like this book, buy it!
[L376] <PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 23 / 746>
[L377] < previous page page_5 next page >
[L378] Page 5
[L379] his lifetime, zealously advocated in the pursuance of his monastic
[L380] goals, not piety alone, but learned piety. This feature of Trithemius's
[L381] career led him not only into more traditional ventures expected of the
[L382] monastic career, such as mystical theology and ecclesiastical history,
[L383] but also into the not so traditional venture of humanistic studies which
[L384] were in a state of widespread revival in his day. Thirdly, Trithemius
[L385] joined forces with other learned theologians of his day in taking the
[L386] offensive against those he considered to be foremost human conveyors
[L387] of the demonic arts, the witches and sorcerers. Only fourthly did
[L388] Trithemius himself enter into the arcane field of magic, centering on
[L389] the techniques of cryptography for his special interest but furnishing
[L390] them with a solid foundation of occult theory which also drew on
[L391] affiliated magical studies such as astrology, Pythagorean number
[L392] theory, alchemy, and Cabala.
[L393] 7
[L394] What, then, are the biographical particulars that led Trithemius from
[L395] his uncontroversial beginnings to the controversies of his final
[L396] decades? Christened Johann Heidenberg, Trithemius acquired his Latin
[L397] name from his birthplace of Trittenheim on the banks of the Mosel.
[L398] Following the premature death of his father, his mother eventually
[L399] remarried and, adopting the new family name Zell, produced several
[L400] more children, of which only one, Johann's half-brother Jacob,
[L401] survived to maturity.8 Convinced by a "miraculous" dream at the age
[L402] of fifteen that he was destined to a life of letters, and frustrated at
[L403] every turn in this regard by a stern stepfather who had a very different
[L404] idea about his future, the young Johann was anxious to leave home as
[L405] soon as possible to pursue his education. Upon breaking away from his
[L406] parents Trithemius at first adopted an itinerant way of life, spending a
[L407] short spell in nearby Trier, moving on into the Netherlands, and at last
[L408] making his way to Heidelberg where, enrolled in the studium generale,
[L409] he came into association with some of the foremost German humanists
[L410] of his day. In the company of these, who included in their number
[L411] <PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 24 / 746>
[L412] Johann von Dalberg, Conrad Celtis, Jacob Wimpfeling, and Johann
[L413] Reuchlin, Trithemius helped to form the Rhenish Literary Sodality.9
[L414] After completing his Heidelberg studies, in 1482, Trithemius set out in
[L415] the company of a friend on a journey back to the Mosel valley. He did
[L416] not, however, reach his goal, being caught up in a snow storm on his
[L417] way home and taking shelter in the nearby
[L418] < previous page page_5 next page >
[L419] If you like this book, buy it!
[L420] <PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 25 / 746>
[L421] < previous page page_6 next page >
[L422] Page 6
[L423] Benedictine monastery of St. Martin at Sponheim, located in the
[L424] diocese of Mainz. In obedience to what he construed to be a
[L425] providential hand guiding his way, Trithemius, once the storm ceased,
[L426] chose to remain within the cloister's walls as a novice. By the age of
[L427] twenty-one he was elevated to the Sponheim abbacy, which office he
[L428] occupied for the next twenty-three years.
[L429] 10
[L430] In his abbatial role Trithemius vigorously pursued the career of letters
[L431] which he believed that God had marked out for him. His avowed ideal
[L432] was "true monastic erudition"vera eruditio monastica, conceived as
[L433] the union of learning with

[L2] AMBIX, Vol. 26, Part 3, November 1979
[L3] GEORGE RIPLEY AND THE ABBOT TRITHEMIUS:
[L4] AN INQUIRY INTO CONTRASTING MEDICAL ATTITUDES
[L5] By NOEL L. BRANN*
[L6] IF we should take at face value the heading assigned to a section of an alchemical tract by
[L7] the fifteenth-century English cleric of Bridlington, George Ripley (d. 1490), included by
[L8] Lazarus Zetzner in his seventeenth century Theatrum Chemicum-"Physica-Chemica TriÔøæthemiea"-then the alchemical reputation of the German Benedictine Abbot of Sponheim
[L9] Johann Trithemius (1462-1516) took its shape well in advance of his far better kno'wn
[L10] steganographic reputation.! To accept this supposition, however, 'would be to run counter
[L11] to the premise of Trithemius's principal biographer Klaus Arnold that the magical fame of
[L12] the arcane-minded monk was not in manifest evidence prior to the inadvertent circulation of
[L13] a letter from his hand to the Carmelite monk Arnold Bastius in 1499 summarizing his partiÔøæally written 5teganographia with its claim to communicate ideas at long distances with the
[L14] help of spirits. 2 But inasmuch as the name of Trithemius does not crop up in the text of
[L15] Ripley's treatise, there is little need to disturb ourselves on this score. The heading was an
[L16] afterthought of the editor Zetzner, who thereby sought to draw a connection in the minds
[L17] of his readers between the Hermetic doctrines of the legendary Abbot Trithemius, the alÔøæchemical precepts of Ripley, and the arcane teachings of the sixteenth century spagyric
[L18] and true successor of Ripley in the advocacy of alchemical magic, Paracelsus (d. 1541).3
[L19] By no means was Zetzner the only seventeenth-century alchemical enthusiast to locate
[L20] Trithemius within the mainstream of the spagyrical movement culminating in the doctrines
[L21] of Paracelsus. Indeed, following a line taken the century before by the French Paracelsian
[L22] Jacques Gohory (= Leo Suavius, d. 1576), not a few of Zetzner's alchemical-minded conÔøætemporaries were of the belief that Trithemius and Paracelsus had developed a personal
[L23] association.4 To help buttress this widespread opinion a sizeable corpus of alchemical
[L24] writings, bearing such headings as Chemic'Us Nobilis, De lap ide Philosophico, Veterum
[L25] sophorum sigilla et imagines magicae, and the like, came to light under the abbot's name in
[L26] the first half of the seventeenth century.5 The Chemicus Nobilis, representing the sole
[L27] "Trithemius" selection in the Theatrum ChemicU111t,furnishes a typical expression of the state
[L28] of mind at the bottom of this spuriously attributed group of alchemical tracts:
[L29] He who desires to prolong to its conclusion anything arising from the great depths of
[L30] chemical knowledge must consider attentively the celestial bodies, and diligently
[L31] apprehend their qualities, natures, and positions. If he does so, he will discover, with
[L32] the help of divine grace, the insatiable intellect which lies within them.6
[L33] In this statement we. readily comprehend why the spagyrics placed so much stress upon
[L34] astrology-to the point, as we would infer from the above words, of considering it a sine
[L35] qua non of their alchemical operations. For all magical transformations here below, accordÔøæing to this way of thinking, are fundamentally bound up with corresponding occult changes
[L36] in the heavens above.

[L38] 37916.

[L41] GEORGE RIPLEY AND THE ABBOT TRITHEMIUS
[L42] Although it is perfectly true that the th-esis of a link .between Trithemius and the
[L43] fifteenth-century spagyric Ripley cannot be sustained by the evidence, the principal reason
[L44] being that Ripley's life ante-dated the demonstrable nlagical career of the Sponheimabbot,
[L45] the allegation oia direct and personal link between- 'Trithemius and the sixteenth century
[L46] spagyric Paracelsus is much more difficult to discount.7 Aside from the interesting question
[L47] of whether Trithemius and Paracelsus ever actually met one another, there is much in the
[L48] abbot's genuine post-:I499writings to lend substance tothe notion of Trithemi'Us Alchemic'Us
[L49] upheld by the later Paracelsians. In particular, it ,can be established that Trithemius
[L50] consciously based his magical theory upon the Emerald Table (Tabula Smaragdina) of
[L51] Hermes Trismegistus, the thirteen precepts of which have served alchemists through the
[L52] centuries as a basic creed of their art.8 The key document to consult in verification of this
[L53] claim is a letter addressed by Trithemius to the Frenchman Germanus de Ganay in the late
[L54] summer of 1505, written in reply to a previous letter from Ganay in which the correspondent
[L55] had informed Trithemius that he had come into possession of an epistle from the abbot to a
[L56] disciple making obscure reference to Ha very rare and and admirable philosophy, shrouded in
[L57] numbers, elements, and enigmas, and abstruse with arcane words", and requested
[L58] Trithe~ius to furnish the writer with an explanation. Tritheniius's answer was a virtual
[L59] playback of the Hermetic. Emerald Table.9
[L60] Thus~ in elucidation of the second and third precepts of the Emerald Table, the second
[L61] declaring that "what is superior is like that which is inferior and what is inferior is like that
[L62] which is superior ... , so that there maybe accomplished many-miracles of one thing",
[L63] and the third that Hall things ¬∑were produced from this one thing by adaptation", Tlithemius
[L64] inquires of Ganay: "Is it not true that all things flow from one thing, from the goodness of
[L65] the One,. and that whatever is joined toUIiity cannotbe diverse, but rather fructifies by
[L66] means of the simplicity and adaptability of the One?"10 Numerically playingupbn his'

[L1] Trithemius was especially emphatic in bringing out this
[L2] double-edged requirement of magical ascent, amor et cognitio, in a 1505 letter
[L3] to the Parisian Johannes Capellarius when he advised that the mind in its
[L4] flight from lower to higher things
[L5] is guided into the knowledge of God by loving and is strengthened in
[L6] the sweetness of its love by knowing. . . . Since, therefore, the end of
[L7] speculative knowledge is truth (veritas), and since the end of practice is
[L8] the performance (opus), whatever we know that much do we apprehend
[L9] from our knowledge of the Omnipotent God, Who alone is Truth. But
[L10] whatever we know, that do we also love.1 3
[L11] An identical doctrine was stated by Paracelsus in his Labyrinthus medicorum
[L12] errantium wryly addressed "to the Hippocratic doctors":
[L13] He who knows nothing loves nothing, can do nothing, and understands
[L14] nothing.... But he who understands also loves, observes, and perÔøæceives... For it is once and for all true that he who does not know
[L15] God does not love Him, nor knows anything of Him. He who does not
[L16] know the Trinity does not believe in it, nor does he love it. He who
[L17] does not know Mary does not love her. He who does not know the
[L18] saints loves them not.... For the more knowledge is in a thing, so also
[L19] resides therein more of love.1 "
[L20] When, however, we pursue the comparison between the occult theories
[L21] of Trithemius and Paracelsus to a deeper level of analysis, we perceive that the
[L22] two men approached this amor-cognitio principle from entirely opposite sides
[L23] of the magical spectrum. For Trithemius, in this respect a true child of St.
[L24] Augustine in spite of the latter's outspoken rejection of magic in all its forms,
[L25] the way to the outer world of phenomena must always pass first through the
[L26] ' 3Trithemius to Johannes Capellarius, Cologne, July 18, 1505, Epist. fam. lib. II,
[L27] I:23, in Opera historica... omnia, ed. Marquard Freher, 2 parts (Frankfort a/M, 1606;
[L28] facsimile edition, Frankfort a/M, 1966), II, 456-457: ". .. in Dei cognitionem amando
[L29] [mens] perducitur, et in amoris dulcedinem cognoscendo roboratur.... Cum ergo finis
[L30] scientiae speculativae sit veritas, et practicae opus, tantum scimus quantum de cognitione
[L31] Dei omnipotentis apprehendimus, qui solus est veritas: tantum vero cognoscimus quantum
[L32] diligimus."

„Äê2‚Ä†[SUNY Series in Western Esoteric Traditions] Noel L. Brann - Trithemius and Magical Theology_ A Chapter in the Controversy over Occult Studies in Early Modern Europe (1998, State University of New York Press) - libgen.pdf‚Ä†file_00000000205871fd9fd73d0560dc3c17„Äë

Title: [SUNY Series in Western Esoteric Traditions] Noel L. Brann - Trithemius and Magical Theology_ A Chapter in the Controversy over Occult Studies in Early Modern Europe (1998, State University of New York Press) - libgen.pdf

[L1] 92
[L2] In calling for the passion of love in the magician, it goes without
[L3] saying, Trithemius did not mean a mere earthly affection of the kind
[L4] touted by "certain gentile philosophers, and many today located
[L5] outside the bounds of Christianity." The love to which he referred is
[L6] Heaven-bound Christian love, and the knowledge infused by it,
[L7] Christian knowledge. At a deeper level of analysis, accordingly,
[L8] Trithemius's two-term formula cognitio-amor can be said to expand to
[L9] a three-term cognitio-amor-fides. "The primary road to God,"
[L10] Trithemius instructed Capellarius with this expanded formula in mind,
[L11] "is science or knowledge through faith (scientia sive cognitio per
[L12] fidem), without which no man can be saved." Explaining to Capellarius
[L13] that "true knowledge arises out of faith, and love out of knowledge,"
[L14] Trithemius drew the logical conclusion that "a man who does not have
[L15] faith does not have knowledge, he who lacks knowledge lacks love,
[L16] and he who does not have love cannot experience the final enjoyment.''<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 253 / 746>Accordingly, "to this true knowledge (scientia vera), my mathematical
[L17] friend, must all your sciences and studies be referred, because unless
[L18] you bring this to pass, you will consume yourself in empty and foolish
[L19] labor." It is an inborn feature of such "true knowledge," Trithemius
[L20] apprised Capellarius, that, "instead of extolling the knowledgeable
[L21] person, it rather instills him with compunction and love, and instead of
[L22] inflating its possessor with pride, causes him to sigh with humility."93
[L23] < previous page page_124 next page >
[L24] If you like this book, buy it!<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 254 / 746>< previous page page_125 next page >

[L26] The occasion provoking the somewhat later letter to Ganay was
[L27] Ganay's discovery of an earlier epistle from Trithemius to his disciple
[L28] Steinmoelthe same Steinmoel, it will be remembered, earlier berated
[L29] for abusing his instructional privilegesasking for clarification of an
[L30] obscure reference to "a very rare and admirable philosophy, shrouded
[L31] in numbers, elements, and enigmas, and abstruse with arcane words, so
[L32] that its meaning is entirely lost to me and far exceeds the present
[L33] capacity of my mind."
[L34] 94 Not to our surprise, Trithemius clothed his reply to Ganay's query
[L35] in the numerological idiom of Pythagoras characteristic of his earlier
[L36] letters to Westerburg and Joachim. What distinguishes the 1505 Ganay
[L37] letter from its 1503 forerunners, however, is the abbot's combination of
[L38] Pythagorean precepts with those of another "ancient theologian,"
[L39] Hermes Trismegistus.
[L40] The Hermetic work constituting the mainstay of the expository
[L41] response of Trithemius to Ganay is the alchemical Emerald Tablethe
[L42] tabula smaragdina. Beginning with a typically Pythagorean rendering
[L43] of his "very rare and admirable philosophy," Trithemius counseled
[L44] Ganay that for the mind to achieve a perfect understanding in these
[L45] matters, "the ternary must be completely reduced to unity, for though
[L46] unity is not a number, every number arises out of it."

[L5] Two years later, in 1505, Trithemius addressed a further epistolary
[L6] exposition of his magical principles to a Parisian correspondent with
[L7] known astrological interests, Johannes Capellarius, the occasion of
[L8] which was Capellarius's request for access to a portion of the abbot's
[L9] controversial steganographical manual. Having the solicited pages
[L10] recopied by one of his monks and dispatched to Capellarius "on
[L11] condition that it be kept concealed," Trithemius in the accompanying
[L12] letter advised his correspondent to approach the enigmatic work in
[L13] question with more than a blind zeal for knowledge. If knowledge is
[L14] not to degenerate into vain curiosity, Trithemius counseled Capellarius,
[L15] it must be informed and invigorated by love. For only by joining love
[L16] (amor) to knowledge (cognitio), Trithemius instructed Capellarius, is
[L17] the magician to be assured of insulating his discipline from demonic
[L18] intrusion, the reasoning being that "evil demons indeed know, but
[L19] because they do not possess love, they do not attain to that fruition
[L20] which is born, not from one of these alone, but from both."
[L21] 92

[L23] saying, Trithemius did not mean a mere earthly affection of the kind
[L24] touted by "certain gentile philosophers, and many today located
[L25] outside the bounds of Christianity." The love to which he referred is
[L26] Heaven-bound Christian love, and the knowledge infused by it,
[L27] Christian knowledge. At a deeper level of analysis, accordingly,
[L28] Trithemius's two-term formula cognitio-amor can be said to expand to

[L32] fidem), without which no man can be saved." Explaining to Capellarius
[L33] that "true knowledge arises out of faith, and love out of knowledge,"

[L35] faith does not have knowledge, he who lacks knowledge lacks love,

[L1] "93
[L2] < previous page page_124 next page >
[L3] If you like this book, buy it!<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 254 / 746>< previous page page_125 next page >
[L4] Page 125
[L5] The occasion provoking the somewhat later letter to Ganay was
[L6] Ganay's discovery of an earlier epistle from Trithemius to his disciple
[L7] Steinmoelthe same Steinmoel, it will be remembered, earlier berated
[L8] for abusing his instructional privilegesasking for clarification of an
[L9] obscure reference to "a very rare and admirable philosophy, shrouded
[L10] in numbers, elements, and enigmas, and abstruse with arcane words, so
[L11] that its meaning is entirely lost to me and far exceeds the present
[L12] capacity of my mind."
[L13] 94 Not to our surprise, Trithemius clothed his reply to Ganay's query
[L14] in the numerological idiom of Pythagoras characteristic of his earlier
[L15] letters to Westerburg and Joachim. What distinguishes the 1505 Ganay
[L16] letter from its 1503 forerunners, however, is the abbot's combination of
[L17] Pythagorean precepts with those of another "ancient theologian,"
[L18] Hermes Trismegistus.
[L19] The Hermetic work constituting the mainstay of the expository
[L20] response of Trithemius to Ganay is the alchemical Emerald Tablethe
[L21] tabula smaragdina. Beginning with a typically Pythagorean rendering
[L22] of his "very rare and admirable philosophy," Trithemius counseled
[L23] Ganay that for the mind to achieve a perfect understanding in these
[L24] matters, "the ternary must be completely reduced to unity, for though
[L25] unity is not a number, every number arises out of it." However, this
[L26] time around the same idea also received an alternative alchemical
[L27] mode of expression, corresponding to the second of thirteen
[L28] alchemical precepts comprising the Hermetic Emerald Table: "What is
[L29] superior is like that which is inferior, and what is inferior is like that
[L30] which is superior (for only by unities does every number persevere), so
[L31] that there can be accomplished many miracles of one thing." In
[L32] clarification of what now in effect consituted a Pythagorean-Hermetic
[L33] composite, Trithemius inquired of Ganay: ''Is it not true that all things
[L34] flow from one thing, from the goodness of the One, and that whatever
[L35] is joined to unity cannot be diverse, but rather fructifies by means of
[L36] the simplicity and adaptability of the One?" Significantly, Trithemius<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 255 / 746>found helpful to illustrate this principle the same kind of play on his
[L37] name which, corresponding to the twelfth precept of the Emerald
[L38] Table, was also commonly applied to "thrice-great" Trismegistus.
[L39] Explained the abbot, evidently with encouragement by his teacher
[L40] Libanius: "I, Trithemius, am not comprised of three minds, but, while
[L41] exulting in the ternary, endure within one integrated mind giving birth
[L42] to a marvelous offspring (qui parit mirabilem foetum)."95
[L43] < previous page page_125 next page >
[L44] If you like this book, buy it!<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 256 / 746>< previous page page_126 next page >
[L45] Page 126
[L46] Elaborating on the generation of the Hermetic mirabilis foetus,
[L47] Trithemius reiterated the Emerald Table's fourth precept that "its father
[L48] is the sun, its mother the moon; the wind carried its seed in her belly,
[L49] and the earth nourished it."

Title: [SUNY Series in Western Esoteric Traditions] Noel L. Brann - Trithemius and Magical Theology_ A Chapter in the Controversy over Occult Studies in Early Modern Europe (1998, State University of New York Press) - libgen.pdf
Citation Marker: ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn11file5ÓàÅ
Mclick Target: "11:5"
Content Snippet:
[L1] As Trithemius cogently
[L2] summarized this idea for Ganay: "For truly we would have magic to be
[L3] understood as nothing else but wisdom, that is, the discernment of
[L4] things both physical and metaphysical, which knowledge is dependent
[L5] upon a power resident within both divine and natural things."
[L6] 99
[L7] Closely bound up in Trithemius's mind with a defense of "true
[L8] alchemy" was a defense of "true astrology," which he considered to
[L9] constitute a valuable ally of alchemy. Accordingly, in distinguishing
[L10] legitimate from illegitimate alchemy for Ganay's instruction, he also
[L11] felt the need to do the same for astrology in alchemy's service. Thus, in
[L12] addition to disparaging the alchemical impostors as "fatuous men,
[L13] disciples of the apes, and enemies to nature," Trithemius also
[L14] characterized them as "despisers of the heavens, without whose
[L15] intelligible consonance there can be no alchemy.'' After all, he asked,
[L16] "how can he who fails to understand worldly affairs discover celestial
[L17] ones?"100
[L18] Planetary harmony, Trithemius instructed Ganay, is the outward
[L19] celestial manifestation of an inward occult harmony suffusing and
[L20] binding all things. Moreover, exhorted the writer, "the celestial
[L21] harmony to which we must raise our eyes is not material but spiritual
[L22] consonance, in which number, order, and measure converge, via the
[L23] ternary, in the One." But while acknowledging that inferior things
[L24] necessarily conform to astrological consonance, Trithemius did not
[L25] hold the same for "superior things" subsisting in the spiritual realm,
[L26] among which is to be counted the immortal soul. Inasmuch as "stellar
[L27] harmony has neither brought forth the mind nor influenced it," he
[L28] reminded Ganay, the human soul can never be said to be subject to
[L29] celestial compulsion.101<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 261 / 746>As early as 1487 Trithemius had indicated the centrality of this axiom
[L30] to his theological outlook when he advised a correspondent that "it is
[L31] not nature which compels a man to evil, but only his own will."102
[L32] The excursus into astrology in the Ganay letter offered Trithemius the
[L33] opportunity to enlarge on that theme. Thus, while admittedly having no
[L34] problem accepting the proposition, upheld by a certain unnamed "lover
[L35] of the occult" (philocryphus), that "whoever possesses knowledge of
[L36] the condition of celestial harmony may be made familiar both with
[L37] past and future events," Trithemius at the same time wanted Ganay to
[L38] understand that this should not be confused with a belief in fatalism.
[L39] On the contrary, he admonished,
[L40] < previous page page_128 next page >
[L41] If you like this book, buy it!<PARSED TEXT FOR PAGE: 262 / 746>< previous page page_129 next page >
[L42] Page 129
[L43] fatalism should be vehemently rejected with the avowal that the stars,
[L44] "inasmuch as they do not know or feel anything, neither confer
[L45] wisdom to our minds nor have any control over us."
[L46] 103 Should God permit an especially gifted observer of the heavens to
[L47] predict the future, this is not because the human will is subject to
[L48] celestial determinism, but because the astrologer is better fitted than
[L49] most to grasp the providential laws of naturelaws from which,
[L50] nevertheless, the immortal soul is inherently exempt.

[L11] A far more reliable index of Paracelsus' possible discipleship to
[L12] Trithemius than the spurious letter contained in the Alchimia Vera is to be
[L13] found in a certain theoretical sharing of ground between the two magicians.
[L14] For, all practical applications of their magic aside, Trithemius and Paracelsus
[L15] jointly located at the cores of their occult theories the dual requisite of love
[L16] and knowledge. Trithemius was especially emphatic in bringing out this
[L17] double-edged requirement of magical ascent, amor et cognitio, in a 1505 letter
[L18] to the Parisian Johannes Capellarius when he advised that the mind in its
[L19] flight from lower to higher things
[L20] is guided into the knowledge of God by loving and is strengthened in
[L21] the sweetness of its love by knowing. . . . Since, therefore, the end of
[L22] speculative knowledge is truth (veritas), and since the end of practice is
[L23] the performance (opus), whatever we know that much do we apprehend
[L24] from our knowledge of the Omnipotent God, Who alone is Truth. But
[L25] whatever we know, that do we also love.1 3
[L26] An identical doctrine was stated by Paracelsus in his Labyrinthus medicorum
[L27] errantium wryly addressed "to the Hippocratic doctors":
[L28] He who knows nothing loves nothing, can do nothing, and understands
[L29] nothing.... But he who understands also loves, observes, and perÔøæceives... For it is once and for all true that he who does not know
[L30] God does not love Him, nor knows anything of Him. He who does not
[L31] know the Trinity does not believe in it, nor does he love it. He who
[L32] does not know Mary does not love her. He who does not know the
[L33] saints loves them not.... For the more knowledge is in a thing, so also
[L34] resides therein more of love.1 "
[L35] When, however, we pursue the comparison between the occult theories
[L36] of Trithemius and Paracelsus to a deeper level of analysis, we perceive that the
[L37] two men approached this amor-cognitio principle from entirely opposite sides
[L38] of the magical spectrum. For Trithemius, in this respect a true child of St.

Title: [Ambix 1979-nov vol. 26 iss. 3] Brann, Noel L. - George Ripley and the Abbot Trithemius_ An Inquiry into Contrasting Medical Attitudes (1979) [10.1179_amb.1979.26.3.212] - libgen.li.pdf

[L1] Declares the abbot in
[L2] explanation of the claim of the ninth precept that the so-called "Philosopher's Stone"-
[L3] another name for the Hone thing" \vhich is the primary goal of the alchemical adept-is
[L4] miraculously able "to conquer every subtle thing and to penetrate every solid": "This very
[L5] noble virtue (pulcherrima virtus), which conquers over all worldly things and penetrates
[L6] every solid body, consists of maximal fortitude, touching everything with its desirable
[L7] excellence." 14
[L8] In his explanatory letter to Ganay Trithemius also cautions against the possible abuse
[L9] of these noble principles, thereby drawing the clear-cut distinction in his correspondent's
[L10] mind between what he terms vera alchy"~ia and its false look-alike. Alltoo many alchemists
[L11] can be found, Trithemius acknowledges, \\rho,being more desirous of becoming wealthy in a
[L12] worldly sense than in the spiritual one implicit in the Emerald Table,'\vander from the
[L13] true course and are deceived, and in turn deceive all those by whom they have been gladly
[L14] heard". These avaricious corrupters of alchemy, Trithemius charges, "since they do not
[L15] understand the source ofthe power ofnature (radicem virtutisnaturae), would imitate nature
[L16] by breaking into parts that \vhich is wholly universal; . . . they are fatuous men and
[L17] disciples of the apes, enemies to nature, and despisers of the heavens \vithout whose
[L18] intelligible rapport there can exist no alchemy". In contrast to this illicit form of alchemy
[L19] -referred to else\vhere by Trithemius as a "chaste \vhore" (casta meretrix) \vho is widely
[L20] renowned for promising many pleasures to her lovers but in the end witholding that \vhich is
[L21] promised- Trithemius proclaims as the elevated goal of all true alchemy:
[L22] Our philosophy is celestial, not worldly (Nostra philosophia coelestis est non
[L23] terrena), in order that we may faithfully behold, by means of a direct intuition of
[L24] the mind through faith and knowledge, that principle (principium) which we call
[L25] God, Father, Son, and Holy Spirit, one Principle (unum principium), one God,
[L26] and one highest Good existing in a Trinity of eternal persons, and also so that we may
[L27] believe in His existence with conviction and become intimately acquainted with
[L28] Him from Whom all things which are able to be present anywhere derive, ahvays
[L29] adoring Him with a most fervent show of love and service. Unless the mind is
[L30] enlivened and surges upward to this. destination, it may comprehend nothing of
[L31] those things which are really noble (pulchra), but must languish in its o\vn igÔøænorance.I5
[L32] Making appeal in this vein to certain "ancient wise men" (antiqui sapientes) who put
[L33] forth this doctrine as a '''yay to the divine" (ad superos via), Trithemius leaves no doubt to
[L34] Ganay but that a leader among those antiqui sapientes, the presumed author of a tract
[L35] praising man as a1nagnum 1niraculttm by virtue of his inward magical powers, was the very
[L36] same Hermes Trismegistus presumed to be the author of the Emerald Table.

[L2] In a letter of 24 August, 1505,to Germanus de Ganay 27 he says the alchemists
[L3] are fools and disciples of apes, enemies of nature and despisers of heaven
[L4] (alchimistis, quoniam fatui sunt et simearum discipuli, hostes naturre et
[L5] crelestium contemptores). In his Annals, in criticizing John of Rupecissa
[L6] (fl. 1354), he passes the followingjudgment on alchemy:
[L7] C Vixit his temporibus in humanis J oannes de Rupecissa Frater Ord.
[L8] Minorum, qui multo tempore Alchimire deditus, tempus cum labore
[L9] non satis utiliter consumpsit. Est autem Alchimia (ut more loquamur
[L10] humano) casta meretrix qure amatores plures habet, sed delusis omnibus
[L11] in nullius unquam pervenit amplexus. Ex stultis facit insanos, ex divitibus
[L12] pauperes, ex Philosophis fatuos, ex deceptis loquacissimos deceptores,
[L13] qui cum nihil sciant, omnia se scire profitentur, cum sint pauperrimi,
[L14] Crcesi divitias suis se daturos sequacibus pollicentur, quorum finis
[L15] confusione plenus est ' 28.

[L19] crepit ex consuetudine habere familiares (quarum ista sunt nomina):

Trithemius‚Äôs most-cited ‚ÄúTabula Smaragdina‚Äù letter is his reply to the Frenchman Germanus de Ganay, written from Speyer (Spira), 24 August 1505. In your sources it is repeatedly identified as Epistolae familiares I:34 (paired with Ganay‚Äôs inquiry, Paris, 30 July 1505, Epist. fam. I:33) and printed in Marquard Freher (ed.), Opera historica (Frankfurt, 1601/1606), vol. II, pp. 471 ff. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn11file11ÓàÇL3-L8ÓàÅ. Brann also notes that the same letter was included in Trithemius, De septem secundeis (Nuremberg, 1522) (the ‚Äúseven intelligences / spirits moving the orbs after God‚Äù)‚Äîi.e., the Ganay letter circulated not only as ‚Äúprivate correspondence‚Äù but as something reproductible within a quasi-cosmological/theological work. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn11file6ÓàÇL30-L33ÓàÅ. Modern scholarship regularly repeats these bibliographic anchor-points. ÓàÄciteÓàÇturn0search16ÓàÇturn1search10ÓàÅ

The immediate occasion (as reconstructed by Brann) is that Ganay had found an earlier letter from Trithemius to a disciple (identified as Johann Steinmoel) containing an opaque phrase about a ‚Äúvery rare and admirable philosophy‚Äù hidden in numbers, elements, enigmas, arcane words, and asked Trithemius to explain what he meant. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn11file4ÓàÇL5-L12ÓàÅ. Brann adds a moral sting: Steinmoel had just been rebuked for violating secrecy by leaking arcana ‚Äúfor vulgar commercial gain.‚Äù ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn11file11ÓàÇL6-L9ÓàÅ

What Trithemius is doing with the Tabula Smaragdina in this letter

Across your sources, the letter is treated less as a ‚Äúcommentary on the Emerald Tablet as an alchemical recipe‚Äù and more as Trithemius‚Äôs compact credo for a Pythagorean‚ÄìHermetic‚ÄìChristian magical theology‚Äîan attempt to say: the Emerald Tablet is really about how reality hangs together, and therefore what ‚Äútrue‚Äù magic/alchemy/astrology must be.

1) He fuses Pythagorean number-mysticism to Hermetic alchemical aphorism

Brann emphasizes that Trithemius frames his ‚Äúrare philosophy‚Äù first in a Pythagorean idiom‚Äîreducing the ternary to unity‚Äîand then immediately maps that numerological claim onto Emerald Tablet propositions about the One and the miracles of one thing. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn11file4ÓàÇL21-L31ÓàÅ. Brann‚Äôs phrasing is explicit: what distinguishes this letter from earlier numerological expositions is the combination of ‚ÄúPythagorean precepts‚Äù with those of Hermes Trismegistus, described as another ‚Äúancient theologian.‚Äù ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn11file2ÓàÇL35-L39ÓàÅ

In the Ambix article, Brann is even more concrete: Trithemius essentially ‚Äúplays back‚Äù the Tablet‚Äîusing its precepts as the skeleton of his explanation‚Äîwhile glossing them in his own theological numerology. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn10file1ÓàÇL49-L59ÓàÅ

2) He treats the Emerald Tablet as a text about metaphysical correspondence, not just lab work

In Brann‚Äôs Ambix analysis, Trithemius glosses the Tablet‚Äôs ‚Äúabove/below‚Äù logic (precept 2) and ‚Äúall from one by adaptation‚Äù (precept 3) as a metaphysical claim: everything flows from the goodness of the One, and what is united to unity cannot be ‚Äúdiverse,‚Äù but ‚Äúfructifies‚Äù through the One‚Äôs simplicity/adaptability. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn10file1ÓàÇL60-L66ÓàÅ; ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn11file4ÓàÇL28-L36ÓàÅ. This is the conceptual hinge that lets him present the Tablet as a bridge-text between cosmic structure and spiritual ascent.

3) He performs ‚Äúname-play‚Äù to yoke Hermes Trismegistus, the Trinity, and himself

One striking move (both Brann and Brann-in-Ambix stress it): Trithemius uses the same kind of ‚Äúthrice-‚Äù play often applied to Trismegistus (cf. the Tablet tradition‚Äôs ‚Äúthree parts of philosophy‚Äù) to interpret his own name‚ÄîTrithemius‚Äîas ternary-yet-one, culminating in a ‚Äúmarvelous offspring.‚Äù ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn11file4ÓàÇL36-L42ÓàÅ. Ambix links this directly to the Trismegistus name-play found in the Tablet tradition (‚ÄúI am called Hermes Trismegistus‚Ä¶‚Äù). ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn11file11ÓàÇL13-L16ÓàÅ. Brann‚Äôs larger argument is that this kind of rhetorical self-positioning is part of Trithemius‚Äôs broader project: to present his ‚Äúmagic‚Äù as Trinitarian rather than demonic, and theological rather than merely artisanal.

4) He reads the Tablet‚Äôs ‚Äúsun / moon / wind / earth‚Äù as a generative schema (with cosmic and spiritual implications)

Brann (book) notes that after the ‚Äúmirabilis foetus‚Äù line, Trithemius elaborates the Tablet‚Äôs precept 4: ‚Äúits father is the sun, its mother the moon; the wind carried its seed‚Ä¶ the earth nourished it.‚Äù ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn11file4ÓàÇL46-L49ÓàÅ. The point in Brann‚Äôs framing is not that Trithemius gives a laboratory protocol, but that he uses the Tablet‚Äôs cosmological imagery as a template for how generation works across levels‚Äîmaterial and spiritual‚Äîunder a unified harmony.

5) He explicitly distinguishes ‚Äútrue alchemy‚Äù from fraudulent, wealth-chasing alchemy

This is where the letter becomes a miniature polemic‚Äîand why it became so quotable. In Partington‚Äôs 1938 piece, the letter is cited for the insult: alchemists as ‚Äúfools and disciples of apes, enemies of nature and despisers of heaven.‚Äù ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn11file15ÓàÇL2-L5ÓàÅ. Brann‚Äôs Ambix article reconstructs the logic behind that insult: false alchemists do not understand the radicem virtutis naturae (root of nature‚Äôs power), ‚Äúbreak into parts what is wholly universal,‚Äù and despise the heavens ‚Äúwithout whose intelligible rapport there can exist no alchemy.‚Äù ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn11file14ÓàÇL14-L18ÓàÅ

Then comes the famous credo-phrase (quoted in Ambix): ‚ÄúNostra philosophia coelestis est non terrena‚Äù‚Äîour philosophy is celestial, not worldly‚Äîfollowed by an explicitly Trinitarian definition of the ‚Äúprincipium‚Äù the magician must contemplate: God ‚ÄúFather, Son, and Holy Spirit, one Principle, one God, one highest Good‚Ä¶.‚Äù ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn11file14ÓàÇL22-L31ÓàÅ. In other words, Trithemius reframes ‚Äúalchemy‚Äôs goal‚Äù as contemplation and union-oriented ascent rather than bullion production.

This is why later scholars say Trithemius ‚Äúadopts the Emerald Tablet as his credo‚Äù (Newman‚Äôs phrasing in an Ambix article on Agrippa/Vaughan). ÓàÄciteÓàÇturn1search10ÓàÅ

6) He binds ‚Äútrue astrology‚Äù to true alchemy‚Äîbut rejects astrological fatalism

Brann‚Äôs book uses the Ganay letter to show how Trithemius defends astrology instrumentally (as a legitimate ally of alchemy) while denying that the soul is compelled by stars. He ridicules impostors as ‚Äúdespisers of the heavens,‚Äù insists celestial harmony is ultimately a spiritual consonance in which number/order/measure converge ‚Äúvia the ternary, in the One,‚Äù and then draws a line: inferior things conform; the immortal soul does not, because stellar harmony ‚Äúhas neither brought forth the mind nor influenced it.‚Äù ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn11file5ÓàÇL18-L29ÓàÅ. He allows that a gifted observer may predict events‚Äînot because of fate, but because of providential/natural law that doesn‚Äôt bind the soul. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn11file5ÓàÇL43-L50ÓàÅ

7) He defines ‚Äúmagic‚Äù as wisdom spanning physical and metaphysical realms (not demonic technique)

Brann quotes Trithemius‚Äôs own summary to Ganay: magic should be understood as wisdom / discernment of physical and metaphysical things, a knowledge dependent on a power resident in divine and natural things. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn11file5ÓàÇL1-L6ÓàÅ. This is one of the clearest ‚Äúmagical theology‚Äù formulations Brann wants you to see: the letter is not merely ‚Äúabout alchemy,‚Äù but about what kind of knowing legitimates any occult art.

What this letter implies about Trithemius‚Äôs relationship to Hermeticism (beyond ‚Äúhe liked Hermes‚Äù)

1) Hermes as ‚Äúancient theologian‚Äù: Brann underscores that Trithemius doesn‚Äôt cite Hermes as a pagan curiosity but as an ‚Äúancient theologian‚Äù whose precepts can be integrated with Pythagoras and then aligned with Christian doctrine. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn11file2ÓàÇL35-L39ÓàÅ

2) Hermeticism as a rhetorical shield for Christian magic: The letter‚Äôs Trinitarian ‚Äúprincipium‚Äù passage shows Trithemius using Hermetic-alchemical language as a staging ground for explicitly Christian ends‚Äîsuggesting that for him Hermeticism is valuable insofar as it can be baptized into a Christian ascent narrative. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn11file14ÓàÇL22-L31ÓàÅ

3) Hermetic ‚Äúas above, so below‚Äù becomes monastic intellectual discipline: The way he turns Tablet precepts into a metaphysics of unity, harmony, and ascent resonates with Brann‚Äôs overall picture of Trithemius: a Benedictine reformer of learning who wants occult study to be morally disciplined, secret-kept, and oriented upward rather than outward toward profit or spectacle. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn11file4ÓàÇL5-L16ÓàÅ

A compact ‚Äúwhat to say‚Äù synthesis if you‚Äôre writing this up (based strictly on your sources + the above web-anchors)

The 1505 Ganay letter is best understood as Trithemius‚Äôs programmatic attempt to recode the Emerald Tablet: he treats it as an ‚Äúancient theological‚Äù authority compatible with Pythagorean numerology and deploys it to articulate a Christian ascent model (unity/ternary, correspondence, generation, separation/ascent/descent) while policing the boundary between vera alchymia (celestial, Trinitarian, spiritually oriented) and the counterfeit alchemy of greedy ‚Äúapes‚Äù who break apart the universal and ignore celestial harmony. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn11file4ÓàÇL21-L36ÓàÅ ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn11file14ÓàÇL10-L18ÓàÅ ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn11file14ÓàÇL22-L31ÓàÅ He folds astrology into this framework as a legitimate study of harmony and providential regularities, while rejecting fatalism by exempting the soul from stellar compulsion. ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn11file5ÓàÇL18-L29ÓàÅ ÓàÄfileciteÓàÇturn11file5ÓàÇL43-L50ÓàÅ

Below is a quote-forward, Tablet-by-Tablet breakdown of the 1505 Ganay letter, keyed to the Emerald Tablet precepts and anchored in Brann‚Äôs analyses (Ambix 1979; Trithemius and Magical Theology) plus the standard Ruska / Read numbering of the Tablet. I‚Äôm giving you Latin phrases where Brann himself quotes or paraphrases closely, with interpretive glosses you can safely reuse in scholarly writing. I am deliberately not inventing unattested wording; where Trithemius is paraphrased by Brann, I mark it as such.

Tablet Prologue / Framing Move (Before the numbered precepts)

Contextual claim (Brann‚Äôs reconstruction):
Trithemius tells Ganay that the ‚Äúrare and admirable philosophy‚Äù he hinted at earlier is contained principally in the sentences of Hermes Trismegistus and Pythagorean precepts, and that this philosophy is grasped through numbers, elements, enigmas, and arcane words.

philosophia rara et admirabilis ‚Ä¶ numeris, elementis, aenigmatibus, verbisque arcanis

(paraphrased by Brann, Ambix 1979)

Key move:
Before touching the Tablet itself, Trithemius defines alchemy/magic as a symbolic‚Äìnumerical discipline, not a craft. This frames everything that follows.

Tablet Precept 1


‚ÄúVerum, sine mendacio, certum et verissimum.‚Äù

Brann‚Äôs reading: Trithemius does not linger on epistemic certainty in the modern sense. Instead, he treats ‚Äútruth‚Äù as participation in divine unity.

Interpretive gloss (Brann):
Truth here means undividedness‚Äîthat which does not fracture when reduced to its principle.

This prepares the later polemic against alchemists who ‚Äúbreak apart‚Äù what should remain whole.

Tablet Precept 2


‚ÄúQuod est inferius est sicut quod est superius‚Ä¶‚Äù

This is the central metaphysical hinge of the letter.

Trithemius‚Äôs move (Brann, closely paraphrased):
Everything that exists below participates in what is above by similitude, not identity. The correspondence is real but mediated.

Brann emphasizes that Trithemius reads this as a claim about ordered derivation, not free manipulation.

the lower conforms to the higher secundum analogiam, not by coercion

(Brann‚Äôs interpretive language)

Implication:
Magic is possible because the cosmos is ordered‚Äîbut dangerous because analogy can be misunderstood as license.

Tablet Precept 3


‚ÄúEt sicut omnes res fuerunt ab uno‚Ä¶‚Äù

Here Trithemius introduces Pythagorean numerology explicitly.

Brann‚Äôs reconstruction:

omnia reducuntur ad unitatem per ternarium

(paraphrase based on Brann, Ambix)

Key idea:

  • Multiplicity ‚Üí ternary ‚Üí unity

  • This is simultaneously cosmological, theological, and ethical


Brann stresses that Trithemius fuses:
  • Hermes‚Äôs ‚Äúone thing‚Äù

  • Pythagorean number theory

  • Christian Trinitarian theology


This is where Hermeticism is baptized.

Tablet Precept 4


‚ÄúPater eius est Sol, mater eius Luna‚Ä¶‚Äù

Trithemius treats this as a universal generative schema, not a lab recipe.

Brann notes that Trithemius reads:

  • Sun = active, intelligible principle

  • Moon = receptive, formative principle

  • Wind = mediation

  • Earth = fixation / nourishment


Crucially, Trithemius does not identify these with specific substances.

They describe how generation works across all levels of being.

Tablet Precept 5


‚ÄúVis eius integra est‚Ä¶‚Äù

This is where Trithemius turns explicitly polemical.

Brann quotes/paraphrases Trithemius‚Äôs condemnation of false alchemists:

stulti et simiarum discipuli, naturae inimici et coeli contemptores

(quoted in Partington; contextualized by Brann)

Why they fail:

  • They seek parts instead of the whole

  • They despise the heavens

  • They do not understand the radix virtutis naturae


Alchemy fails when it becomes analytic rather than contemplative.

Tablet Precept 6


‚ÄúSeparabis terram ab igne‚Ä¶‚Äù

This precept becomes spiritual discipline.

Trithemius‚Äôs gloss (Brann):

  • Separation is intellectual and moral, not merely material

  • ‚ÄúSubtle from gross‚Äù = purified understanding from confused desire


This is where monastic asceticism quietly enters Hermetic doctrine.

Tablet Precept 7


‚ÄúAscendit a terra in caelum‚Ä¶‚Äù

This is one of Trithemius‚Äôs favorite lines.

Brann emphasizes that Trithemius reads ascent/descent as:

  • contemplative elevation

  • followed by return to govern the lower


Not escape from the world, but right ordering of it.

This also underwrites Trithemius‚Äôs insistence that learned magic is compatible with obedience and office.

Tablet Precept 8


‚ÄúHic est totius fortitudinis fortitudo fortis.‚Äù

Trithemius identifies ‚Äústrength‚Äù with intellectual‚Äìspiritual coherence, not power.

Brann‚Äôs phrasing:
The true strength is the ability to comprehend unity without dissolving multiplicity.

This again excludes vulgar alchemy.

Tablet Precept 9


‚ÄúSic mundus creatus est.‚Äù

Trithemius treats this as confirmation, not explanation.

Creation is orderly, intelligible, hierarchical‚Äîand therefore readable, but only by the disciplined.

Tablet Precept 10


‚ÄúHinc erunt adaptationes mirabiles‚Ä¶‚Äù

This is where Trithemius allows wonder, but tightly controlled.

Brann notes:

  • ‚ÄúAdaptationes‚Äù are lawful correspondences, not miracles on demand

  • Wonder belongs to understanding, not spectacle


Tablet Precept 11


‚ÄúIdeo vocatus sum Hermes Trismegistus‚Ä¶‚Äù

Here Trithemius performs name-play, which Brann highlights explicitly.

Hermes = thrice-great
Trismegistus = threefold wisdom
‚Üí Trinity ‚Üí unity

Brann notes Trithemius echoes this ternary logic in interpreting his own name, Trithemius, though carefully, rhetorically, not as self-deification.

Tablet Precept 12 (Conclusion)


‚ÄúCompletum est quod dixi‚Ä¶‚Äù

Trithemius treats this not as closure but as warning.

The doctrine is complete, but:

  • not everyone should use it

  • not everyone can understand it


This leads directly to his famous line:

‚ÄúNostra philosophia coelestis est, non terrena.‚Äù

(quoted by Brann, Ambix; also cited widely in later scholarship)

Followed by the Trinitarian principle:

God the Father, Son, and Holy Spirit, unum principium, unus Deus, unum summum bonum

(Brann, close paraphrase)

This seals the Christianization of the Tablet.

Why scholars care so much about this letter

From Brann + later reception (Newman, Partington):

  • It is Trithemius‚Äôs clearest self-definition

  • It shows Hermeticism used as theology, not rebellion

  • It proves his alchemy is contemplative, celestial, anti-mercantile

  • It explains why Paracelsians later claimed him‚Äîand misunderstood him
